Following this order (especially grain before dimensions/facts) prevents most modeling mistakes.

Q42. Can a fact table have no foreign keys, or a dimension have measures? mid

A fact almost always has foreign keys (that's its context), but a factless fact is all keys and no measures, and a degenerate-only fact carries an id without a dimension table. Dimensions can hold numeric attributes that aren't additive measures — e.g., a product's standard weight or a customer's credit-limit band — those are legitimate descriptive numbers used for grouping, not summing. The distinction is behavioral: measures get aggregated across rows; dimension numbers get filtered/grouped.

Q43. How do you avoid double-counting when joining a fact to a many-to-many dimension? hard

When a bridge table creates a many-to-many join, a fact row can fan out into multiple rows, inflating SUMs. Mitigations: (1) include an allocation factor (weight that sums to 1 per fact) in the bridge and multiply measures by it so totals reconcile; (2) aggregate the fact before joining the bridge; (3) use COUNT(DISTINCT fact_key) for counts. Always sanity-check that a grouped total still equals the ungrouped grand total — a mismatch signals fan-out double-counting.

Q44. When would you choose a snowflake over a star? mid

Snowflaking a dimension makes sense when it is very large with a repeated, normalizable hierarchy where the redundancy is costly or the sub-entity changes independently — e.g., a product dimension whose category/department attributes are shared by millions of products and updated centrally. It can also help when a BI tool needs a clean hierarchy table. Otherwise, prefer the star: the extra joins and complexity of snowflaking usually aren't worth the modest storage savings on modern warehouses.

Q45. What's the difference between a natural key, surrogate key, and business key in a warehouse? mid

Natural key / business key — the identifier from the source system that has real-world meaning (customer_id, SKU); the two terms are used interchangeably. Surrogate key — the warehouse-generated integer PK of the dimension, meaningless outside the warehouse. In practice a dimension row has a surrogate key (its PK, used by facts) and stores the business key as an attribute (for lineage and matching during loads). Facts join on surrogate keys, not business keys.

Q46. How do you model a hierarchy (e.g., category → subcategory → product)? mid

For a fixed-depth hierarchy, the star approach flattens it into columns on one dimension row: department, category, subcategory, product_name all in dim_product — simple to query and group. For a variable-depth / ragged hierarchy (like an org chart), use a bridge/closure table that maps each node to all its ancestors with a depth level. Flattening is preferred whenever the depth is known and stable.

Q47. What does "single source of truth" mean and how do models support it? mid

Single source of truth (SSOT) means every metric and entity is defined once, consistently, so different teams' numbers agree. Dimensional modeling supports it through conformed dimensions (one shared dim_customer/dim_date), centrally defined facts, and a layered transformation approach (dbt marts) where business logic lives in one versioned place. Without SSOT you get the classic problem of two dashboards showing different "revenue" because each recomputed it differently.

Q48. What is a "wide" vs "long" data shape and which suits analytics? mid

Long/narrow data has few columns and many rows, often with a key-value or metric-per-row structure (e.g., one row per metric per entity). Wide data has one row per entity with many attribute columns. Fact tables are naturally long (one row per event); consumption/OBT marts are wide. Columnar warehouses handle both well, but wide marts are friendlier for BI tools, while long fact tables are more flexible and additive. Reshaping between them (pivot/unpivot) is a common transformation task.

Q49. How do you validate that your dimensional model is correct? mid

Checks: (1) grain uniqueness — no duplicate rows at the declared grain (test primary-key uniqueness); (2) referential integrity — every fact FK matches a dimension surrogate key (no orphans, no NULLs beyond the 'unknown' member); (3) totals reconcile to the source system (sum of fact amount = source total); (4) SCD2 integrity — exactly one current row per natural key and non-overlapping valid_from/valid_to windows; (5) additivity sanity (semi-additive measures aren't summed over time). dbt tests (unique, not_null, relationships) automate these.

Q50. In a modern cloud warehouse, is dimensional modeling still relevant? hard

Yes — the principles (grain, conformed dimensions, clear facts vs attributes, SCD for history) remain essential for correctness and shared understanding, even if the physical form loosens. Cheap storage and fast columnar engines mean teams often materialize denormalized wide tables / OBTs on top of a dimensional core, and skip aggressive snowflaking. So you still model dimensionally to reason about the data, then denormalize pragmatically for performance. Interviewers want you to know both the timeless model and the modern OBT tradeoff.

Data Lakes, Warehouses, Lakehouses & Modern Architectures

The vocabulary of the "design a data platform" interview round — know these four words cold and why the industry moved between them.

What & Why

A data warehouse (DW) is a database optimized for analytics: structured, schema-on-write, columnar storage, built for fast aggregate queries over cleaned historical data (Snowflake, BigQuery, Redshift). You define the schema before loading, so data is trusted but rigid. A data lake is cheap object storage (S3, ADLS, GCS) holding raw files of any shape — CSV, JSON, Parquet, images, logs — schema-on-read. It is flexible and cheap but easily becomes a "data swamp" without governance.

A lakehouse merges the two: it puts a transactional table layer (Delta Lake, Apache Iceberg, Hudi) on top of cheap lake storage, giving you ACID transactions, schema enforcement, time travel and DW-like SQL performance directly on open file formats. You get the lake's low cost and openness plus the warehouse's reliability — no copying data into a separate DW. This is the dominant 2026 pattern.

Data mesh is not a technology — it is an org/architecture principle: decentralize data ownership to domain teams (payments, lending, fraud), each publishing well-documented, SLA-backed data products, with a self-serve platform and federated governance. It answers scaling teams, not just tech. In interviews, distinguish clearly: DW/lake/lakehouse are storage-and-compute choices; mesh is an organizational operating model layered on top.

Where it fits: ingestion lands raw data in the lake (bronze), transforms clean it (silver), and business-ready marts (gold) serve BI and ML. Orchestration (Airflow) schedules it, a catalog tracks metadata/lineage, and the whole thing is a "platform" you'll be asked to draw on a whiteboard.

Learning Checklist

Projects & Practice

  • Fintech analytics platform diagram — draw an end-to-end architecture (sources: transactions DB, card-swipe events, KYC files → ingestion → bronze/silver/gold lakehouse → BI + fraud ML) using draw.io or Excalidraw. Write a 1-page doc justifying each choice (why lakehouse, batch vs streaming per source, catalog). Proves system-design vocabulary for interviews.
  • ETL vs ELT hands-on — take the Kaggle "Online Retail" dataset. Path A (ETL): clean/aggregate in pandas, then load only the result into DuckDB. Path B (ELT): load raw CSV into DuckDB first, transform with SQL. Compare code complexity, re-run cost, and flexibility; document when each wins.
  • Medallion mini-lakehouse — with DuckDB + Parquet on local disk, build bronze (raw ingest), silver (dedup/typed), gold (daily revenue mart) as three query layers. Proves you understand layering, not just a single table.

Interview Q&A (25)

Q1. What is a data warehouse in one sentence? basic

A data warehouse is a centralized analytical database that stores cleaned, structured, historical data in a columnar format, optimized for fast aggregate/reporting queries rather than single-row transactional writes. Examples: Snowflake, Google BigQuery, Amazon Redshift.

Q2. What is a data lake and how does it differ from a warehouse? basic

A data lake is cheap, scalable object storage (S3, ADLS, GCS) that holds raw data of any format — structured, semi-structured, or unstructured. Key differences: the lake is schema-on-read (structure applied when you query), the warehouse is schema-on-write (structure enforced at load). Lakes are cheaper and more flexible; warehouses are more governed and faster for SQL analytics.

Q3. What does "schema-on-read" vs "schema-on-write" mean? basic

Schema-on-write means you define and validate the table structure before loading data (warehouses) — bad rows are rejected up front, so reads are fast and trusted. Schema-on-read means you dump raw files and only impose a schema when a query reads them (lakes) — flexible and fast to ingest, but structure/quality problems surface at read time.

Q4. What is a lakehouse and what problem does it solve? mid

A lakehouse adds a transactional table format (Delta Lake, Apache Iceberg, Apache Hudi) on top of cheap lake storage, so you get ACID transactions, schema enforcement, updates/deletes, time travel, and warehouse-grade SQL performance directly on open Parquet files. It solves the historical "two systems" problem: you no longer copy data from a lake into a separate warehouse — one copy serves both BI and ML, reducing cost and staleness.

Q5. Explain the medallion architecture. basic

Medallion organizes a lakehouse into three quality tiers. Bronze = raw ingested data, kept as-is for replay/audit. Silver = cleaned, deduplicated, typed, and conformed (joined/standardized) data. Gold = business-level aggregates and marts consumed by dashboards and ML. Each layer is a set of tables, and data flows bronze → silver → gold, so failures are traceable and reprocessing is cheap.

Q6. How would you explain the medallion architecture to a non-technical PM? mid

"Think of a kitchen. Bronze is the raw groceries exactly as delivered — nothing thrown away in case we need to check the receipt. Silver is prepped ingredients: washed, chopped, labeled. Gold is the finished dishes served to customers — your dashboards. We keep all three so if a dish tastes wrong, we can trace back to which prep step or which delivery caused it." The point: layered quality and traceability, not raw jargon.

Q7. What's the difference between ETL and ELT? basic

ETL = Extract, Transform, Load: you clean and reshape data on a separate compute engine before loading it into the warehouse. ELT = Extract, Load, Transform: you load raw data into the warehouse/lakehouse first, then transform it in-place using the warehouse's own SQL/compute. ELT stores raw data (enabling re-transforms) while ETL only stores the final result.

Q8. Why did ELT largely win in the cloud era? mid

Three reasons. (1) Cloud storage is cheap, so keeping raw data is affordable. (2) Cloud warehouses (Snowflake/BigQuery) have massive elastic compute, so transforming inside them is fast and scales on demand. (3) Storing raw data means you can re-run transformations when business logic changes or bugs are found, without re-extracting from source. Tools like dbt made in-warehouse SQL transformation the standard, cementing ELT.

Q9. When might you still prefer ETL over ELT? mid

When you must not land certain raw data in the warehouse for compliance/PII reasons (mask or tokenize before load), when the source is huge but only a small aggregate is needed (transform to shrink before paying to store), when heavy transformations are cheaper on a dedicated engine (Spark) than in the warehouse, or when the target system can't transform efficiently. In fintech, pre-load PII masking is a common ETL driver.

Q10. Contrast batch, streaming, and micro-batch processing. basic

Batch: process a bounded chunk on a schedule (e.g., all of yesterday's data at 2am) — simple, high throughput, high latency. Streaming: process each event as it arrives (Flink, Kafka Streams) — lowest latency, more complex. Micro-batch: process tiny batches every few seconds (Spark Structured Streaming's default) — a practical middle ground giving near-real-time latency with batch-style tooling.

Q11. What is the Lambda architecture and its main drawback? mid

Lambda has two parallel paths: a batch layer that recomputes accurate results over all historical data, and a speed layer that produces fast approximate results on recent data; a serving layer merges them. Its main drawback is duplicated logic — you maintain the same business rules twice (once in batch code, once in streaming code), which is costly and error-prone as they drift apart.

Q12. What is the Kappa architecture and when is it a good fit? mid

Kappa uses a single streaming path: everything flows through an append-only log (Kafka), and there is no separate batch layer. To "reprocess" history you simply replay the log through the same streaming code. It removes Lambda's dual-codebase problem. It fits when your workloads are naturally event-driven and one streaming engine can serve both real-time and reprocessing needs; it's weaker when heavy historical joins are cheaper in batch.

Q13. Design ingestion for 10 million events/day — walk through it. mid

10M/day ≈ 116 events/sec average, higher at peaks — modest volume. Options: (1) If near-real-time is needed, push events to Kafka, consume with Spark Structured Streaming or a Kafka connector, write to bronze Delta/Iceberg tables. (2) If hourly/daily freshness suffices, buffer events to files in the lake and run scheduled batch loads via Airflow. I'd choose streaming-to-bronze with micro-batch writes for fraud use cases, batch for reporting. Key concerns: idempotency/dedup (event IDs), schema evolution, partitioning by event date, and monitoring lag.

Q14. When would you choose a warehouse over a lakehouse? mid

Choose a pure warehouse when your data is almost entirely structured, your team wants a fully managed SQL experience with minimal engineering (Snowflake/BigQuery), you have strong BI/finance workloads and small-to-mid data, and you don't need ML on raw/unstructured data or open-format portability. Choose a lakehouse when you have large/varied data, want to avoid vendor lock-in with open formats, need both BI and ML on one copy, or want lower storage cost at scale.

Q15. What is a "data swamp" and how do you prevent it? mid

A data swamp is a data lake that has degraded into an ungoverned dumping ground — no catalog, no ownership, unknown quality, duplicated and undiscoverable data. Prevent it with: a data catalog and mandatory metadata, clear zone conventions (bronze/silver/gold), enforced schemas at silver+, data ownership per domain, quality checks, lineage tracking, and retention/lifecycle policies. Governance, not storage, is what separates a lake from a swamp.

Q16. What is data mesh and how is it different from a data lake? hard

A data lake is a technical centralized storage choice. Data mesh is an organizational operating model: it decentralizes data ownership to the business domains that know the data best (payments, lending), each treating its data as a product with owners, SLAs, documentation, and quality guarantees. It rests on four principles: domain ownership, data-as-a-product, self-serve data platform, and federated computational governance. Mesh addresses scaling teams and accountability; a lake/lakehouse can be the technology mesh runs on.

Q17. What makes something a "data product"? mid

A data product is a curated, self-contained dataset (or table/API/stream) treated with product discipline: it has a clear owner, discoverable metadata in a catalog, documented schema and semantics, quality and freshness SLAs, versioning, and defined consumers. It should be discoverable, addressable, trustworthy, self-describing, interoperable, and secure — the "DATSIS" qualities from data mesh literature.

Q18. Where does orchestration fit in a data architecture? basic

Orchestration (Airflow, Dagster, Prefect) is the scheduler and dependency manager that wires the platform together. It defines pipelines as DAGs — "extract source A, then load bronze, then run silver transforms, then refresh gold, then trigger BI" — handling scheduling, dependencies, retries, backfills, alerting, and SLAs. It doesn't move/store data itself; it coordinates the systems that do.

Q19. What is data lineage and why do interviewers care? mid

Lineage is the traceable path of data from source to destination — which sources, transformations, and jobs produced a given column or table. It matters for impact analysis ("if I change this source, what breaks?"), debugging ("this dashboard number is wrong — where?"), compliance/audit (proving how a regulatory figure was derived), and trust. Tools like DataHub, OpenLineage, and Unity Catalog capture it, ideally at column level.

Q20. What role does a data catalog play? mid

A data catalog is the searchable inventory of an organization's data: table/column schemas, descriptions, owners, tags, freshness, popularity, and lineage. It makes data discoverable (analysts find the right table instead of remaking it), governable (classify PII, enforce access), and trustworthy. Examples: Unity Catalog, AWS Glue Data Catalog, DataHub, Amundsen. It's the antidote to the data swamp.

Q21. Design an end-to-end architecture for a fintech analytics platform. hard

Sources: transactional Postgres (accounts, ledgers), card-swipe event stream, KYC document store. Ingest: CDC (Debezium→Kafka) for the DB, direct Kafka for events, batch file drops for KYC. Storage (lakehouse): land raw in bronze Delta/Iceberg on S3, partitioned by date. Transform: Spark/dbt build silver (typed, deduped, PII-tokenized) and gold marts (daily revenue, per-customer balances, fraud features). Orchestrate: Airflow DAGs with retries/SLAs. Serve: BI (Looker/Tableau) on gold; a feature store + ML for fraud on silver/gold. Cross-cutting: Unity/Glue catalog, column-level lineage, encryption + access controls, data-quality tests. Justify streaming for fraud (low latency) and batch for finance reporting (accuracy).

Q22. How do you decide between batch and streaming for a given pipeline? mid

Ask what latency the business decision needs. If action must happen in seconds (fraud block, real-time balance), stream. If daily/hourly freshness is fine (finance reports, executive dashboards), batch — it's simpler, cheaper, and easier to get exactly-once correctness. Also weigh volume/cost, team skills, and existing tooling. A common answer: "stream only where latency creates business value; batch everything else."

Q23. What are the trade-offs of open table formats (Iceberg/Delta) vs a proprietary warehouse? hard

Open formats (Iceberg, Delta, Hudi) give data portability (many engines can read the same files), no vendor lock-in, lower storage cost on object stores, and one copy for BI+ML. Proprietary warehouses (Snowflake) give a polished managed experience, mature SQL performance, less operational burden, and strong governance out of the box. Trade-off: openness/cost/flexibility vs simplicity/managed convenience. Many orgs use both — warehouse for BI, lakehouse for scale/ML — or Snowflake now reading Iceberg to bridge them.

Q24. How does idempotency matter in ingestion architecture? hard

Ingestion pipelines fail and retry, and streams deliver at-least-once, so the same record can arrive twice. Idempotency means reprocessing produces the same result — no double counting. Achieve it with: unique event/business keys and dedup (MERGE/upsert on key), deterministic partitioning, idempotent writes (overwrite a partition rather than append blindly), and offset/watermark tracking. In fintech this is critical — a duplicated transaction event must not double a balance.

Q25. Compare Lambda vs Kappa and state which you'd pick today and why. hard

Lambda maintains separate batch and speed layers — accurate but duplicates logic. Kappa runs one streaming path and reprocesses by replaying the log — simpler codebase, needs a durable, replayable log (Kafka) and a streaming engine that scales to historical loads. Today I'd default toward Kappa (or a lakehouse-streaming hybrid) because modern engines (Flink, Spark Structured Streaming) plus replayable logs remove much of Lambda's motivation and eliminate dual-codebase drift. I'd keep a batch path only where historical reprocessing is dramatically cheaper or where correctness-critical recomputation is easier in batch.

File Formats & Storage: Parquet, Avro, ORC & Compression

Format choice = performance + cost. "Why Parquet?" and "Avro or Parquet?" are guaranteed interview questions.

What & Why

How you store data on disk determines how fast and cheap your queries are. CSV and JSON are human-readable text: universal but slow, bulky, untyped, and with no built-in schema — fine for tiny data or interchange, terrible for analytics at scale. The big shift is row vs columnar storage. Row-based formats keep all fields of a record together (great for writing/reading whole records). Columnar formats store each column's values together, so analytical queries that touch a few columns read far less data and compress dramatically better.

Parquet is the columnar workhorse of analytics: it stores data in row groups, each split into per-column chunks, with rich encodings (dictionary, run-length, delta) and per-column statistics that enable predicate pushdown — skipping chunks that can't match a filter. ORC is a similar columnar format popular in the Hive/Hadoop world. Avro is row-based with a schema stored alongside the data, excellent for write-heavy streaming (Kafka) and for schema evolution.

On top of format sits compression (snappy, gzip, zstd) and partitioning (folder layout like /date=2026-07-05/ that lets engines skip whole directories). Two chronic pain points are the small-files problem (thousands of tiny files kill performance) and schema evolution (adding/removing/renaming columns over time without breaking readers). Mastering these is core DE craft — and cheap wins: converting a job's output from CSV to partitioned Parquet often cuts cost and runtime by 10x.

Learning Checklist

Projects & Practice

  • CSV → Parquet benchmark — take a large Kaggle CSV (e.g., "NYC Taxi Trips" or "Online Retail"). Convert to Parquet (snappy) in pandas/PySpark, and measure on-disk size and the read/query time for a filtered aggregate on both. Document the size reduction and speedup. Proves you can quantify format impact.
  • Inspect Parquet metadata with pyarrow — use pyarrow.parquet.ParquetFile to print number of row groups, rows per group, per-column encodings, compression, and min/max statistics. Explain how those stats power predicate pushdown. Proves you understand internals, not just APIs.
  • Compression bake-off — write the same DataFrame to Parquet with snappy, gzip, and zstd; compare size and write/read time. Recommend a default and justify it. Proves practical judgment on the classic tradeoff.
  • Partition & compact — write a dataset partitioned by date, deliberately create many small files, then compact them into ~128MB files; measure the query improvement. Proves you can diagnose and fix the small-files problem.

Interview Q&A (25)

Q1. Why are CSV and JSON poor choices for large analytical datasets? basic

They're plain text: large on disk, slow to parse, untyped (everything is a string until cast), and carry no reliable schema. There's no column pruning — reading one column means scanning entire rows. JSON adds repeated key names on every record, bloating size. For analytics at scale you want a typed, columnar, compressed binary format like Parquet instead.

Q2. What is the difference between row-based and columnar storage? basic

Row-based stores all fields of a record contiguously (row 1's name,age,city, then row 2's…) — efficient when you read/write whole records. Columnar stores each column's values contiguously (all names, then all ages…). Columnar wins for analytics: queries touching a few columns read only those columns, and storing like-typed values together compresses far better.

Q3. Why does columnar storage compress better than row storage? mid

Because a column holds values of the same type and often similar/repeated values (e.g., a "country" column with few distinct values, or a sorted timestamp column). Similar adjacent data compresses far more effectively and enables encodings like dictionary and run-length. In a row layout, adjacent bytes are mixed types (string, int, date), which compress poorly.

Q4. Describe the internal structure of a Parquet file. mid

A Parquet file is divided into row groups (horizontal slices of rows, e.g., 128MB each). Within a row group, each column is a column chunk, and each chunk is split into pages (the compression/encoding unit). The footer at the end stores the schema and per-column-chunk statistics (min, max, null count) plus offsets. Readers read the footer first to plan which chunks to skip.

Q5. What is predicate pushdown and how does Parquet enable it? mid

Predicate pushdown means applying a filter as early as possible so less data is read. Parquet stores min/max statistics per column chunk in the footer. If you query WHERE amount > 1000 and a chunk's max amount is 500, the engine skips that entire chunk without decompressing it. Combined with column pruning (reading only needed columns), this can cut I/O by orders of magnitude.

Q6. What are common Parquet encodings and what do they do? mid

Dictionary encoding replaces repeated values with small integer codes (great for low-cardinality columns like status). Run-length encoding (RLE) compresses long runs of the same value into value+count. Delta encoding stores differences between consecutive values (great for sorted IDs/timestamps). These are applied per column before the general compression codec, and are a big reason Parquet is so compact.

Q7. What is Avro and when would you use it? basic

Avro is a compact, binary, row-based format that stores its schema (as JSON) with the data. Because it's row-oriented it's efficient for writing whole records and for streaming, and its schema-with-data design gives excellent schema evolution. Use it for write-heavy and streaming workloads — notably Kafka message payloads via a schema registry — and for data interchange between systems.

Q8. When would you choose Avro over Parquet, and vice versa? mid

Choose Avro for write-heavy, streaming, record-at-a-time workloads where you often read whole records and need robust schema evolution — e.g., Kafka events, landing/ingestion. Choose Parquet for read-heavy analytical workloads where queries scan a subset of columns over many rows — e.g., warehouse/lakehouse tables feeding BI and aggregations. A common pattern: Avro on the wire (Kafka), Parquet at rest (analytics tables).

Q9. What is ORC and how does it compare to Parquet? mid

ORC (Optimized Row Columnar) is a columnar format born in the Hive/Hadoop ecosystem. Like Parquet it uses columnar layout with statistics and lightweight indexes; it organizes data into stripes (analogous to row groups) and has strong built-in ACID/update support within Hive. In practice ORC and Parquet perform similarly; Parquet has broader adoption across Spark, cloud, and Python tooling, so it's the more common default outside pure-Hive shops.

Q10. Compare snappy, gzip, and zstd compression codecs. mid

Snappy: fast compress/decompress, modest ratio — the common Parquet default, good CPU/size balance. Gzip: high compression ratio but slow and CPU-heavy; good for cold/archival data. Zstd: modern codec giving gzip-like (often better) ratios at near-snappy speed with tunable levels — increasingly the best all-round choice. Pick snappy/zstd for hot analytical data, gzip when storage cost dominates and reads are rare.

Q11. What does "splittable" mean and why does it matter? mid

A file is splittable if a distributed engine can divide it into independent chunks processed in parallel by different workers. A single gzipped CSV is not splittable — gzip must be decompressed from the start — so one huge gzip file is read by one task, killing parallelism. Parquet is splittable at row-group boundaries, and snappy/zstd inside Parquet keep it splittable. This is a key reason to avoid one giant gzip file.

Q12. Show how to convert a CSV to Parquet in pandas. basic

With pandas + pyarrow:

import pandas as pd

df = pd.read_csv("trips.csv")
df.to_parquet(
    "trips.parquet",
    engine="pyarrow",
    compression="snappy",
    index=False,
)

Reading back is pd.read_parquet("trips.parquet", columns=["fare","date"]), and passing columns= triggers column pruning so only those columns are read.

Q13. How do you inspect a Parquet file's metadata with pyarrow? mid

Use ParquetFile:

import pyarrow.parquet as pq

pf = pq.ParquetFile("trips.parquet")
meta = pf.metadata
print(meta.num_rows, meta.num_row_groups)

rg = meta.row_group(0)
col = rg.column(0)
print(col.compression, col.encodings)
print(col.statistics.min, col.statistics.max)

This reveals row groups, rows per group, per-column compression/encodings, and the min/max statistics that drive predicate pushdown.

Q14. What is the small-files problem and why is it bad? mid

It's having thousands of tiny files (KBs each) instead of fewer larger ones. Each file has fixed overhead: a metadata/footer read, a file-open request, and a task to schedule. Thousands of tiny files mean huge scheduling overhead, slow listing on object stores, poor compression, and bloated metadata. Streaming jobs and over-partitioning are common causes. Result: queries that should take seconds take minutes.

Q15. How do you fix the small-files problem? mid

Compaction: periodically rewrite many small files into fewer large ones (target ~128MB–1GB). In Spark use repartition()/coalesce() before writing; in Delta use OPTIMIZE, in Iceberg the rewrite_data_files action, in Hudi clustering. Also fix the root cause: don't over-partition, batch small streaming writes, and tune writer output size. Prevention plus scheduled compaction is the standard answer.

Q16. How does partitioning work physically, and how does it speed queries? mid

Partitioning writes data into folders named by a key, e.g. /event_date=2026-07-05/part-0.parquet. When a query filters on that key (WHERE event_date = '2026-07-05'), the engine performs partition pruning — it lists and reads only the matching folder and skips all others without opening their files. It's coarse-grained skipping at the directory level, complementing Parquet's fine-grained row-group skipping.

Q17. How do you choose a partition column? What's over-partitioning? mid

Partition on a low-to-moderate cardinality column that queries frequently filter on — usually a date (day/month). Over-partitioning is choosing a high-cardinality key (like user_id or full timestamp), producing millions of tiny folders/files — this recreates the small-files problem and slows metadata operations. Rule of thumb: each partition should hold a healthy amount of data (hundreds of MB to GBs), not a handful of rows.

Q18. Given 2 TB/day, how would you size partitions and target file sizes? hard

Partition by day → each daily partition ≈ 2TB. With a target file size of ~256MB, that's 2,000,000MB ÷ 256MB ≈ ~8,000 files per day — reasonable. If a single day is too large for some queries, add a second partition level (e.g., hour) to get ~330 files/hour, or partition by a natural business key. The math: files ≈ partition_size ÷ target_file_size; keep target files in the 128MB–1GB range and each partition comfortably large but not a monolith.

Q19. What is column pruning and how do you trigger it? basic

Column pruning (projection pushdown) is reading only the columns a query needs, skipping the rest entirely — cheap in columnar formats since each column is stored separately. You trigger it by selecting specific columns rather than SELECT *: in Spark df.select("fare","date"), in pandas read_parquet(path, columns=[...]). On a wide table, reading 2 of 50 columns can cut I/O ~25x.

Q20. How does schema evolution differ between Avro and Parquet? hard

Avro was designed for evolution: readers and writers can use different schema versions, and Avro resolves them using defaults and rules (add a field with a default → old readers ignore it, new readers get the default). It supports backward/forward/full compatibility, enforced by schema registries in Kafka. Parquet handles additive evolution well (new columns read as null for old files) but is stricter on renames/type changes; in practice, lakehouse table formats (Delta/Iceberg) add robust evolution semantics (safe adds, renames, type widening) on top of Parquet files.

Q21. What is the difference between backward and forward schema compatibility? hard

Backward compatible: new schema can read data written with the old schema (common when you deploy a new consumer for old data — e.g., you added a field with a default). Forward compatible: old schema can read data written with the new schema (old consumers still work when producers upgrade — e.g., they ignore new fields). Full = both. Schema registries enforce a chosen policy so a bad change is rejected before it breaks consumers.

Q22. Convert CSV to Parquet in PySpark with partitioning — show the code. mid

Read the CSV, then write partitioned Parquet:

df = (spark.read
        .option("header", True)
        .option("inferSchema", True)
        .csv("s3://bucket/raw/trips/*.csv"))

(df.repartition("event_date")
   .write
   .mode("overwrite")
   .partitionBy("event_date")
   .option("compression", "snappy")
   .parquet("s3://bucket/curated/trips/"))

partitionBy creates the folder layout; repartition controls file count per partition to avoid tiny files.

Q23. Why might Parquet still read slowly, and how do you diagnose it? hard

Common causes: too many small files (footer/scheduling overhead), poor sort order so min/max stats overlap and pushdown skips nothing, over-partitioning, wide SELECT * defeating column pruning, or filters on non-statistics-friendly columns. Diagnose by checking file counts/sizes, inspecting row-group stats (do min/max ranges cluster or overlap?), and reading query-plan/scan metrics (bytes/files scanned vs total). Fixes: compaction, sort/z-order on filter columns, select fewer columns, right-size partitions.

Q24. When is CSV/JSON actually the right choice? basic

When you need human readability or maximum interoperability with tools that don't speak Parquet — e.g., small config/reference files, quick data exchange with external partners, debugging, or ingesting from an API that emits JSON. They're also fine for genuinely small data where performance is irrelevant. For anything analytical or large, convert to Parquet at the bronze/silver boundary.

Q25. Design a full storage strategy for a fintech pipeline: formats, compression, partitioning. hard

In-flight: card/transaction events on Kafka as Avro with a schema registry (compact, evolvable). Bronze (raw at rest): land as Parquet or a Delta/Iceberg bronze table, partitioned by ingest date, snappy/zstd compression — keep raw for replay. Silver: cleaned, typed, PII-tokenized Parquet-backed lakehouse tables, partitioned by event_date, sorted/z-ordered on high-filter columns (account_id) for pushdown, target ~256MB files with scheduled compaction. Gold: aggregated marts, partitioned by month, zstd for good ratio. Cross-cutting: enforce schema evolution policy, catalog + column-level lineage, and lifecycle rules to age cold data to cheaper storage. Justify: Avro for streaming write-efficiency and evolution; Parquet/lakehouse for analytical reads, pushdown, and ACID.

PySpark & Apache Spark

Distributed compute for data too big for one machine — the #1 most-tested Data Engineering skill after SQL.

What & Why

Start from zero. A single computer has one CPU (a handful of cores), some RAM (say 16–64 GB), and a disk. That is fine for a spreadsheet or a 1 GB CSV. But data platforms routinely deal with terabytes: years of transactions, clickstreams, sensor logs. If a file is 2 TB and your machine has 64 GB of RAM, you physically cannot load it into memory. Even reading it from disk on one machine would take hours, and one CPU processing row-by-row would take days. This is the core problem: the data is too big for one machine, and one machine is too slow.

The fix is distributed computing: instead of one big machine, use a cluster of many ordinary machines (nodes) working together. Split the 2 TB into, say, 200 chunks of 10 GB, hand one chunk to each machine, let all 200 process their chunk in parallel, then combine the results. What took days on one machine takes minutes on 200. The hard part is coordinating all this: splitting data, scheduling work, moving data between machines, and recovering when a machine dies mid-job. You do not want to write that plumbing by hand.

Apache Spark is the engine that does all that plumbing for you. You write code as if operating on one big table; Spark figures out how to split the work across the cluster, runs it in parallel, and handles failures. It keeps data in memory where possible (much faster than the older Hadoop MapReduce, which wrote to disk between every step). Spark has four libraries on one core: Spark SQL / DataFrames (the part DEs use daily), Structured Streaming (real-time), MLlib (machine learning), and GraphX. It runs the same code on your laptop or on a 1000-node cluster.

PySpark is the Python API for Spark (Spark itself is written in Scala/JVM). You write Python; PySpark translates DataFrame operations into JVM work. Why it dominates DE interviews: almost every batch pipeline in product/fintech companies (via Databricks, EMR, Synapse, or Glue) is Spark. Interviewers test it because it separates people who can write a query from people who understand how the query executes at scale — shuffles, partitions, skew, joins, memory. Master SQL first, then PySpark; together they cover the bulk of what a DE actually does. This section teaches Spark assuming you have never touched it.

Learning Checklist

Projects & Practice

  • Get Spark running (two free options). Easiest, no install: sign up for Databricks Community Edition (free forever, no credit card) → create a cluster → create a Python notebook → a spark SparkSession is already created for you. Local install: pip install pyspark (needs Java 8/11/17 installed first — check with java -version, install via brew install openjdk@17 on Mac), then in Python: from pyspark.sql import SparkSession; spark = SparkSession.builder.master("local[*]").appName("dev").getOrCreate(). local[*] means "run on my laptop using all cores." This proves you can set up an environment.
  • NYC Taxi / Olist end-to-end batch pipeline. Download the Olist Brazilian E-Commerce dataset (or NYC TLC Yellow Taxi trips). Build: ingest raw CSVs with an explicit schema → clean (drop nulls/dupes, cast types, fix bad rows with when/otherwise) → aggregate (revenue per month, avg delivery days per state, top products — using groupBy/agg and window functions) → write partitioned Parquet (.write.partitionBy("year","month").parquet(...)). Then read it back and query it. This proves the full DataFrame API, joins, and partitioned writes — the exact shape of a real DE job.
  • Skew-handling exercise. Take any dataset with a lopsided key (e.g. many orders from one seller/state). Join it and watch one task run 100x longer in the Spark UI (Stages tab). Then fix it with (a) a broadcast join if one side is small, and (b) salting the hot key. Compare stage times before/after. This is a favorite senior-DE interview scenario.
  • Mini Structured Streaming job. Use the built-in rate source (generates fake rows per second — no Kafka needed): spark.readStream.format("rate").load() → add a windowed aggregation with a withWatermark → write to console sink with a checkpoint location. Watch micro-batches print every trigger. This proves you understand the streaming model before touching Kafka.
  • Read the Spark UI on every job. For each project, open the Spark UI (port 4040 locally, or the cluster UI in Databricks). Learn to read: Jobs → Stages → Tasks, the DAG visualization, shuffle read/write sizes, and skewed task durations. Interviewers love "how would you debug a slow job" — the answer always starts with the UI.

Interview Q&A (100)

Q1. What problem does Apache Spark solve? basic

It processes data that is too big or too slow to handle on a single machine, by distributing the work across a cluster of machines that run in parallel. You write code as if on one big table; Spark splits the data, schedules parallel tasks, and combines results, while handling machine failures automatically. It keeps data in memory where possible, making it far faster than the older disk-based Hadoop MapReduce.

Q2. What is the difference between Spark and PySpark? basic

Spark is the engine, written in Scala and running on the JVM. PySpark is the Python API for that engine — you write Python, and PySpark hands DataFrame operations to the JVM to execute. For DataFrame/SQL work there is almost no performance difference between PySpark and Scala, because both compile down to the same optimized plan. PySpark is the most common choice in DE because Python is the lingua franca of data.

Q3. What is a cluster, a node, driver, and executor? basic

A cluster is a group of machines. Each machine is a node. When you run a Spark job, one process is the driver (the "brain": it holds your code, builds the execution plan, and schedules work) and many processes are executors (the "workers": they run tasks on data and report results back). Executors run on the worker nodes; each executor has a set of CPU cores and a chunk of memory.

Q4. What does the driver do vs the executors? basic

The driver runs your main program, creates the SparkSession, builds the DAG (logical plan), splits it into stages and tasks, and sends tasks to executors. It also collects results back (e.g. from collect() or count()). The executors do the actual data crunching: read partitions, run transformations, hold cached data, and shuffle data to each other. If the driver dies, the whole application dies; if one executor dies, Spark reschedules its tasks elsewhere.

Q5. What is the cluster manager? basic

It is the component that allocates machines/resources to Spark. When the driver starts, it asks the cluster manager for executors (with X cores and Y memory each). Options: Standalone (Spark's own simple manager), YARN (Hadoop's, common on EMR), Kubernetes (containers, increasingly common), and Mesos (legacy). Databricks abstracts this away. The cluster manager only allocates resources; Spark's driver still schedules the actual tasks.

Q6. Cluster mode vs client mode? mid

This is about where the driver runs. In client mode, the driver runs on the machine that submitted the job (e.g. your laptop or an edge node) — good for interactive work and notebooks, but the driver is a network hop away from executors and dies if your laptop disconnects. In cluster mode, the driver runs inside the cluster on a worker node — better for production, resilient, and low-latency to executors. You set it with --deploy-mode cluster in spark-submit.

Q7. What is a SparkSession and how do you create one? basic

It is the single entry point to Spark's DataFrame/SQL functionality (it wraps the older SparkContext). You use it to read data, run SQL, and configure the app.

from pyspark.sql import SparkSession

spark = (SparkSession.builder
         .appName("my_job")
         .master("local[*]")      # laptop, all cores
         .config("spark.sql.shuffle.partitions", "200")
         .getOrCreate())

In Databricks/notebooks, spark already exists. getOrCreate() reuses an existing session if one is running.

Q8. What is an RDD? mid

RDD = Resilient Distributed Dataset: Spark's original low-level abstraction — an immutable, partitioned collection of objects spread across the cluster, with no schema. "Resilient" because Spark can rebuild lost partitions using lineage (the recipe of transformations that produced it). You rarely use RDDs directly today; you use DataFrames, which are built on top of RDDs but add a schema and the Catalyst optimizer.

Q9. RDD vs DataFrame vs Dataset — which do you use and why? mid

RDD: low-level, no schema, no optimizer — you control everything but get no automatic speedups. DataFrame: a distributed table with named, typed columns; goes through the Catalyst optimizer and Tungsten engine, so it is far faster — this is what PySpark DEs use 95% of the time. Dataset: a typed DataFrame available only in Scala/Java (compile-time type safety); it does not exist in Python (PySpark has no Dataset API). Use DataFrames.

Q10. Why is the DataFrame API faster than raw RDDs? mid

Because DataFrames know the schema and go through Catalyst (a query optimizer that reorders and simplifies your operations — e.g. pushing filters down before joins) and Tungsten (which manages memory off-heap in a compact binary format and generates optimized bytecode). RDDs are opaque Python/JVM objects that Spark can't inspect or optimize, and in PySpark they force costly serialization between Python and the JVM. So DataFrames both plan smarter and execute leaner.

Q11. What is Catalyst? mid

Catalyst is Spark SQL's query optimizer. It takes your DataFrame code, builds a logical plan, applies rule-based optimizations (predicate pushdown, constant folding, column pruning, join reordering), then produces one or more physical plans and picks the cheapest via cost estimates. The result is a physical plan of RDD operations. You can see its output with df.explain(True), which prints the parsed, analyzed, optimized, and physical plans.

Q12. What is Tungsten? mid

Tungsten is Spark's execution backend focused on CPU and memory efficiency. It stores rows in a compact off-heap binary format (avoiding JVM object overhead and reducing garbage collection), uses cache-aware algorithms, and does whole-stage code generation — compiling a whole chain of operations into a single optimized Java function instead of interpreting each operator. Together with Catalyst, it is why DataFrames are fast.

Q13. Transformations vs actions? basic

Transformations define a new DataFrame from an existing one and are lazy — they don't run, they just build the plan. Examples: select, filter, withColumn, groupBy, join. Actions trigger execution and return a result or write data. Examples: count, collect, show, write, take, foreach. Rule of thumb: if it returns a DataFrame it's a transformation; if it returns a value or writes output it's an action.

Q14. What is lazy evaluation and why does Spark use it? mid

Lazy evaluation means transformations aren't executed when you write them — Spark just records them into a plan (the DAG). Execution only happens when you call an action. This lets Catalyst see the whole chain and optimize it as a unit: it can push a filter before a join, prune unused columns, and combine steps to avoid extra passes over the data. If Spark ran each line eagerly it couldn't do these global optimizations and would waste work.

Q15. What is a DAG, a stage, and a task? mid

The DAG (Directed Acyclic Graph) is the graph of transformations Spark builds from your code. When an action runs, Spark splits the DAG into stages — a stage is a run of operations that can happen without moving data across the network. Stage boundaries are shuffles. Each stage is split into tasks: one task per partition, each running the stage's logic on one chunk of data. Tasks are the smallest unit of parallel work sent to executors.

Q16. What causes a new stage boundary? mid

A shuffle — i.e. any wide transformation that requires data to be repartitioned across the network (groupBy, join on a non-broadcast key, distinct, repartition, sort). Everything that can be pipelined without moving data (map-like operations: select, filter, withColumn) stays in the same stage. So counting stages in the Spark UI roughly tells you how many shuffles your job has.

Q17. How do you read a CSV with an explicit schema? basic

Always define a schema in production — inferSchema=True makes Spark read the file twice and can guess types wrong.

from pyspark.sql.types import StructType, StructField, StringType, IntegerType, DoubleType

schema = StructType([
    StructField("order_id", StringType(), True),
    StructField("amount", DoubleType(), True),
    StructField("qty", IntegerType(), True),
])

df = (spark.read
      .option("header", True)
      .schema(schema)
      .csv("data/orders.csv"))
Q18. Why is Parquet the default file format for DE, not CSV? mid

Parquet is columnar (stores each column together), compressed, and carries its schema in the file. Benefits: you can read only the columns you need (column pruning), skip row-groups that can't match your filter using min/max stats (predicate pushdown), and files are much smaller. CSV is row-based, uncompressed, typeless text — Spark must read every byte and re-parse types. Rule: land raw as CSV/JSON, but store curated/analytical data as Parquet (or Delta).

Q19. How do you write a DataFrame to Parquet? basic 
df.write.mode("overwrite").parquet("out/orders")

# common modes: "overwrite", "append", "ignore", "error" (default)

mode controls what happens if the path exists. Reading back is just spark.read.parquet("out/orders") — no schema needed, it's embedded.

Q20. How do you read from a relational database via JDBC? mid 
df = (spark.read.format("jdbc")
      .option("url", "jdbc:postgresql://host:5432/db")
      .option("dbtable", "public.orders")
      .option("user", "u").option("password", "p")
      .option("partitionColumn", "id")   # parallel reads
      .option("lowerBound", 1).option("upperBound", 1000000)
      .option("numPartitions", 8)
      .load())

Without partitionColumn/numPartitions, Spark reads the whole table through one connection (one task) — slow. Those options let Spark issue parallel range queries.

Q21. select vs withColumn — when to use each? basic

select chooses/derives a specific set of output columns; withColumn adds or replaces one column while keeping all the others.

from pyspark.sql import functions as F

df.select("id", (F.col("amount") * 1.1).alias("with_tax"))

df.withColumn("with_tax", F.col("amount") * 1.1)

Chaining many withColumn calls is fine but a single select with several expressions can be cleaner and slightly cheaper to plan.

Q22. How do you filter rows? basic

filter and where are identical (where is a SQL-friendly alias).

df.filter(F.col("amount") > 100)
df.where("amount > 100 AND status = 'paid'")   # SQL string
df.filter((F.col("amount") > 100) & (F.col("status") == "paid"))

Use &/|/~ (not and/or/not) and wrap each condition in parentheses.

Q23. How do you do conditional logic (CASE WHEN)? basic 
from pyspark.sql import functions as F

df.withColumn("tier",
    F.when(F.col("amount") >= 1000, "gold")
     .when(F.col("amount") >= 100, "silver")
     .otherwise("bronze"))

when/otherwise is Spark's CASE WHEN ... ELSE. Omitting otherwise yields null for unmatched rows.

Q24. dropDuplicates vs distinct? basic

distinct() deduplicates over all columns. dropDuplicates(["a","b"]) deduplicates over a subset, keeping one (arbitrary) row per combination. Both trigger a shuffle.

df.distinct()
df.dropDuplicates(["user_id", "day"])   # one row per user per day

To keep the latest row per key deterministically, use a window with row_number and filter rn == 1 instead.

Q25. How do you do a groupBy with aggregations? basic 
from pyspark.sql import functions as F

(df.groupBy("state")
   .agg(F.count("*").alias("orders"),
        F.sum("amount").alias("revenue"),
        F.avg("amount").alias("avg_amount"),
        F.countDistinct("user_id").alias("users")))

groupBy(...).agg(...) is Spark's GROUP BY. It is a wide transformation (causes a shuffle). Pass multiple aggregations inside a single agg.

Q26. What join types does Spark support? basic

inner (default), left/left_outer, right, outer/full, left_semi (keep left rows that have a match, no right columns — like EXISTS), and left_anti (keep left rows with no match — like NOT EXISTS).

orders.join(customers, on="cust_id", how="left")
orders.join(blacklist, on="cust_id", how="left_anti")  # non-blacklisted
Q27. How do you write a window function (running total, rank)? mid 
from pyspark.sql import functions as F
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

w = Window.partitionBy("user_id").orderBy("order_date")

df = (df
  .withColumn("running_total", F.sum("amount").over(w))
  .withColumn("order_seq", F.row_number().over(w))
  .withColumn("prev_amount", F.lag("amount", 1).over(w)))

A window computes a value per row using a group of related rows, without collapsing them like groupBy does.

Q28. Find the top-N rows per group (e.g. top 3 orders per user). mid 
from pyspark.sql import functions as F
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

w = Window.partitionBy("user_id").orderBy(F.col("amount").desc())

top3 = (df.withColumn("rn", F.row_number().over(w))
          .filter(F.col("rn") <= 3)
          .drop("rn"))

This "row_number then filter" pattern is one of the most-tested Spark/SQL questions. Use rank if you want ties to share a rank, dense_rank to avoid gaps.

Q29. row_number vs rank vs dense_rank? mid

All assign a number over an ordered window. row_number: strictly 1,2,3,4 — ties broken arbitrarily. rank: ties get the same number, then it skips (1,1,3). dense_rank: ties get the same number, no skip (1,1,2). Use row_number for "exactly N per group," rank/dense_rank when tied values should share a position.

Q30. How do you flatten an array column (explode)? mid

explode turns each element of an array (or each key/value of a map) into its own row.

from pyspark.sql import functions as F

# df has columns: order_id, items = ["a","b","c"]
df.select("order_id", F.explode("items").alias("item"))
# -> one row per (order_id, item)

Use explode_outer to keep rows whose array is null/empty (produces a null row instead of dropping them).

Q31. How do you pivot data? mid 
(df.groupBy("user_id")
   .pivot("month", ["Jan","Feb","Mar"])   # list values = faster
   .agg(F.sum("amount")))

pivot rotates distinct row values into columns. Always pass the explicit value list when you know it — otherwise Spark runs an extra job to discover the distinct values, and can be slow/expensive.

Q32. How do you run raw SQL on a DataFrame? basic

Register the DataFrame as a temporary view, then query it. DataFrame API and SQL compile to the exact same optimized plan — use whichever is clearer.

df.createOrReplaceTempView("orders")

result = spark.sql("""
    SELECT state, SUM(amount) AS revenue
    FROM orders
    WHERE status = 'paid'
    GROUP BY state
""")
Q33. Temp view vs global temp view vs table? mid

createOrReplaceTempView: visible only within the current SparkSession, gone when it ends. createGlobalTempView: visible across sessions in the same application, referenced as global_temp.name. saveAsTable / a managed/external table: persisted in the metastore (Hive/Unity Catalog), survives the app and is queryable by others. Views are just query aliases (no data stored); tables persist data.

Q34. What is a partition in Spark? basic

A partition is a chunk of the DataFrame's data that lives on one executor and is processed by one task. If a DataFrame has 200 partitions, Spark can run up to 200 tasks in parallel (limited by available cores). Partitioning is how Spark achieves parallelism: more partitions = more parallel tasks (up to a point), but too many tiny partitions add scheduling overhead.

Q35. What is a shuffle and why is it expensive? mid

A shuffle is Spark redistributing data across the cluster so that rows with the same key end up on the same executor (needed for groupBy, join, distinct, etc.). It's expensive because it involves writing intermediate data to disk, serializing it, sending it over the network between executors, and reading it back — the slowest operations in a cluster. Minimizing and shrinking shuffles is the core of Spark performance tuning.

Q36. Wide vs narrow transformations? mid

Narrow: each input partition contributes to exactly one output partition — no data moves across the network (map, filter, select, withColumn, union). They pipeline within a stage. Wide: output partitions depend on data from many input partitions, requiring a shuffle (groupBy, join, distinct, repartition, sort). Wide transformations create stage boundaries and are where jobs get slow.

Q37. What is spark.sql.shuffle.partitions and how do you tune it? mid

It sets the number of partitions produced after a shuffle (default 200). If your post-shuffle data is small, 200 creates tiny partitions and wasteful overhead; if it's huge, 200 makes each partition too big (risking OOM/spill). Tune so each partition is roughly 100–200 MB. With AQE enabled (default in Spark 3), Spark coalesces shuffle partitions automatically, reducing the need to hand-tune this.

Q38. repartition vs coalesce? mid

repartition(n) reshuffles data into exactly n partitions (full shuffle, can increase or decrease, balances sizes, can repartition by column). coalesce(n) only reduces partition count by merging existing partitions without a full shuffle — cheaper, but can create uneven partitions. Use coalesce to reduce partitions cheaply before a write; use repartition when you need balanced partitions or to partition by a key.

df.coalesce(10).write.parquet(...)      # fewer output files, cheap
df.repartition(200, "user_id")           # balance + colocate by key
Q39. How do you decide the number of partitions for a job? hard

Target a partition size around 128–256 MB so tasks are neither tiny (overhead) nor huge (spill/OOM). A common heuristic: partitions ≈ total input size / target size, and aim for at least 2–4x the total executor cores so all cores stay busy and stragglers can be balanced. For shuffles, set spark.sql.shuffle.partitions accordingly (or let AQE coalesce). Always verify with the Spark UI: look at task input sizes and durations.

Q40. What is partitionBy on write, and how is it different from repartition? mid

repartition affects in-memory partitions (parallelism). partitionBy on write lays out files into a directory hierarchy on disk by column value — enabling partition pruning at read time.

df.write.partitionBy("year", "month").parquet("out/")
# out/year=2026/month=07/part-*.parquet

Then spark.read.parquet("out").filter("year=2026 AND month=7") reads only that folder, skipping the rest.

Q41. What is bucketing and when do you use it? hard

Bucketing pre-shuffles a table into a fixed number of buckets by a hash of the bucket column when you write it (.bucketBy(50, "user_id").saveAsTable(...)). If two tables are bucketed the same way on the join key, Spark can join them without a shuffle at query time, because matching keys are already co-located. Use it for large tables that are repeatedly joined/aggregated on the same key. It's a write-time cost that pays off across many reads.

Q42. partitionBy vs bucketBy? hard

partitionBy creates one folder per distinct value — great for low-cardinality columns you filter on (year, country); high-cardinality columns create millions of tiny folders (bad). bucketBy hashes values into a fixed number of files — good for high-cardinality join keys (user_id) to avoid shuffles. You can combine them. Rule: partition on filter columns, bucket on join columns.

Q43. What is a broadcast join and when does Spark use it? mid

When one side of a join is small, Spark sends (broadcasts) a full copy of that small table to every executor, so each executor can join its partitions of the big table locally — no shuffle of the big table. Spark auto-broadcasts tables below spark.sql.autoBroadcastJoinThreshold (default 10 MB). You can force it: big.join(F.broadcast(small), "id"). It's the single biggest join speedup when applicable.

Q44. What is a sort-merge join? mid

The default strategy for joining two large tables. Spark shuffles both sides so matching keys land on the same partition, sorts each partition by the key, then merges them like a zipper. It handles arbitrarily large tables but pays for two shuffles and two sorts. If one side is small, a broadcast join is much cheaper; sort-merge is the fallback when both sides are big.

Q45. What join strategies does Spark have, and how does it choose? hard

Main ones: Broadcast Hash Join (one side small — fastest), Sort-Merge Join (both large, default for equi-joins), Shuffle Hash Join (one side fits in memory per partition, builds a hash table), and Broadcast/Cartesian Nested Loop (non-equi or cross joins — avoid). Catalyst picks based on table size stats and thresholds; AQE can switch to broadcast at runtime once it sees the actual size. Check with df.explain().

Q46. Why can a join between two 10 TB tables blow up, and how do you handle it? hard

Neither side fits in memory to broadcast, so Spark does a sort-merge join — two massive shuffles, and any skewed key can OOM one task. Strategies: (1) filter/prune both sides first (predicate + column pushdown) to shrink data; (2) pre-bucket both tables on the join key to avoid the shuffle; (3) if one side has few distinct join keys, reduce it and broadcast; (4) enable AQE skew join handling; (5) salt the skewed keys; (6) increase shuffle partitions and executor memory. Also confirm you actually need all 10 TB — often an incremental/date-filtered join suffices.

Q47. What is data skew? mid

Skew is when data is unevenly distributed across partitions — typically one join/group key has vastly more rows than others (e.g. country = 'US' is 80% of rows, or a null key). After a shuffle those rows all land on one partition, so one task processes gigabytes while the rest finish instantly. That one straggler task determines the whole stage's runtime and can OOM. You spot it in the Spark UI: one task's duration and shuffle-read size dwarf the others.

Q48. How do you detect skew? mid

In the Spark UI → Stages tab, open the stage's task table and look at the max vs 75th-percentile task duration and shuffle-read bytes — a huge gap means skew. You can also count rows per key: df.groupBy("join_key").count().orderBy(F.desc("count")).show(). If a few keys dominate, you have skew.

Q49. How do you fix skew with salting? Show code. hard

Salting spreads a hot key across N sub-keys so its rows land on N partitions instead of one. Add a random salt to the big (skewed) side and explode the small side across all salt values, then join on (key, salt).

from pyspark.sql import functions as F

N = 10
big_salted = big.withColumn("salt", (F.rand()*N).cast("int"))

# replicate small side for every salt value
salts = spark.range(N).withColumnRenamed("id", "salt")
small_salted = small.crossJoin(salts)

joined = big_salted.join(small_salted, ["key", "salt"], "inner")

With AQE's skew-join optimization on, Spark can auto-split skewed partitions and you often don't need manual salting.

Q50. What is cache/persist and how do they differ? mid

Both store a DataFrame in memory (and/or disk) so it isn't recomputed each time it's reused. cache() is shorthand for persist(MEMORY_AND_DISK) (the default storage level for DataFrames). persist(level) lets you choose the storage level explicitly. Caching is only triggered by the next action; it's useful when a DataFrame is used in multiple downstream actions.

Q51. What are the persist storage levels? mid

MEMORY_ONLY: in RAM, deserialized (fastest, but recomputes partitions that don't fit). MEMORY_AND_DISK: RAM, spilling to disk if it doesn't fit (DataFrame default). MEMORY_ONLY_SER / MEMORY_AND_DISK_SER: serialized (less RAM, more CPU). DISK_ONLY: disk only. Adding _2 replicates each partition on two nodes for fault tolerance. Choose based on whether the data fits in memory and how expensive recomputation is.

Q52. When does caching hurt rather than help? hard

When the DataFrame is used only once (no reuse), caching just wastes memory and adds a materialization cost. It also hurts when the cached data is large enough to evict other cached data or push executors toward OOM/GC pressure, and when the source is already fast to reread (small Parquet with pushdown). Cache selectively, only for datasets reused by multiple actions, and unpersist() when done.

Q53. What is Adaptive Query Execution (AQE)? mid

AQE (on by default since Spark 3.2) lets Spark re-optimize a query during execution using real runtime statistics, not just estimates. Three main features: (1) dynamically coalescing shuffle partitions (merges tiny post-shuffle partitions), (2) switching sort-merge to broadcast join when a side turns out small, and (3) skew join handling (splitting oversized partitions). It reduces manual tuning significantly.

Q54. What is predicate pushdown and column pruning? mid

Predicate pushdown: Spark pushes filters down to the data source so unneeded rows are skipped before being read (e.g. Parquet skips row-groups whose min/max stats can't match your WHERE). Column pruning: Spark reads only the columns your query references. Both dramatically cut I/O. They work best with columnar formats like Parquet/ORC and partitioned layouts; with CSV, Spark still has to read the whole file.

Q55. What is the small-files problem and how do you fix it? hard

Writing many tiny files (e.g. streaming micro-batches, or over-partitioned writes) creates thousands of small Parquet files. Each file = task overhead, metadata bloat, and slow listing — reads become dominated by opening files, not reading data. Fixes: coalesce/repartition before writing to control output file count; periodically compact (read many small files, rewrite as fewer big ones ~128–512 MB); on Delta Lake use OPTIMIZE; avoid partitioning on high-cardinality columns.

Q56. Static vs dynamic partition overwrite on write? hard

With mode("overwrite") on a partitioned table, static (default, partitionOverwriteMode=static) overwrites the entire table/target path. dynamic (spark.conf.set("spark.sql.sources.partitionOverwriteMode","dynamic")) overwrites only the partitions present in the DataFrame being written, leaving other partitions untouched. Dynamic is what you want for incremental daily loads — you rewrite just today's date= partition without clobbering history.

Q57. What is a UDF and why are Python UDFs slow? mid

A UDF (User Defined Function) lets you run custom Python logic on a column. It's slow in PySpark because each row must be serialized from the JVM, sent to a Python worker process, executed row-by-row in Python, then serialized back — this crosses the JVM↔Python boundary and bypasses Catalyst/Tungsten optimizations (it's a black box the optimizer can't see into). Always prefer built-in pyspark.sql.functions first.

Q58. Show a regular UDF and why you'd avoid it. mid 
from pyspark.sql.functions import udf
from pyspark.sql.types import StringType

@udf(StringType())
def bucketize(x):
    return "hi" if x > 100 else "lo"

df.withColumn("b", bucketize("amount"))   # slow, row-by-row Python

The same logic with a built-in is far faster: F.when(F.col("amount") > 100, "hi").otherwise("lo"). Only reach for a UDF when no built-in exists.

Q59. What is a pandas UDF (vectorized UDF) and why is it faster? hard

A pandas UDF processes data in batches as pandas Series using Apache Arrow for zero-copy transfer between the JVM and Python, instead of one row at a time. This slashes serialization overhead and lets you use vectorized pandas/NumPy operations. It's typically several times faster than a regular Python UDF while still allowing custom Python logic.

import pandas as pd
from pyspark.sql.functions import pandas_udf

@pandas_udf("double")
def add_tax(s: pd.Series) -> pd.Series:
    return s * 1.1

df.withColumn("with_tax", add_tax("amount"))
Q60. UDF vs built-in vs pandas UDF — decision order? mid

(1) Built-in pyspark.sql.functions — always first; fully optimized by Catalyst/Tungsten. (2) Spark SQL expression via expr() if the logic is expressible in SQL. (3) pandas UDF — when you need Python/NumPy logic but want vectorized speed. (4) Regular Python UDF — last resort, only for logic no other option supports. Never write a UDF for something a built-in already does.

Q61. Why is serialization important in Spark, and what is Kryo? hard

Whenever data crosses the network (shuffles, broadcasts) or is cached in serialized form, objects must be serialized to bytes and back — a real CPU cost. Java's default serializer is slow and produces large output; Kryo is faster and more compact (spark.serializer=org.apache.spark.serializer.KryoSerializer). This mainly matters for RDD/custom-object workloads; DataFrames already use Tungsten's efficient binary format, so Kryo tuning is less relevant for pure DataFrame pipelines.

Q62. What is executor memory made of? hard

An executor's JVM heap (spark.executor.memory) is split by the unified memory manager into: reserved memory (~300 MB), and the rest into execution memory (for shuffles, joins, sorts, aggregations) and storage memory (for cached data). Execution and storage share a pool and can borrow from each other (spark.memory.fraction, default 0.6). Separately, overhead memory (spark.executor.memoryOverhead) holds off-heap/JVM/Python-worker needs outside the heap.

Q63. What causes an executor OutOfMemory (OOM) error? hard

Common causes: (1) skew — one partition is huge; (2) a huge shuffle with too few partitions (each too big); (3) a large broadcast that doesn't fit; (4) collect() pulling a big result to the driver (driver OOM); (5) too much caching crowding out execution memory; (6) wide aggregations/collect_list building giant in-memory structures; (7) heavy Python UDFs blowing the Python worker's overhead memory. Fixes match the cause: more/ smaller partitions, salting, avoid collect, raise memory/overhead, drop unnecessary caching.

Q64. "collect() is dangerous" — why? mid

collect() pulls the entire DataFrame from all executors back to the driver's memory as a Python list. On big data this OOMs the driver instantly and serializes everything through one process. Use show() to peek, take(n)/limit(n) for a sample, aggregations to reduce first, or write to save results distributed. Only collect() small, already-reduced results.

Q65. What is spilling and why does it slow a job? mid

When execution memory can't hold the data for a shuffle/sort/aggregation, Spark spills the excess to local disk, then reads it back later. It prevents OOM but is far slower than staying in memory (disk I/O + extra serialization). Frequent spill (visible as "Spill (Memory)/(Disk)" in the Spark UI) signals partitions are too big or memory too small — fix with more partitions, more executor memory, or less data per task.

Q66. What is speculative execution? hard

With spark.speculation=true, if some tasks in a stage run much slower than the median (stragglers, often due to a slow node), Spark launches duplicate copies of those tasks on other executors and takes whichever finishes first. It helps with transient hardware slowness but does not fix data skew (the duplicate hits the same big partition) and wastes resources if enabled carelessly.

Q67. What is Structured Streaming? mid

Spark's streaming engine that treats a live data stream as an unbounded table that keeps growing. You write the same DataFrame code as for batch; Spark runs it incrementally as new data arrives. Under the hood it processes data in small micro-batches (default) — each trigger reads new data, runs your query, and updates the sink. This gives you batch-like APIs with near-real-time results and exactly-once guarantees.

Q68. What are the source, transform, and sink of a streaming job? mid 
stream = (spark.readStream.format("kafka")      # SOURCE
          .option("subscribe", "events").load())

agg = (stream.groupBy("user_id")                 # TRANSFORM
             .count())

query = (agg.writeStream.format("console")       # SINK
         .outputMode("update")
         .option("checkpointLocation", "/chk")
         .start())
query.awaitTermination()

Source = where data comes from (Kafka, files, rate). Transform = your DataFrame logic. Sink = where results go (console, files, Kafka, Delta, foreachBatch).

Q69. What is a trigger in Structured Streaming? mid

A trigger controls how often a micro-batch runs. Options: default (process each batch as soon as the previous finishes), processingTime="10 seconds" (fixed interval), availableNow (process all currently-available data in batches, then stop — great for incremental batch), and the deprecated once. Set with .trigger(processingTime="30 seconds").

Q70. What is a watermark and why do you need it? hard

Data can arrive late (out of order). A watermark tells Spark how late data may be, so it knows when a time window is "closed" and its aggregation state can be dropped from memory.

events.withWatermark("event_time", "10 minutes") \
      .groupBy(F.window("event_time", "5 minutes")).count()

Here Spark waits up to 10 minutes for late events before finalizing each 5-minute window, then discards that window's state — without a watermark, state grows forever and eventually OOMs.

Q71. What are the output modes in Structured Streaming? mid

append: only new rows are written; for aggregations, a row is emitted once its window is finalized (needs a watermark). update: only rows whose result changed this batch are written (good for running aggregations to a key-value sink). complete: the entire result table is rewritten every batch (only feasible for small aggregated results). Choose based on your query type and sink.

Q72. What is checkpointing in streaming and why is it required? mid

A checkpoint is a durable location (e.g. S3/DBFS) where Spark saves the stream's progress: which offsets were read and the intermediate aggregation state. If the job crashes and restarts, it resumes exactly where it left off instead of reprocessing or losing data — this is what enables exactly-once semantics with replayable sources. It's set via .option("checkpointLocation", "/path") and is mandatory for most sinks.

Q73. How does Structured Streaming achieve exactly-once? hard

Three ingredients: (1) a replayable source that tracks offsets (Kafka, files) so Spark can re-read the same data after a failure; (2) checkpointing of read offsets and query state; and (3) an idempotent/transactional sink (e.g. Delta Lake's atomic commits, or file sinks with a manifest) so re-processed batches don't produce duplicates. With all three, each input record affects the result exactly once even across failures/restarts.

Q74. Micro-batch vs continuous processing? hard

Micro-batch (default): Spark collects new data over a short interval and processes it as a tiny batch — latency in the hundreds of ms to seconds, high throughput, full feature support. Continuous processing (experimental): processes records one at a time for ~1 ms latency, but supports only a limited set of operations and no aggregations. Almost all production jobs use micro-batch.

Q75. What is foreachBatch and when is it useful? hard

foreachBatch gives you each micro-batch as a normal batch DataFrame, so you can apply any batch operation the streaming sink doesn't natively support — e.g. upsert/MERGE into a Delta table, write to a JDBC database, or write the same batch to multiple sinks.

def upsert(batch_df, batch_id):
    batch_df.write.mode("append").saveAsTable("t")

stream.writeStream.foreachBatch(upsert) \
      .option("checkpointLocation","/chk").start()
Q76. What is spark-submit? basic

The command-line tool to launch a Spark application on a cluster. You point it at your Python/JAR file and pass resource and config flags.

spark-submit \
  --master yarn --deploy-mode cluster \
  --num-executors 10 \
  --executor-cores 4 \
  --executor-memory 8g \
  --conf spark.sql.shuffle.partitions=400 \
  my_job.py --date 2026-07-05
Q77. Explain executor-cores, executor-memory, num-executors. mid

executor-cores: number of concurrent tasks one executor runs (= its parallelism). executor-memory: heap size per executor. num-executors: how many executor processes to launch. Total parallelism = num-executors × executor-cores. Total cluster memory used ≈ num-executors × (executor-memory + overhead). You size these to fit the cluster's nodes while keeping each executor a reasonable size.

Q78. Do the executor sizing math for a cluster of 6 nodes, 16 cores & 64 GB each. hard

Reserve 1 core + ~1 GB per node for the OS/daemons → 15 cores, 63 GB usable per node. Use ~5 cores per executor (more than 5 hurts HDFS/S3 throughput). 15 / 5 = 3 executors per node → 6 nodes × 3 = 18 executors, minus 1 for the driver = 17 executors. Memory per executor = 63 GB / 3 ≈ 21 GB; subtract ~7% overhead → --executor-memory 19g. So: --num-executors 17 --executor-cores 5 --executor-memory 19g. Total ~85 concurrent tasks.

Q79. Why not use one giant executor per node (all cores/memory)? hard

Because very large executors cause long JVM garbage-collection pauses (huge heaps) and more than ~5 concurrent tasks per executor saturate HDFS/S3 I/O and hurt throughput. And a single "fat" executor per node gives no room for overhead memory and makes failures costlier (losing one executor loses a whole node's work). Several medium executors (~5 cores, ~20 GB) balance parallelism, GC, and fault tolerance better.

Q80. What is dynamic allocation? mid

With spark.dynamicAllocation.enabled=true, Spark adds executors when there's a backlog of pending tasks and releases idle ones back to the cluster manager, instead of holding a fixed count. It saves resources on shared clusters and adapts to varying workload size. It requires an external shuffle service (or similar) so shuffle data survives when executors are removed. Databricks/EMR support it.

Q81. Databricks vs EMR vs Synapse vs Glue — what's the difference? mid

Databricks: fully managed Spark platform (notebooks, Delta Lake, Unity Catalog, jobs) on AWS/Azure/GCP — the market leader, easiest, most polished. EMR: AWS's managed Hadoop/Spark clusters — more DIY, you manage more config, cheaper at scale. Synapse: Azure's analytics service with Spark pools + SQL pools integrated. Glue: AWS serverless Spark for ETL (no cluster to manage, pay per job) — great for pipelines, less interactive. All run the same Spark engine; they differ in management, ecosystem, and cost model.

Q82. What does Databricks add on top of open-source Spark? mid

A managed runtime (optimized Spark, Photon vectorized engine), collaborative notebooks, cluster management/autoscaling, Delta Lake (ACID transactions, time travel, MERGE, OPTIMIZE on data lakes), Unity Catalog (governance/lineage), Jobs/Workflows scheduling, and MLflow. You get Spark without managing infrastructure, plus features that make a data lake behave more like a warehouse (the "lakehouse").

Q83. Show a full read → transform → write mini pipeline. mid 
from pyspark.sql import functions as F

df = spark.read.option("header", True).csv("orders.csv")

clean = (df
    .withColumn("amount", F.col("amount").cast("double"))
    .filter(F.col("amount").isNotNull())
    .dropDuplicates(["order_id"]))

agg = (clean.groupBy("state")
       .agg(F.sum("amount").alias("revenue")))

agg.write.mode("overwrite").partitionBy("state").parquet("out/rev")
Q84. How do you handle nulls in PySpark? basic 
from pyspark.sql import functions as F

df.na.drop(subset=["amount"])                 # drop rows null in amount
df.na.fill({"amount": 0, "state": "NA"})      # fill defaults
