df.withColumn("amt", F.coalesce("amount", F.lit(0)))
df.filter(F.col("state").isNull())            # find nulls

Note: null == null is not true in Spark — use isNull()/isNotNull(), and remember joins drop null-key matches by default.

Q85. How do you rename, drop, and cast columns? basic 
df.withColumnRenamed("old", "new")
df.drop("col_a", "col_b")
df.withColumn("id", F.col("id").cast("long"))
df.selectExpr("id", "CAST(amount AS double) AS amount")
Q86. What's the difference between orderBy and sortWithinPartitions? mid

orderBy/sort produce a globally sorted result, which requires a shuffle (a range partitioner) — expensive. sortWithinPartitions sorts each partition independently with no shuffle — useful when you only need local ordering (e.g. before a partitioned write, or feeding an operation that just needs sorted-within-partition data). Avoid a global orderBy unless you truly need total ordering.

Q87. How do you union two DataFrames safely? mid

union combines by column position, so mismatched column order silently corrupts data. Prefer unionByName, which aligns by column name and can tolerate missing columns.

a.unionByName(b, allowMissingColumns=True)

Both are narrow transformations (no shuffle). They do not deduplicate — add .distinct() if needed.

Q88. How do you read the physical plan and spot a shuffle? mid

Call df.explain() (or df.explain("formatted")). Look for Exchange nodes — each Exchange is a shuffle. Also note the join type shown (BroadcastHashJoin vs SortMergeJoin) and any *(n) markers indicating whole-stage codegen. Reducing Exchange nodes (via broadcast joins, bucketing, or avoiding needless repartitions) is how you cut cost.

Q89. Scenario: a daily job that used to take 20 min now takes 3 hours. Debug steps? hard

(1) Open the Spark UI and find the slow stage. (2) Check task time distribution — is one task a straggler (skew, maybe a new hot key or a flood of nulls)? (3) Check input data volume — did the source grow, or did a full reload happen instead of incremental? (4) Look for excessive spill or GC (memory pressure). (5) Check the plan — did a join fall back from broadcast to sort-merge because a table crossed the threshold? (6) Look for a small-files explosion at the source. (7) Check cluster health — fewer executors, a lost node, or noisy-neighbor contention. Fix the specific bottleneck the UI reveals.

Q90. Scenario: "ExecutorLostFailure" in the logs — what does it mean and what do you check? hard

An executor died and its tasks must be rerun. Common causes: OOM (the container was killed by YARN/K8s for exceeding memory — check for "killed by YARN for exceeding memory limits", raise memoryOverhead), a node failure or spot-instance reclaim, long GC pauses triggering a heartbeat timeout, or disk full from shuffle spill. Check the executor's stderr, memory config, and whether skew is overloading one executor. Fixes: increase overhead/memory, add partitions, handle skew, or use on-demand instances for the driver.

Q91. Scenario: design an incremental daily load into a partitioned table. hard

Read only the new data (source filtered by ingestion date or a high-watermark like updated_at > last_run, ideally using source-side partition pruning). Transform/clean it. Write with dynamic partition overwrite to the target's date=YYYY-MM-DD partition so you rewrite only today's slice without touching history. Control output file count with coalesce/repartition to avoid small files. Store the new watermark for the next run, and make the write idempotent (overwrite-by-partition, or MERGE on Delta) so a re-run doesn't duplicate. Orchestrate with Airflow, passing the run date as a parameter.

Q92. Scenario: you must join a 5 TB fact with a 50 MB dimension. Best approach? mid

Broadcast the 50 MB dimension so the 5 TB fact is never shuffled: fact.join(F.broadcast(dim), "key"). Since 50 MB may exceed the default 10 MB auto-broadcast threshold, either raise spark.sql.autoBroadcastJoinThreshold or use the explicit broadcast() hint. Each executor keeps the small table in memory and joins its fact partitions locally — no shuffle, dramatically faster than sort-merge.

Q93. Scenario: your write produces 50,000 tiny Parquet files. Fix it. mid

Too many partitions/tasks each wrote a small file. Reduce output partitions before writing: df.coalesce(200).write... (or repartition if you need balanced sizes), and avoid partitionBy on a high-cardinality column. Target ~128–512 MB per file. For an existing mess, run a compaction job (read all, rewrite as fewer files) or, on Delta Lake, OPTIMIZE table to bin-pack small files.

Q94. Scenario: a groupBy is slow with 200 partitions but the result has only 5 groups. Why? hard

After the shuffle, all rows for a group land on one partition, so only 5 of the 200 post-shuffle partitions have data — 195 tasks do nothing and any single overloaded group becomes a straggler (skew). If those 5 groups are also huge, you may spill/OOM. Options: enable AQE (coalesces empty partitions and can split skewed ones), pre-aggregate with a partial/combiner step, or salt the skewed group keys. Lowering spark.sql.shuffle.partitions also removes the empty-task overhead.

Q95. How do you deduplicate keeping the latest record per key? mid 
from pyspark.sql import functions as F
from pyspark.sql.window import Window

w = Window.partitionBy("id").orderBy(F.col("updated_at").desc())

latest = (df.withColumn("rn", F.row_number().over(w))
            .filter(F.col("rn") == 1)
            .drop("rn"))

This is deterministic (unlike dropDuplicates) — it keeps the row with the newest updated_at per id, the standard pattern for CDC/upsert staging.

Q96. What is the difference between count() being an action and being slow? mid

count() is an action, so it triggers the whole lineage to execute — if you call it repeatedly (e.g. for logging after each step), you re-run all upstream transformations each time. On big pipelines this is a common accidental cost. If you need counts for validation, cache first, use approximate counts, or restructure to count once. Don't sprinkle count()/show() through production code.

Q97. What is Delta Lake and why do DEs use it? mid

Delta Lake is a storage layer on top of Parquet that adds a transaction log, giving data lakes warehouse-like features: ACID transactions (safe concurrent writes), MERGE/upserts and deletes (hard with plain Parquet), time travel (query old versions), schema enforcement/evolution, and OPTIMIZE for compaction. It's the default table format on Databricks and central to the "lakehouse" pattern — you get reliability and updates without a separate database.

Q98. How do you configure Spark settings, and which are most impactful? mid

Set via spark-submit --conf, SparkSession.builder.config(...), or spark.conf.set(...) at runtime (for SQL/session configs). The highest-impact ones for DE: spark.sql.shuffle.partitions, spark.sql.autoBroadcastJoinThreshold, spark.sql.adaptive.enabled (AQE), executor memory/cores/count, spark.executor.memoryOverhead, and spark.sql.sources.partitionOverwriteMode. Note some configs are fixed at startup (executor sizing) while session/SQL configs can change at runtime.

Q99. Explain the full life of a Spark job from your code to results. hard

(1) You write DataFrame transformations — Spark records them lazily into a logical plan. (2) You call an action. (3) Catalyst analyzes and optimizes the logical plan into a physical plan. (4) The driver splits it into a DAG of stages at each shuffle boundary. (5) Each stage becomes tasks (one per partition). (6) The driver asks the cluster manager for executors and schedules tasks onto them. (7) Executors read their partitions, run the tasks (with Tungsten codegen), and shuffle data between stages as needed. (8) Results are returned to the driver or written to the sink. Failed tasks/partitions are recomputed via lineage.

Q100. Give a checklist to optimize a slow Spark job. hard

(1) Read the Spark UI first — find the slow stage and its task skew/spill. (2) Reduce data early: filter and select only needed columns (predicate pushdown, column pruning); use Parquet/Delta. (3) Minimize shuffles: broadcast small dimensions, bucket for repeated joins, avoid needless repartition/global sort. (4) Handle skew: AQE skew join, salting. (5) Right-size partitions (~128–256 MB) and tune shuffle.partitions. (6) Enable AQE. (7) Cache only reused DataFrames; avoid stray count()/collect(). (8) Fix small files with coalesce/compaction. (9) Tune memory: raise overhead if OOM, add partitions if spilling. (10) Prefer built-ins over UDFs. (11) Size executors sensibly (~5 cores, medium memory).

Apache Kafka — Event Streaming for Real-Time Data

The distributed log that moves events (payments, clicks, CDC) between systems in real time — the backbone of every fintech data platform.

What & Why

Imagine every important thing that happens in a company — a card swipe, a login, a fraud check, a row changing in a database — as an event. An event is just a small immutable fact: "at 10:03:22, card 4111… was charged ₹450 at Merchant X". Event streaming means capturing these events the moment they happen and delivering them, in order, to any system that wants them — not once a night in a batch, but within milliseconds. Apache Kafka is the most popular system for doing this.

Mentally, Kafka is a giant, durable, append-only log (like a never-ending notebook you only ever add lines to). Producers write events to the end of the log; consumers read from wherever they left off. Kafka keeps the events on disk for a while (hours, days, or forever) and replicates them across multiple machines so nothing is lost if one machine dies. That's the whole magic: a fast, replayable, fault-tolerant pipe between systems that decouples the app that produces data from the many apps that consume it.

Fintech loves Kafka for concrete reasons. Payments: a single transaction fans out to ledger, fraud scoring, notifications, and analytics — Kafka lets one producer feed all of them without the payment service knowing they exist. Fraud detection: you need to score a transaction in milliseconds against recent behaviour; a stream of events feeding a real-time model is exactly what Kafka provides. CDC (Change Data Capture): instead of hammering the production Postgres/MySQL with queries, tools like Debezium read the DB's write-ahead log and publish every insert/update/delete to Kafka, so your warehouse and search index stay in sync in near real time. Audit & replay: because the log is durable and replayable, you can re-process history when you fix a bug or build a new consumer — a regulatory dream.

Where it sits in a data platform: Kafka is the ingestion and transport layer. Source apps and databases publish to Kafka; stream processors (Kafka Streams, ksqlDB, Spark/Flink Structured Streaming) transform in flight; and sinks (data warehouse, S3/lake, Elasticsearch, another microservice) consume the results. It replaces brittle point-to-point integrations and nightly batch files with one central nervous system.

Learning Checklist

Projects & Practice

  • Run Kafka locally with docker-compose — spin up a single-broker Kafka in KRaft mode (no ZooKeeper) plus the CLI tools. Conceptually: one docker-compose.yml service using image apache/kafka:3.7.0, set KAFKA_NODE_ID, KAFKA_PROCESS_ROLES=broker,controller, KAFKA_LISTENERS / KAFKA_ADVERTISED_LISTENERS (e.g. PLAINTEXT://localhost:9092), a controller listener, and KAFKA_CONTROLLER_QUORUM_VOTERS=1@localhost:9093. Then docker compose up -d and create a topic with kafka-topics.sh --create --topic payments --partitions 3. Proves: you can stand up infra and reason about listeners/ports.
  • Python producer & consumer simulating payment events — using kafka-python, write a producer that emits JSON payment events keyed by card_id to topic payments, and a consumer group that reads and prints running totals per card. Proves: keys → partitioning → ordering per card, consumer groups, offset commits, delivery semantics in practice.
  • Wire Kafka into Spark Structured Streaming — read the payments topic with spark.readStream.format("kafka"), parse the JSON, aggregate spend per merchant in 1-minute windows, and write results to console/Parquet. Proves: Kafka-as-source, schema-on-read, windowed streaming aggregation — the exact pattern in fraud/analytics pipelines.
  • CDC mini-lab with Debezium (stretch) — run Postgres + Kafka Connect + Debezium Postgres connector via Docker; change rows in a accounts table and watch insert/update/delete events land in a Kafka topic. Proves: understanding of CDC, the real way fintech mirrors OLTP databases into the lake.

Interview Q&A (50)

Q1. What problem does Kafka solve? basic

Kafka decouples the systems that produce data from the many systems that consume it, and delivers events durably and in order, in real time. Before Kafka, you built brittle point-to-point integrations (app A calls app B calls app C) or nightly batch files. Kafka replaces that with one central, replayable log: producers write once, and any number of consumers read independently at their own pace. It buffers spikes, survives machine failures, and lets you add new consumers later without touching producers.

Q2. Explain the pub-sub model in Kafka. basic

Pub-sub (publish-subscribe) means producers publish messages to a named topic without knowing who reads them, and consumers subscribe to that topic without knowing who wrote the data. The topic is the meeting point. Unlike a traditional message queue where a message is consumed once and deleted, Kafka retains messages so many independent consumer groups can each read the full stream. This decoupling is why one payments topic can feed the ledger, fraud, and analytics simultaneously.

Q3. What is a topic, and what is a partition? basic

A topic is a named category/stream of events (e.g. payments). A topic is physically split into one or more partitions, each of which is an ordered, append-only log stored on a broker. Partitions are the unit of parallelism and ordering: Kafka guarantees order within a partition but not across partitions. More partitions = more consumers can read in parallel = higher throughput.

Q4. What is an offset? basic

An offset is a monotonically increasing integer ID assigned to each message within a partition — its position in that partition's log (0, 1, 2, …). Consumers track which offset they've read up to. To resume after a restart, a consumer commits its offset (e.g. "I've processed up to offset 4500 in partition 2"). Offsets are per-partition, so the same number in two partitions refers to different messages.

Q5. How does a message get assigned to a partition? basic

If the message has a key, Kafka hashes the key (murmur2) and takes modulo of partition count, so the same key always lands in the same partition — this gives per-key ordering. If there's no key, the default partitioner spreads messages across partitions (sticky/round-robin) for balance. You can also write a custom partitioner. Example: keying payment events by card_id means all events for one card are ordered in one partition.

Q6. What is a consumer group? basic

A consumer group is a set of consumer instances that share a group.id and cooperate to read a topic. Kafka assigns each partition to exactly one consumer in the group, so the group divides the work and scales horizontally. Different groups reading the same topic each get the full stream independently. If you have 6 partitions and 3 consumers in a group, each reads 2 partitions; add a 4th consumer and Kafka rebalances.

Q7. What is a broker and a Kafka cluster? basic

A broker is a single Kafka server process that stores partition data and serves reads/writes. A cluster is a set of brokers working together. Partitions and their replicas are spread across brokers so load is balanced and no single broker holds everything. Clients bootstrap by connecting to any broker, which tells them the full cluster topology (which broker leads which partition).

Q8. What is ZooKeeper, and what is KRaft? basic

Historically Kafka used ZooKeeper (a separate cluster) to store metadata: which broker is controller, topic configs, partition leadership. Since Kafka 2.8+ and production-ready in 3.3+, KRaft mode replaces ZooKeeper with a built-in Raft-based metadata quorum — brokers themselves manage metadata. New deployments should use KRaft: simpler, fewer moving parts, faster metadata operations. In interviews, mention that ZooKeeper is being removed entirely in Kafka 4.x.

Q9. Is Kafka a message queue or a database? basic

Neither exactly — it's a distributed commit log. Like a queue, it moves messages between systems; unlike a queue, messages aren't deleted on read and can be re-read by many consumers. Like a database, it durably stores data on disk and replicates it; unlike a database, you can't query it by arbitrary keys — you read sequentially by offset. Think "durable, replayable stream" rather than queue or DB.

Q10. Why is Kafka so fast? mid

Three main reasons. (1) Sequential disk I/O: appending to and reading a log sequentially is nearly as fast as memory and far faster than random access. (2) Zero-copy: Kafka uses sendfile to move data from page cache to the network socket without copying through the app, saving CPU. (3) Batching & compression: producers batch many records and compress them, so fewer, bigger network/disk operations. It also relies heavily on the OS page cache rather than a JVM heap cache.

Q11. What does "retention" mean in Kafka? basic

Retention controls how long Kafka keeps messages before deleting them. It's configured per topic by time (retention.ms, e.g. 7 days) and/or size (retention.bytes). Once data is older/bigger than the limit, old log segments are deleted. Because data is retained regardless of whether it's been consumed, a new consumer can start from the beginning (auto.offset.reset=earliest) and replay history, as long as it's still within retention.

Q12. Why does fintech use Kafka specifically? basic

Fintech needs three things Kafka gives cheaply: (1) real-time — payments and fraud can't wait for a nightly batch; (2) durability & audit — a replayable, ordered log is a regulator-friendly record of every event; (3) fan-out — one transaction event feeds ledger, fraud, notifications, and analytics without coupling. CDC via Debezium also lets them mirror OLTP databases into the lake without straining production DBs.

Q13. What do producer acks settings (0, 1, all) mean? mid

acks=0: fire-and-forget, the producer doesn't wait for any acknowledgement — fastest, but data can be lost. acks=1: the leader broker confirms it wrote the record, but if the leader crashes before followers replicate, you can lose it. acks=all (a.k.a. -1): the leader waits until all in-sync replicas (ISR) have the record — safest, needed for durability guarantees. For payments you use acks=all combined with min.insync.replicas>=2.

Q14. What is a producer key and why use it? mid

The key determines the partition, and thus ordering. All records with the same key go to the same partition and stay ordered relative to each other. Use a key when order matters per entity — e.g. key by account_id so all events for one account are processed in sequence (deposit before withdrawal). No key = random spread = higher parallelism but no cross-message ordering.

producer.send("payments", key=b"card_4111", value=b'{"amt":450}')
Q15. What are linger.ms and batch.size? mid

They control producer batching. batch.size is the max bytes per partition batch; linger.ms is how long the producer waits to fill a batch before sending. Default linger.ms=0 sends immediately. Raising it (e.g. 5-20ms) lets more records accumulate into fewer, larger, compressed batches — higher throughput at the cost of slightly more latency. It's the classic throughput-vs-latency knob.

Q16. What happens when a producer's send fails? mid

The producer retries automatically up to retries (effectively infinite by default in modern clients) within delivery.timeout.ms. Retries can cause duplicates or reordering unless you enable idempotence. With enable.idempotence=true (default in newer versions), Kafka de-dupes retries and preserves order. If the timeout is exceeded, the send callback receives an exception and your app must decide what to do (log, dead-letter, alert).

Q17. How do consumers commit offsets, and what's the risk of auto-commit? mid

Consumers periodically record how far they've read. With enable.auto.commit=true, the client commits offsets on a timer (auto.commit.interval.ms). The risk: it may commit offset 500 before you've actually finished processing message 500 — if you crash, those messages are skipped (data loss) or, if committed after a poll but before processing, reprocessed. For correctness, disable auto-commit and call commitSync() only after successfully processing a batch.

Q18. What is a consumer rebalance and when does it happen? mid

A rebalance is when Kafka reassigns partitions among the consumers in a group. It triggers when a consumer joins, leaves, or is deemed dead (missed heartbeats), or when partitions are added. During a rebalance (with the classic eager protocol) all consumers stop consuming briefly ("stop-the-world"), which hurts latency. Cooperative/incremental rebalancing (default in newer clients) reassigns only affected partitions, reducing disruption.

Q19. What is max.poll.records and max.poll.interval.ms? mid

max.poll.records caps how many records one poll() returns. max.poll.interval.ms is the max time allowed between polls — if your processing of a batch takes longer than this, Kafka assumes the consumer is dead and kicks it out, triggering a rebalance. So if processing is slow, either process faster, lower max.poll.records, or raise max.poll.interval.ms. This is a very common cause of "my consumer keeps rebalancing" incidents.

Q20. Explain heartbeats vs poll in consumer liveness. hard

Two independent liveness checks. A background heartbeat thread pings the group coordinator every heartbeat.interval.ms; if none arrives within session.timeout.ms, the consumer is considered dead. Separately, the main thread must call poll() at least every max.poll.interval.ms; if processing stalls it, the consumer is removed even though heartbeats are fine. Heartbeats detect a crashed process; poll interval detects a stuck/slow processing loop.

Q21. How do you achieve parallelism in Kafka? mid

Through partitions and consumer instances. A topic's throughput scales with partition count; a consumer group's throughput scales with the number of consumers up to the partition count. If a topic has N partitions, at most N consumers in a group do useful work — extra consumers sit idle. So you choose partition count based on target parallelism (plus room to grow), because you can add partitions but not easily reduce them.

Q22. What happens if you have more consumers than partitions? mid

The surplus consumers are assigned zero partitions and stay idle — they consume nothing until a partition frees up (e.g. another consumer dies). Kafka's parallelism ceiling within a single group is the number of partitions. This is why you size partitions with future scale in mind: if you'll eventually want 20 parallel consumers, you need at least 20 partitions.

Q23. Can Kafka guarantee ordering? To what extent? mid

Kafka guarantees ordering within a partition, not across partitions. To keep events for one entity ordered, give them the same key so they land in the same partition. If you use multiple partitions and need global order, you can't get it without a single partition (which kills parallelism). Also beware: producer retries without idempotence can reorder within a partition — enable idempotence to preserve order under retry.

Q24. What is a custom partitioner and when would you write one? hard

A partitioner decides which partition a record goes to. The default hashes the key. You'd write a custom one when default hashing causes hot partitions — e.g. a few huge merchants dominate one partition. A custom partitioner could route those "whale" keys across multiple partitions or apply business logic (route VIP customers to a dedicated partition). You implement the Partitioner interface (Java) or pass a callable in kafka-python.

Q25. What are the three delivery semantics? mid

At-most-once: messages may be lost but never duplicated (commit offset before processing). At-least-once: messages are never lost but may be duplicated (process, then commit; a crash before commit means reprocessing) — the common default. Exactly-once: each message affects the result once and only once, achieved with idempotent producers + transactions. Most systems use at-least-once plus idempotent processing (dedup by key) as a pragmatic exactly-once.

Q26. What is an idempotent producer and how does it work? hard

With enable.idempotence=true, the producer is assigned a Producer ID (PID) and stamps each record with a sequence number per partition. The broker tracks the last sequence it accepted per (PID, partition); if a retry resends a record with an already-seen sequence, the broker discards the duplicate. This prevents duplicate writes caused by retries and preserves ordering, giving exactly-once delivery to the log (not end-to-end processing) with zero code changes. It's on by default in recent Kafka.

Q27. What are Kafka transactions and what do they enable? hard

Transactions let a producer write to multiple partitions/topics atomically — all messages commit or none do. Combined with reading input, processing, and writing output plus committing consumer offsets in the same transaction, they enable exactly-once read-process-write. You set a transactional.id, call initTransactions(), then beginTransaction() / send() … / commitTransaction(). Consumers must set isolation.level=read_committed to skip aborted messages.

Q28. Explain end-to-end exactly-once processing. hard

Exactly-once end-to-end requires: (1) an idempotent producer so writes aren't duplicated on retry; (2) transactions that atomically write output and commit the input offsets, so you can't process input twice or lose output; (3) downstream consumers reading with read_committed. Kafka Streams does all this for you when you set processing.guarantee=exactly_once_v2. If a sink is external (a DB), you also need idempotent writes there (upsert by key) because Kafka can't make an external system transactional.

Q29. Does exactly-once mean I never process a message twice? hard

It means the effect happens once, not that the code runs once. Under the hood there can still be retries and reprocessing, but transactions/idempotence ensure the committed output and offsets reflect each input exactly once — aborted attempts are invisible to read_committed consumers. For non-Kafka sinks, "effect once" requires idempotent operations (e.g. upsert) because Kafka's transaction can't roll back an external database.

Q30. How do you handle duplicates if you only have at-least-once? mid

Make your processing idempotent so duplicates are harmless. Common patterns: include a unique event ID and dedupe by storing processed IDs (with TTL); write to the sink via upsert/merge keyed on a natural key so reprocessing overwrites rather than double-counts; or make aggregations idempotent (set a value rather than increment). This "at-least-once + idempotent sink" is the pragmatic exactly-once used in most real pipelines.

Q31. What are replicas, leaders, and followers? mid

Each partition has a replication factor (e.g. 3), meaning it's stored on 3 brokers. One replica is the leader — all reads and writes for that partition go through it. The others are followers, which continuously copy from the leader. If the leader broker dies, a follower is promoted to leader. Replication is what makes Kafka fault-tolerant: lose a broker, no data lost, service continues.

Q32. What is the ISR (in-sync replica set)? hard

The ISR is the set of replicas (leader + followers) that are fully caught up with the leader within replica.lag.time.max.ms. Only ISR members are eligible to become leader on failover, so no committed data is lost. With acks=all, a write is acknowledged only after all ISR members have it. If a follower falls behind, it's removed from the ISR until it catches up. Shrinking ISR is an early warning of broker or network trouble.

Q33. What is min.insync.replicas and why does it matter? hard

It's the minimum number of in-sync replicas that must acknowledge a write when acks=all. With replication factor 3 and min.insync.replicas=2, a write succeeds as long as at least 2 replicas have it — you tolerate losing 1 broker with zero data loss. If ISR drops below this minimum, producers get NotEnoughReplicas errors and stop (fail fast) rather than risk losing data. This pairing (acks=all + min.insync.replicas=2 + RF=3) is the standard durable config.

Q34. What is unclean leader election? hard

If all in-sync replicas are down, Kafka can either wait (partition unavailable) or promote an out-of-sync replica to leader — an unclean leader election. The latter restores availability but loses the messages the out-of-sync replica never received (data loss). It's controlled by unclean.leader.election.enable, which defaults to false in modern Kafka. For fintech you keep it false: consistency over availability.

Q35. How does replication factor affect durability and cost? mid

Higher replication factor = more copies = more brokers can fail without data loss, but it multiplies storage and inter-broker network traffic (each write is copied N times). RF=3 is the common sweet spot: survives 1 broker loss with min.insync.replicas=2, and 2 broker losses without data loss (though it becomes unavailable). RF=1 has no fault tolerance and is only for dev/throwaway data.

Q36. What is log compaction and when do you use it? hard

Normal retention deletes old data by age/size. Log compaction (cleanup.policy=compact) instead keeps at least the latest value for each key, deleting older versions. It turns a topic into a durable, replayable "table" of current state keyed by ID — perfect for changelog/CDC topics, user profiles, or config where you only care about the current value per key. A tombstone (null value) marks a key for deletion. Kafka Streams uses compacted topics for its state stores.

Q37. JSON vs Avro for message serialization — trade-offs? mid

JSON is human-readable and easy to start with, but verbose (field names repeated in every message), untyped, and has no built-in schema enforcement. Avro is compact binary, carries a schema, and — via Schema Registry — enforces structure and enables safe evolution. At scale (billions of events), Avro's smaller size and schema safety win; for a quick demo or low volume, JSON is fine. Protobuf is a common alternative to Avro with similar benefits.

Q38. What is the Schema Registry and why do you need it? hard

The Schema Registry is a service that stores and versions message schemas (Avro/Protobuf/JSON-Schema). Producers register a schema and send only a small schema ID plus binary data; consumers fetch the schema by ID to deserialize. Crucially it enforces compatibility rules (backward/forward/full) so a producer can't push a breaking change that would crash consumers. It's what makes evolving event contracts across many teams safe.

Q39. Explain schema evolution and compatibility modes. hard

Schemas change over time; compatibility modes define what's allowed. Backward: new schema can read old data (safe to add optional fields / remove fields with defaults) — consumers upgrade first. Forward: old schema can read new data — producers upgrade first. Full: both. The registry rejects changes that violate the configured mode, e.g. adding a required field with no default under backward compatibility. This prevents "one team's deploy broke everyone's consumers" incidents.

Q40. What is Kafka Connect? mid

Kafka Connect is a framework and runtime for moving data between Kafka and external systems without writing code, using configuration-driven connectors. Source connectors pull data into Kafka (from a DB, S3, an API); sink connectors push Kafka data out (to a warehouse, Elasticsearch, S3). It runs as a scalable cluster of workers, handles offset tracking and retries, and supports transforms (SMTs). It's the standard way to integrate Kafka with the rest of the stack.

Q41. What is CDC and how does Debezium use Kafka Connect? hard

CDC (Change Data Capture) streams every row change (insert/update/delete) from a database in near real time. Debezium is a set of Kafka Connect source connectors that read a database's transaction log (Postgres WAL, MySQL binlog) and publish each change as a Kafka event — without querying the DB or adding triggers. This lets you mirror OLTP tables into the lake/warehouse continuously, keep a search index in sync, or drive event-driven microservices, all without loading the production database.

Q42. Kafka Streams vs ksqlDB — what's the difference? mid

Kafka Streams is a Java/Scala library you embed in your app to do stateful stream processing (map, filter, join, windowed aggregations) with exactly-once support — no separate cluster, it scales by running more app instances. ksqlDB layers a SQL-like language on top so you can define streams and tables and transformations with SQL queries, run as a server. Streams = full programmatic control in code; ksqlDB = quick, declarative SQL for people who don't want to write Java.

Q43. What is consumer lag and why monitor it? mid

Consumer lag = (latest offset produced) − (offset the consumer has committed), per partition. It's how many messages the consumer is behind. Rising lag means consumers can't keep up with producers — latency grows and, if it exceeds retention, you lose data. You monitor it with kafka-consumer-groups.sh --describe, Burrow, or Prometheus/Grafana, and alert when lag or its rate of change crosses a threshold. Lag is the single most important consumer health metric.

Q44. A consumer group's lag is spiking. How do you diagnose it? hard

Check whether it's a producer surge or a consumer slowdown. Steps: (1) confirm the spike with kafka-consumer-groups --describe — is lag rising on all partitions or a few? (2) A few hot partitions → skewed keys, rebalance the key or add partitions. (3) All partitions → consumers too slow: look at processing time per record, downstream (DB/API) latency, GC pauses, or a stuck consumer. (4) Scale out consumers up to partition count, increase throughput per consumer, or fix the downstream bottleneck. Also verify no repeated rebalances are stalling consumption.

Q45. You have "rebalancing storms" — the group constantly rebalances. Fixes? hard

Usually consumers are being evicted because processing exceeds max.poll.interval.ms, or heartbeats miss due to long GC/pauses. Fixes: reduce max.poll.records so each batch is processed within the interval, or raise max.poll.interval.ms; tune session.timeout.ms/heartbeat.interval.ms; enable cooperative/incremental rebalancing so only affected partitions move; use group.instance.id (static membership) so a quick restart doesn't trigger a full rebalance; and fix any flapping/slow consumers. Avoid heavy synchronous work between polls.

Q46. How do you handle a "poison message" that keeps crashing your consumer? hard

A poison message is one your consumer can't process (bad schema, corrupt data), so it throws, never commits, and reprocesses the same message forever — blocking the partition. Solution: wrap processing in try/catch, and after N retries route the bad record to a dead-letter topic (DLQ) with error metadata, then commit the offset and move on. Investigate the DLQ separately. Kafka Connect and Spring Kafka have built-in DLQ support. Never let one bad record halt the whole partition.

Q47. How do you deal with backpressure when consumers can't keep up? hard

Kafka naturally buffers on disk, so producers aren't blocked — the pressure shows up as growing consumer lag. Options: (1) scale consumers out (up to partition count) — so provision enough partitions; (2) speed up per-record processing (batch DB writes, async I/O, remove blocking calls); (3) throttle upstream if the source can be slowed; (4) shed load by writing to a slower "overflow" path. The key insight: Kafka decouples producer and consumer speeds, turning a real-time crunch into a lag you can catch up on, provided retention is long enough.

Q48. How do you choose the number of partitions for a topic? hard

Base it on target throughput and desired consumer parallelism. Estimate: partitions ≥ target throughput ÷ per-partition throughput (both produce and consume), and ≥ the max consumers you'll want in a group. Add headroom because increasing partitions later re-hashes keys (breaking per-key ordering for existing data) and you can't easily decrease. But don't over-partition: each partition adds file handles, memory, and rebalance/leader-election overhead, and slows failover. A common starting point is a few dozen; thousands per broker is a smell.

Q49. Kafka vs Kinesis vs Event Hubs vs Pub/Sub — how do they compare? hard

Kafka: open-source, most features/control, runs anywhere, but you (or a vendor like Confluent) operate it. AWS Kinesis: fully managed, shard-based (similar to partitions), tight AWS integration, 24h–365d retention, simpler but less flexible and AWS-locked. Azure Event Hubs: managed, offers a Kafka-compatible endpoint, good on Azure. GCP Pub/Sub: managed, auto-scaling, no partitions to size (great ops-simplicity) but weaker ordering guarantees and a different model. Rule of thumb: Kafka for control/portability/rich ecosystem; the cloud-native ones for less ops if you're all-in on one cloud.

Q50. Walk me through designing a real-time fraud pipeline on Kafka. hard

(1) Ingest: the payment service (and Debezium CDC from the accounts DB) publishes events to a transactions topic, keyed by account_id so per-account order is preserved, with acks=all, RF=3, min.insync.replicas=2, idempotent producer. (2) Enrich/score: a stream processor (Kafka Streams or Spark/Flink) joins the transaction stream against a compacted account_profile topic and windowed aggregates (spend in last 5 min) to score fraud in milliseconds, writing to a fraud_scores topic. (3) Act: high-score events go to a fraud_alerts topic consumed by a blocker service and notifications. (4) Analytics/audit: a sink connector lands all topics into the lake/warehouse for model retraining and regulatory replay. Guarantees: exactly-once within Kafka via transactions, idempotent upserts to external stores, DLQ for bad records, and lag alerting so fraud scoring never falls behind.

Apache Airflow — Workflow Orchestration

The tool that schedules, runs, retries, and monitors your data pipelines as code — the industry-standard orchestrator for ETL/ELT.

What & Why

A data pipeline is rarely one step. A daily job might: call an API, clean the data, load it into a warehouse, run a dbt transform, then refresh a dashboard — where each step depends on the previous one succeeding. You could glue these together with cron and shell scripts, but cron only answers "run this script at 6am". It has no idea whether step 2 depends on step 1, whether step 1 actually succeeded, how to retry a flaky API call, how to alert you on failure, how to backfill last month, or how to show you a history of runs. Orchestration is the layer above scheduling that manages these dependencies, retries, and visibility.

Apache Airflow is the most widely used orchestrator. You define pipelines as DAGs (Directed Acyclic Graphs) written in Python: nodes are tasks, edges are dependencies, and "acyclic" means no loops (a task can't depend on itself). Because pipelines are code, they're version-controlled, reviewable, testable, and dynamic. Airflow's scheduler figures out what should run and when, hands tasks to executors/workers, records everything in a metadata database, and gives you a web UI to see runs, logs, and retry failed tasks with a click.

Where it sits vs Azure Data Factory (ADF): both orchestrate data workflows, but ADF is a managed, low-code, GUI-driven service tied to Azure, strong at moving data with connectors. Airflow is code-first, cloud-agnostic, and infinitely extensible via Python and operators — it excels at complex dependencies and custom logic, and it typically orchestrates other tools (triggers a Spark job, runs dbt, kicks off an ADF pipeline) rather than doing the heavy data movement itself. Many teams use Airflow as the "conductor" and specialized tools as the "musicians".

In a data platform, Airflow is the control plane for batch: it decides the order and timing, handles failure and retries, and provides the audit trail. It does not stream (that's Kafka's job) and shouldn't move large data through itself — it should trigger the systems that do.

Learning Checklist

Projects & Practice

  • Install Airflow locally — use the official docker-compose.yaml from the Airflow docs, or the astro CLI (astro dev init then astro dev start) which scaffolds a project and runs webserver/scheduler/DB in Docker. Proves: you can stand up the full Airflow stack and reach the UI at localhost:8080.
  • Daily ETL DAG (API → transform → load MySQL) — build a DAG scheduled @daily with three tasks: extract (PythonOperator calls a public API, e.g. exchange rates or a Kaggle-style CSV endpoint), transform (clean with pandas), load (write to MySQL via MySqlHook). Add retries=3, retry_delay, and an on_failure_callback that sends email/Slack. Proves: operators, dependencies, hooks/connections, retries, and alerting — the bread-and-butter of a DE job.
  • Backfill exercise — set start_date two weeks in the past with catchup=True, run airflow dags backfill -s -14d, and watch Airflow create one run per day using each day's data interval. Then make the pipeline idempotent (delete-then-insert / upsert keyed by date) and re-run a range to prove no duplicates. Proves: understanding of logical dates, catchup/backfill, and idempotency — heavily tested in interviews.
  • Sensor + trigger pattern (stretch) — add a FileSensor/S3 sensor (or deferrable variant) that waits for an upstream file before the ETL runs, and have one DAG trigger another with TriggerDagRunOperator. Proves: waiting/eventing patterns and cross-DAG orchestration.

Interview Q&A (50)

Q1. What is Apache Airflow and what problem does it solve? basic

Airflow is a platform to programmatically author, schedule, and monitor workflows (data pipelines) defined as code. It solves the problem cron can't: managing dependencies between many steps, retrying failures, alerting, backfilling history, and giving visibility (UI, logs, run history) into complex pipelines. You write pipelines as Python DAGs; Airflow runs them in the right order, at the right time, and tracks every run.

Q2. What is a DAG? basic

DAG = Directed Acyclic Graph. In Airflow it represents one workflow: tasks are nodes, dependencies are directed edges, and "acyclic" means there are no loops (nothing can depend on itself directly or indirectly). The DAG defines what tasks exist and in what order they run — e.g. extract → transform → load. A DAG is defined once as code and can run many times (one DAG Run per schedule interval).

Q3. Why not just use cron? basic

Cron only triggers a command at a time; it has no notion of dependencies, no retries, no alerting, no history, no backfill, and no way to see if step 1 succeeded before running step 2. If a job fails at 3am, cron doesn't retry or tell you. Airflow adds dependency management, automatic retries with delay, failure/SLA alerting, a UI with logs and run history, backfilling, and pipelines as version-controlled code.

Q4. What is a task and a task instance? basic

A task is a single unit of work in a DAG, defined by an operator (e.g. "run this Python function"). A task instance is a specific run of that task for a specific DAG Run / data interval — it has a state (queued, running, success, failed, etc.). So one task in the code produces many task instances over time, one per scheduled run.

Q5. What is an operator? Name common ones. basic

An operator is a template that defines what a task does. Common ones: PythonOperator (run a Python callable), BashOperator (run a shell command), EmptyOperator (a placeholder/grouping node), sensors like FileSensor (wait for a condition), and provider operators like SnowflakeOperator, SparkSubmitOperator, S3ToRedshiftOperator. You pick or write the operator that matches the step.

Q6. How do you set task dependencies? basic

Use the bitshift operators: extract >> transform >> load means extract runs, then transform, then load. << is the reverse. You can also call task.set_downstream(other)/set_upstream(), or with the TaskFlow API dependencies are inferred from function calls. Lists express fan-out/fan-in, e.g. extract >> [t1, t2] >> load.

extract >> [clean, validate] >> load
Q7. What is schedule_interval (schedule) and what values can it take? basic

It defines how often the DAG runs. Values: a cron string ("0 6 * * *"), a preset (@daily, @hourly, @weekly, @once), a timedelta (timedelta(hours=6)), a dataset trigger (data-aware scheduling), or None for manual/triggered-only DAGs. In newer Airflow the parameter is called schedule.

Q8. What is start_date and why does it matter? mid

start_date is the logical date from which the DAG's schedule begins. The first run covers the interval starting at start_date. It should be a static datetime, not datetime.now() (which shifts every parse and breaks scheduling). Combined with catchup, a past start_date makes Airflow backfill all missed intervals up to now. Getting start_date wrong (dynamic or misaligned with the interval) is a classic scheduling bug.

Q9. "Why did my DAG run with yesterday's date?" Explain the logical/execution date. mid

Airflow runs a scheduled interval after the interval has completed. A @daily DAG for the data of July 4th runs at the end of July 4th — i.e. early July 5th. The run's logical date (historically execution_date) is the start of the data interval it covers (July 4th), not the wall-clock time it actually ran (July 5th). So the "date" you see is the period the run is responsible for, not when it fired. This trips up everyone once. Use data_interval_start/data_interval_end to reason about the window explicitly.

Q10. What is a data interval? mid

Each DAG Run is responsible for a data interval — a time window of data to process. For a @daily DAG, a run's interval is [midnight of day N, midnight of day N+1). The run fires at the end of that window. Templated fields expose data_interval_start and data_interval_end so you can filter your query to exactly that window (e.g. WHERE created_at >= '{{ data_interval_start }}' AND created_at < '{{ data_interval_end }}'). This is the modern, unambiguous replacement for reasoning with execution_date.

Q11. What is catchup? mid

catchup controls whether Airflow runs all the intervals between start_date and now when the DAG is first enabled (or after being paused). With catchup=True, a DAG with a start_date two weeks ago immediately creates 14 daily runs. With catchup=False, it only runs the most recent interval going forward. Teams usually set catchup=False to avoid a flood of unexpected historical runs, then backfill deliberately when needed.

Q12. What is backfill and how is it different from catchup? mid

Backfill is deliberately (re)running a DAG over a past date range — e.g. airflow dags backfill -s 2026-06-01 -e 2026-06-14 my_dag — to fill or re-process history. Catchup is the automatic version that happens on enable based on start_date. Both create one run per interval using that interval's logical date. Backfill is manual and range-controlled; catchup is automatic. Both require idempotent tasks to avoid duplicating data.

Q13. How do retries work in Airflow? basic

Set retries and retry_delay on a task (or in default_args). If a task fails, Airflow waits retry_delay and reruns it, up to retries times, before marking it failed. You can add retry_exponential_backoff=True and max_retry_delay for backing off flaky external calls. Retries handle transient failures (API timeout, brief network blip) automatically.

default_args = {"retries": 3, "retry_delay": timedelta(minutes=5)}
Q14. What is XCom and what is it for? mid

XCom ("cross-communication") lets tasks pass small values to each other via the metadata database. A task pushes a value (return value auto-pushes with the TaskFlow API, or xcom_push), and a downstream task pulls it (xcom_pull). Use it for small things: a filename, a row count, an ID. It's stored in the metadata DB, so it must not be used for large data (see Q49).

@task
def extract(): return {"path": "/tmp/data.csv"}
@task
def load(info): print(info["path"])
load(extract())
Q15. What are Connections and Hooks? mid

A Connection stores credentials/endpoints for an external system (a MySQL DB, S3, an API) in Airflow, referenced by a conn_id — so secrets aren't hard-coded in DAGs. A Hook is the reusable client that uses a connection to talk to that system (e.g. MySqlHook(mysql_conn_id="mydb").get_conn()). Operators use hooks under the hood. Together they give clean, secure, reusable access to external systems.

Q16. What are Variables in Airflow? basic

Variables are key-value config stored in Airflow's metadata DB (or a secrets backend), accessed via Variable.get("api_base_url") or the UI. Use them for environment-specific settings you don't want to hard-code (URLs, thresholds, feature flags). Avoid calling Variable.get() at the top level of a DAG file because it runs on every DAG parse and hammers the DB — fetch inside tasks or use Jinja templating.

Q17. What is the TaskFlow API? mid

The TaskFlow API (Airflow 2+) lets you write tasks as plain Python functions decorated with @task and DAGs with @dag. Return values automatically become XComs, and dependencies are inferred from how you call the functions — no explicit >> or manual xcom_push/pull. It makes DAGs read like normal Python and reduces boilerplate.

@dag(schedule="@daily", start_date=datetime(2026,1,1), catchup=False)
def etl():
    @task
    def extract(): return [1,2,3]
    @task
    def load(x): print(sum(x))
    load(extract())
etl()
Q18. What is a Sensor? mid

A sensor is a special operator that waits for a condition to be true before allowing downstream tasks — e.g. FileSensor waits for a file, ExternalTaskSensor waits for another DAG's task, SqlSensor waits for a query to return rows. It has two modes: poke (holds a worker slot and checks repeatedly) and reschedule (frees the slot between checks). Sensors let a pipeline start only when its inputs are ready.

Q19. What are deferrable operators / the triggerer? hard

Traditional sensors in poke mode occupy a worker slot the entire time they wait — wasteful for long waits. Deferrable operators (and deferrable sensors) instead suspend themselves and hand an async trigger to a lightweight triggerer process, freeing the worker slot entirely. When the condition fires, the task resumes on a worker. This lets you have thousands of pending waits without exhausting worker slots — essential at scale.

Q20. What is idempotency and why must Airflow tasks be idempotent? hard

Idempotent means running a task again with the same inputs produces the same result without side effects like duplicated rows. Airflow re-runs tasks all the time — retries, backfills, manual reruns — so a non-idempotent task (e.g. blind INSERT) will double-insert on rerun. Make tasks idempotent: delete-then-insert for the interval, MERGE/upsert keyed by a natural key, or write to a partition keyed by the logical date so a rerun overwrites cleanly.

Q21. What is an SLA in Airflow and how do you get notified? mid

An SLA (Service Level Agreement) is a deadline for a task/DAG. Set sla=timedelta(hours=2) on a task; if the task hasn't completed within that time after the DAG Run started, Airflow records an SLA miss and can call an sla_miss_callback or email. Note SLA misses don't fail the task — they just flag lateness. Use them to alert when pipelines are running slow even if not failing.

Q22. How do you send failure alerts (email/Slack)? mid

Set email_on_failure=True with email in default_args for basic email, or attach an on_failure_callback that posts to Slack via SlackWebhookHook/SlackWebhookOperator. The callback receives context (dag_id, task_id, execution date, log URL) so the message is actionable. Many teams centralize this in a shared callback used across all DAGs.

def alert(ctx):
    SlackWebhookHook(slack_webhook_conn_id="slack").send(
        text=f"FAILED {ctx['task_instance'].task_id}")
default_args = {"on_failure_callback": alert}
Q23. What are trigger rules? hard

A trigger rule decides when a task runs based on its upstream tasks' states. Default is all_success (run only if all parents succeeded). Others: all_failed, one_success, one_failed, none_failed, none_failed_min_one_success, all_done (run regardless of upstream outcome). You use them for cleanup/notification tasks (all_done) or branching logic where you want a task to run even if some upstreams were skipped or failed.

Q24. How do you build a dynamic DAG? hard

Because a DAG is Python, you can generate tasks in a loop from a config list — e.g. one task per table read from a YAML file. For runtime-dependent fan-out use dynamic task mapping (.expand()), which creates a variable number of task instances based on an upstream result. Caution: don't do expensive work (DB/API calls) at parse time to generate the DAG, since the file is parsed frequently.

@task
def process(x): return x*2
process.expand(x=[1,2,3,4])  # 4 mapped task instances
Q25. What is branching (BranchPythonOperator)? mid

Branching lets a DAG choose which downstream path to run at runtime. BranchPythonOperator (or @task.branch) returns the task_id(s) to follow; unchosen branches are marked skipped. Use it for conditional logic like "if the file has rows, load it; else send an empty-data alert". Remember skipped upstreams affect downstream trigger rules — often you'll need none_failed_min_one_success on a join task after a branch.

Q26. What are the main Airflow components/architecture? mid

Core pieces: the Scheduler (parses DAGs, decides what to run, schedules task instances), the Webserver (the UI to view/trigger DAGs and read logs), the Metadata Database (Postgres/MySQL storing DAG runs, task states, connections, variables, XComs), the Executor (how/where tasks run) with its Workers, and in newer versions a Triggerer (for deferrable tasks). DAG files live in a folder both scheduler and workers can read.

Q27. What is the metadata database and what does it store? mid

It's a relational DB (Postgres or MySQL in production; SQLite only for local trials) that is Airflow's source of truth: DAG runs and their states, task instance states and try numbers, scheduling info, XComs, Connections, Variables, users, and logs metadata. The scheduler and webserver both read/write it. If it's slow or overloaded, the whole system slows — so you keep XComs small, clean old runs, and give it enough resources.

Q28. What is an Executor? Compare Local, Celery, Kubernetes. hard

The executor determines how tasks are run. LocalExecutor: runs tasks as subprocesses on the single scheduler machine — simple, good for small/dev setups, limited by one box. CeleryExecutor: distributes tasks to a pool of persistent workers via a broker (Redis/RabbitMQ) — scales horizontally, workers always on. KubernetesExecutor: launches each task in its own Kubernetes pod — great isolation and elastic scaling (pods spin up/down per task), no idle workers, but per-task pod startup overhead. There's also a Celery+Kubernetes hybrid.

Q29. What is the SequentialExecutor and why avoid it in prod? basic

The SequentialExecutor runs one task at a time and is the default with a SQLite metadata DB. It's fine for quick local experimentation but can't run tasks in parallel, so it's unusable for real workloads. In production you use LocalExecutor (with Postgres) at minimum, or Celery/Kubernetes for scale.

Q30. How does the scheduler decide what to run? hard

The scheduler continuously parses the DAG files, and for each DAG checks whether a new data interval is due (based on schedule and last run). It creates DAG Runs and their task instances, then evaluates each task's dependencies (upstream states, trigger rules, pools, and concurrency limits). Task instances that are ready are placed in a queued state and handed to the executor to run. It also handles retries, catchup, and marking timeouts.

Q31. What controls parallelism in Airflow? hard

Several layered limits: parallelism (global max running task instances across the whole Airflow), max_active_tasks_per_dag (a.k.a. dag_concurrency — max concurrent tasks in one DAG), max_active_runs_per_dag (max simultaneous runs of a DAG), and Pools (named slot buckets to cap concurrent access to a shared resource like a DB). Worker/executor capacity also bounds it. If tasks sit in queued, one of these limits (or no free workers) is usually the reason.

Q32. My task is stuck in "queued" and never runs. Why? hard

Queued means the scheduler wants to run it but no execution slot is available. Common causes: hit parallelism, max_active_tasks_per_dag, or an exhausted Pool; no/too-few Celery workers running or workers unhealthy; the executor can't launch (Kubernetes quota/pod scheduling issues); or a stuck scheduler. Diagnose by checking the running-task counts vs limits, worker/pod health, and pool usage in the UI, then raise the relevant limit or add capacity.

Q33. My new DAG isn't showing up in the UI. What do you check? mid

Checklist: is the file in the configured dags_folder and named/importable correctly? Does it have an actual DAG object at module top level (not hidden in a function)? Are there import errors (check "DAG Import Errors" in the UI or scheduler logs)? Has the scheduler re-parsed yet (dag_dir_list_interval)? Is the filename/contents missing the words "dag"/"airflow" that the default parser filter looks for? Is the DAG paused? Fixing the import error or waiting for the parse usually resolves it.

Q34. What is a zombie task and how does Airflow handle it? hard

A zombie task is a task instance whose process has died (worker crashed, pod killed, OOM) but the metadata DB still shows it as "running" because it stopped sending heartbeats. The scheduler detects missing heartbeats beyond a threshold, marks the task as failed, and triggers retries per the task's config. To reduce zombies: give tasks enough memory, avoid killing workers, and set sensible execution_timeout.

Q35. What is execution_timeout and why use it? mid

execution_timeout caps how long a single task instance may run; if exceeded, Airflow kills it and marks it failed (then retries apply). It prevents a hung task (stuck API call, runaway query) from occupying a slot forever and blocking the pipeline. Always set it on tasks that call external systems. It's distinct from SLA, which just flags lateness without killing the task.

Q36. How do retries interact with idempotency and partial writes? hard

If a task writes half its data then fails and retries, a non-idempotent task can duplicate or corrupt the partial write. So design the task so a retry safely overwrites: write to a temp/staging location then atomically swap, or delete-then-insert / upsert for the interval so re-running produces the same final state. The rule: every task should be safe to run any number of times for the same logical date. Retries only help if the task is idempotent.

Q37. How do you prevent overlapping runs of the same DAG? mid

Set max_active_runs=1 on the DAG so only one run executes at a time (the next waits). For task-level mutual exclusion across DAGs, use a Pool with one slot. depends_on_past=True makes a task wait for its own previous run's success, enforcing sequential-by-interval execution. Choose based on whether the constraint is per-DAG-run or per-shared-resource.

Q38. What does depends_on_past do? mid

When depends_on_past=True, a task instance only runs if the same task in the previous DAG Run succeeded. It enforces chronological correctness for stateful pipelines (e.g. a running balance that must be built in order). The downside: one failed historical run blocks all later runs until fixed, and it complicates backfills. wait_for_downstream is a stricter variant.

Q39. How do you pass configuration/parameters into a DAG run? mid

Options: Params (declared on the DAG, editable when triggering manually), the conf passed to a manual/triggered run (accessed via {{ dag_run.conf }}), Variables for shared config, and Connections for credentials. For triggering one DAG from another with config, use TriggerDagRunOperator(conf=...). Templated fields let you inject these values with Jinja at runtime.

Q40. What is Jinja templating in Airflow? mid

Many operator fields are templated, meaning you can embed Jinja placeholders resolved at runtime, like {{ ds }} (logical date as YYYY-MM-DD), {{ data_interval_start }}, {{ params.x }}, or {{ ti.xcom_pull(...) }}. This lets a single DAG definition produce date-specific queries/paths per run — e.g. SELECT * FROM t WHERE dt = '{{ ds }}'. It's how you make tasks parameterized by their data interval.

Q41. What are Pools and when do you use them? hard

A Pool is a named bucket of concurrency slots that limits how many tasks (across all DAGs) can use a shared resource at once. Example: create a pool warehouse with 5 slots and assign it to all tasks that hit the warehouse, so you never run more than 5 concurrent heavy queries and overload it. Tasks needing a slot wait in queued until one frees. Pools are the main tool for protecting fragile downstream systems.

Q42. What is an SLA miss vs a task failure — and how do you alert on slowness? mid

A failure is a task that errored (and exhausted retries). An SLA miss is a task that succeeded (or is still running) but didn't finish within its sla deadline — it's about lateness, not correctness. To alert on slowness without failing runs, set SLAs plus an sla_miss_callback, and/or track task duration in monitoring (StatsD/Prometheus) with alerts on trend. Failures use on_failure_callback/email.

Q43. How do you test and validate a DAG? mid

Levels: (1) parse/import test — importing the DAG file raises no errors and has no cycles (a simple pytest that loads the DagBag). (2) unit tests on the Python callables in isolation. (3) airflow dags test my_dag 2026-07-04 or airflow tasks test my_dag my_task 2026-07-04 to run without scheduling/DB side effects. (4) Lint for anti-patterns (no top-level heavy code). CI typically runs the import test on every commit.

Q44. Why should you avoid heavy code at the top level of a DAG file? hard

The scheduler parses every DAG file repeatedly (every few seconds) just to build the DAG structure. Any top-level code — DB queries, API calls, big loops, Variable.get() — runs on every parse, which slows the scheduler, hammers external systems, and can cause flaky/slow parsing or DAG import timeouts. Keep top-level code cheap (just define structure); put real work inside task callables that only run at execution time.

Q45. How would you build a daily ETL DAG (API → transform → load MySQL) with retries and alerts? hard

Define a @daily DAG with catchup=False, default_args containing retries=3, retry_delay, and an on_failure_callback to Slack. Three tasks: extract calls the API filtered to {{ data_interval_start }} and stages the raw file; transform cleans it with pandas; load uses MySqlHook to upsert (delete-then-insert for that date) so it's idempotent. Chain extract >> transform >> load.

@dag(schedule="@daily", start_date=datetime(2026,1,1),
     catchup=False, default_args={"retries":3,
     "retry_delay":timedelta(minutes=5),
     "on_failure_callback":slack_alert})
def daily_etl():
    @task
    def extract(**c): ...      # call API for data_interval
    @task
    def transform(raw): ...    # pandas clean
    @task
    def load(df): ...          # MySqlHook upsert by date
    load(transform(extract()))
daily_etl()
Q46. What is dynamic task mapping and how is it different from a loop? hard

A Python loop that creates tasks builds a fixed number of tasks decided at parse time. Dynamic task mapping (.expand()) creates a variable number of task instances at runtime, based on the output of an upstream task — e.g. one task per file returned by a listing task. It's the right tool when the fan-out count isn't known until the DAG runs, and Airflow shows the mapped instances as a group in the UI.

Q47. Compare managed Airflow: MWAA, Cloud Composer, Astronomer. mid

MWAA (Amazon Managed Workflows for Apache Airflow): AWS-managed Airflow, tight AWS/IAM/S3 integration. Cloud Composer: Google Cloud's managed Airflow on GKE, integrated with GCP services. Astronomer (Astro): a cloud-agnostic commercial platform with the excellent astro CLI, strong tooling, and support — runs on any cloud. All remove the ops burden of running scheduler/DB/workers yourself; you pick based on which cloud you're on and how much tooling/support you want.

Q48. How do you run Airflow locally for development? basic

Two easy paths. (1) Official Docker Compose: download Airflow's docker-compose.yaml, run docker compose up airflow-init then docker compose up, and open localhost:8080. (2) Astronomer CLI: astro dev init scaffolds a project, astro dev start spins up webserver/scheduler/Postgres in containers. Put DAGs in the dags/ folder. Avoid a bare SQLite/SequentialExecutor setup except for the tiniest experiments.

Q49. Why is passing large data between tasks via XCom an anti-pattern? What's the fix? hard

XComs are stored in the metadata database, so pushing a big DataFrame or file through XCom bloats the DB, slows the scheduler/webserver, and can hit size limits. The correct pattern is to pass a reference, not the data: task A writes the data to shared storage (S3/GCS/a warehouse table/a file path) and pushes only the location/key via XCom; task B reads the data from that location. Use a custom XCom backend (e.g. S3) if you want this transparently. Keep XCom for small metadata only.

Q50. Design a robust production DAG orchestrating a fintech daily pipeline. hard

Requirements: reliability, idempotency, alerting, no overload. Design: schedule @daily, catchup=False, max_active_runs=1. Start with a deferrable sensor waiting for the upstream file/CDC snapshot (frees worker slots). Then extract filtered to {{ data_interval_start }}/data_interval_end; a validate task (branch to an alert path if data quality fails); transform; and an idempotent load (delete-then-insert / MERGE by date) that runs in a Pool capped to protect the warehouse. Set retries with exponential backoff, execution_timeout to avoid hung tasks, and sla with an sla_miss_callback. Attach an on_failure_callback to Slack, and a final all_done cleanup/notification task. Keep XComs tiny (pass file paths, not data), keep top-level code cheap, and add a DAG-import CI test. This gives an auditable, re-runnable, self-healing pipeline suitable for regulated fintech.

Microsoft Azure for Data Engineering

The cloud most Indian DE job posts ask for — learn its storage, orchestration (ADF), and analytics services and you cover 70% of Azure DE interviews.

What & Why

What is "the cloud", from zero? Instead of buying servers and running them in your own building, you rent computers, storage, and software from a provider (Microsoft Azure, Amazon AWS, Google GCP) over the internet, and pay per hour/per GB. You never touch physical hardware. A region is a physical cluster of Microsoft data centres in one geography (e.g. Central India in Pune, South India in Chennai). You pick a region close to your users/data to reduce latency and to satisfy data-residency laws (Indian fintech data often must stay in India). An Availability Zone is an isolated data centre inside a region; spreading across zones survives one building failing.

How Azure organises your stuff. Top to bottom: a Tenant (your whole org's identity boundary in Microsoft Entra ID) → Subscription (a billing + quota boundary; all cost rolls up here) → Resource Group (a folder that groups related resources so you can deploy, tag, and delete them together — e.g. one RG per project/environment like rg-fintech-dev) → individual Resources (a storage account, a data factory, a SQL database). Delete the resource group and everything inside it is gone — great for tearing down a demo.

IAM basics (who can do what). Identity lives in Microsoft Entra ID (formerly Azure Active Directory). You grant permissions with RBAC (Role-Based Access Control): you assign a role (a bundle of permissions like "Storage Blob Data Reader") to a principal (a user, a group, or a service's managed identity) at a scope (subscription / resource group / single resource). Least privilege = grant the narrowest role at the narrowest scope. A managed identity is an automatic identity Azure gives to a service (e.g. a Data Factory) so it can log in to other services (like storage) without you storing any password.

Why Azure first for you. In the Indian market a large share of DE roles (especially in banks, fintech, and Microsoft-partner service companies) are Azure-centric: Azure Data Factory for orchestration, ADLS Gen2 for the data lake, Azure Databricks for Spark, and Synapse / Microsoft Fabric for warehousing. The DP-203 (being folded into Fabric's DP-700) certification maps almost 1:1 to these interview topics. Master this section and you can speak the language of most Azure DE JDs.

Learning Checklist

Projects & Practice

  • Set up a free Azure account — sign up at azure.microsoft.com/free (₹ credit for 30 days + always-free tiers). Create a resource group rg-de-lab, a storage account with hierarchical namespace ON (that makes it ADLS Gen2), and containers raw, curated. Proves: subscriptions, RGs, ADLS Gen2 basics.
  • End-to-end ADF pipeline — build a pipeline: HTTP/CSV source (e.g. a public COVID or NYC-Taxi CSV) → Copy activity into raw in ADLS → call an Azure Databricks notebook that cleans/aggregates → write Parquet into curated. Use a linked service + managed identity for storage auth. Proves: linked services, datasets, copy activity, Databricks integration, medallion zones.
  • Watermark-based incremental pipeline — source a table (Azure SQL DB seeded from a Kaggle dataset like "Online Retail"). Store last-loaded ModifiedDate in a small control table; each run Lookup the watermark → Copy only rows greater than it → update the watermark. Proves: parameterization, Lookup, dynamic queries, incremental patterns.
  • Trigger + alert setup — attach a tumbling window trigger (hourly) to the incremental pipeline, then create an Azure Monitor alert that emails you if the pipeline fails. Proves: trigger types, monitoring, alerting.

Interview Q&A (50)

Q1. What is cloud computing and why do companies use it? basic

Cloud computing means renting compute, storage, and services on demand from a provider over the internet instead of owning hardware. Companies use it for elasticity (scale up/down in minutes), no upfront capital cost (pay-as-you-go OpEx), global reach (deploy in many regions), and managed services (the provider patches and runs the infrastructure, so engineers focus on data logic, not servers).

Q2. Explain IaaS, PaaS, and SaaS with an Azure example each. basic

IaaS (Infrastructure as a Service): you rent raw infrastructure and manage the OS and app — e.g. an Azure Virtual Machine. PaaS (Platform as a Service): the provider manages OS/runtime, you just deploy config or code — e.g. Azure SQL Database or Azure Data Factory. SaaS (Software as a Service): fully finished software you just log in to — e.g. Microsoft 365 / Power BI. As a DE you mostly live in PaaS services.

Q3. What is an Azure region and an Availability Zone? basic

A region is a set of data centres in one geographic area (e.g. Central India, Pune). An Availability Zone is one or more physically separate data centres within that region with independent power/network. Placing replicas across zones protects you from a single data centre outage. You choose a region for latency, cost, feature availability, and data-residency compliance.

Q4. Explain the Tenant → Subscription → Resource Group → Resource hierarchy. basic

A Tenant is your organisation's identity boundary in Microsoft Entra ID. A Subscription is a billing and quota container — all resource costs roll up to it. A Resource Group is a logical folder that groups related resources so they share lifecycle, tags, and permissions. A Resource is an individual service instance (storage account, data factory). You typically use one RG per project+environment (e.g. rg-fintech-prod).

Q5. What is RBAC and what are its three parts? basic

RBAC (Role-Based Access Control) governs "who can do what, where." Three parts: a security principal (user, group, service principal, or managed identity), a role definition (a set of allowed actions, e.g. "Storage Blob Data Contributor"), and a scope (subscription / resource group / single resource). A role assignment binds those three. Follow least privilege: smallest role at the narrowest scope.

Q6. What is a managed identity and why is it useful for a DE? mid

A managed identity is an Entra ID identity automatically created and managed for an Azure service (like a Data Factory or Databricks). It lets that service authenticate to other services (e.g. ADLS, Key Vault) without you storing any secret or password. You just grant the managed identity an RBAC role on the target. This removes credential leakage risk and is the recommended auth pattern in ADF linked services.

Q7. What is Microsoft Entra ID? basic

Microsoft Entra ID (formerly Azure Active Directory) is Azure's cloud identity and access management service. It stores users, groups, and service principals, handles authentication (sign-in, MFA, conditional access), and is the identity source that RBAC assigns roles to. Almost every Azure service authenticates through Entra ID.

Q8. What is an Azure Storage Account? basic

A storage account is the top-level container for Azure Storage services — Blobs, Files, Queues, Tables, and (if hierarchical namespace is enabled) ADLS Gen2. It defines the redundancy (LRS/ZRS/GRS), performance tier (standard/premium), and a unique endpoint URL. Inside it you create containers, which hold your data.

Q9. What is the difference between Blob storage and ADLS Gen2? mid

ADLS Gen2 is a Blob storage account with the hierarchical namespace (HNS) feature turned on. Plain Blob has a flat namespace where "folders" are just name prefixes; renaming/deleting a "folder" means touching every object. HNS gives real directories, so operations like rename/delete a folder are single atomic metadata operations — much faster for big-data workloads — plus POSIX-style ACLs. For a data lake you always want ADLS Gen2 (HNS on).

Q10. What are Blob access tiers and when do you use each? mid

Hot: frequently accessed data, highest storage price but lowest access price. Cool: infrequently accessed, kept ≥30 days (cheaper storage, higher read cost). Cold: even rarer, ≥90 days. Archive: offline, cheapest storage but data must be "rehydrated" (hours) before reading, kept ≥180 days. Use lifecycle management rules to auto-move old data (e.g. raw files older than 90 days) from Hot → Cool → Archive to cut cost.

Q11. What is Azure Data Factory and what problem does it solve? basic

Azure Data Factory (ADF) is Azure's cloud orchestration and data-integration service — it moves data between 90+ sources/sinks and coordinates transformations on a schedule. It solves the "glue" problem: instead of hand-writing and cron-ing scripts, you visually build pipelines that copy, transform, and monitor data, with retries, dependencies, and alerting built in. Think of it as managed, cloud-native ETL/ELT orchestration.

Q12. Define linked service, dataset, pipeline, and activity in ADF. basic

Linked service: a connection string/credential to a data store or compute (e.g. "my ADLS account", "my Databricks workspace"). Dataset: a named view of the data structure/location within a linked service (e.g. "the customers.csv in the raw container"). Activity: a single unit of work (Copy, Lookup, ForEach, Notebook). Pipeline: an ordered group of activities you run and monitor together. So: pipeline contains activities; activities use datasets; datasets use linked services.

Q13. What are the Integration Runtime (IR) types in ADF? mid

The IR is the compute engine that actually runs the movement/transform. Three types: Azure IR — fully managed, for cloud-to-cloud data movement and Data Flow execution; Self-hosted IR (SHIR) — an agent you install on an on-prem or VNet VM to reach private/on-prem sources; Azure-SSIS IR — a managed cluster to lift-and-shift existing SSIS packages. You pick the IR per linked service/activity.

Q14. What is a Self-hosted IR and when must you use one? mid

A Self-hosted IR is a small agent you install on a machine that can reach a private network. Use it when the source/sink is not publicly reachable: an on-prem SQL Server, an on-prem file share, a database behind a firewall/private endpoint, or anything in a locked-down VNet. The SHIR makes the outbound connection to ADF, so you don't open inbound firewall ports. You can install it on multiple nodes for high availability.

Q15. Compare Copy activity and Mapping Data Flows. mid

Copy activity is optimised bulk data movement (source → sink) with light schema mapping; it's cheap and fast and runs on the IR directly. Mapping Data Flows are visual, code-free transformations (joins, aggregates, derived columns, SCDs) that ADF compiles to and runs on a managed Spark cluster behind the scenes. Use Copy to land raw data; use Data Flows (or Databricks) when you need real transformation logic.

Q16. How do parameters, variables, and expressions work in ADF? mid

Parameters are inputs passed into a pipeline/dataset at start time (read-only during the run). Variables are mutable values you Set/Append during the run. Expressions use the @{...} syntax and functions to compute values dynamically, e.g. @{formatDateTime(utcnow(),'yyyy/MM/dd')} to build a date folder path. Together they let one pipeline handle many tables/dates instead of copy-pasting.

Q17. How do you build a dynamic/metadata-driven pipeline in ADF? hard

Store a control/config table listing the objects to load (table name, source, target path, watermark column). A Lookup activity reads that table; a ForEach activity iterates the rows; inside the loop a parameterized Copy activity uses expressions like @item().TableName to build the source query and sink path. Result: one pipeline loads 100 tables driven by data, not by 100 hard-coded copies.

Q18. Explain the watermark pattern for incremental loads. hard

A watermark is the "high-water mark" of the last successful load — usually the max ModifiedDate or a monotonically increasing ID. Steps: (1) Lookup the stored watermark from a control table; (2) Lookup the current max value in the source; (3) Copy only rows where ModifiedDate > @oldWatermark AND <= @newWatermark; (4) on success, update the control table to the new watermark. This loads only changed rows each run instead of the full table.

SELECT * FROM sales
WHERE ModifiedDate > '@{activity('LookupOldWM').output.firstRow.wm}'
  AND ModifiedDate <= '@{activity('LookupNewWM').output.firstRow.maxwm}'
Q19. What trigger types does ADF support? mid
