Four kinds: Schedule (run on a wall-clock schedule/recurrence), Tumbling window (fixed, contiguous, non-overlapping time windows with state, dependency, and backfill support), Event-based (fires on a Storage blob created/deleted event or a custom event via Event Grid), and Manual/on-demand (triggered by REST/SDK/UI). One pipeline can have multiple triggers.

Q20. Schedule trigger vs Tumbling window trigger — key differences? hard

A schedule trigger just fires at times; it's stateless, has no window concept, can't backfill the past, and one trigger can start many pipelines. A tumbling window trigger owns discrete time slices, is stateful (each window is retried independently), supports backfilling historical windows, passes WindowStart/WindowEnd system variables to the pipeline, and supports dependencies (window N waits for window N-1 or an upstream trigger). Use tumbling windows for time-partitioned incremental loads.

Q21. How do you pass WindowStart/WindowEnd into a pipeline? mid

Define pipeline parameters (e.g. windowStart, windowEnd) and, in the tumbling-window trigger, map them to the system variables @trigger().outputs.windowStartTime and @trigger().outputs.windowEndTime. Inside the pipeline you use those parameters in your source query filter, so each window loads exactly its own time slice — enabling clean, retryable, backfillable incremental loads.

Q22. How do you configure error handling and retries in ADF? mid

Each activity has a retry count and retry interval and a timeout in its policy — good for transient failures. Activities have output paths (success, failure, completion, skip); wire a failure path to a logging/notification activity. Use If Condition / Until for logic, and a pipeline-level failure to trigger an alert. For "run cleanup regardless," use the completion (upon completion) dependency.

Q23. How do you call an Azure Databricks notebook from ADF and pass parameters? mid

Create a Databricks linked service (using a managed identity or PAT and a job/interactive cluster). Add a Notebook activity pointing to the notebook path. Under "Base parameters" pass key/values (e.g. run_date = @{pipeline().parameters.windowStart}). In the notebook read them with dbutils.widgets.get("run_date"). Return values back to ADF with dbutils.notebook.exit(json), read via the activity output.

Q24. How does Databricks access ADLS Gen2 securely? mid

Preferred: use a managed identity / service principal with the "Storage Blob Data Contributor" role and Unity Catalog external locations, or OAuth passthrough — no keys in code. Older patterns use a service principal's client secret stored in Key Vault, referenced via a Databricks secret scope, then spark.conf settings or a mount point. Never hard-code storage account keys in notebooks.

Q25. Describe a good ADLS Gen2 data-lake zone layout. mid

Use the medallion layout: raw/bronze (exact source copy, immutable, original format), silver/cleaned (deduped, typed, conformed, usually Parquet/Delta), gold/curated (business aggregates ready for BI). Separate containers per zone, and folder-partition by ingest date, e.g. raw/sales/ingest_date=2026/07/05/. This makes reprocessing, security, and cost tiering per zone straightforward.

Q26. Why partition data by date folders in the lake? mid

Partition folders (e.g. year=2026/month=07/day=05/) let query engines and Spark prune — read only the folders matching the filter instead of scanning everything. This slashes scan cost and time, enables easy incremental overwrite of just one day, and simplifies retention (delete old date folders). Choose a partition key with moderate cardinality that matches common filters; avoid over-partitioning into millions of tiny folders.

Q27. How do you control access to data in ADLS Gen2? hard

Three layers: (1) RBAC roles at account/container level (e.g. Storage Blob Data Reader) — coarse-grained; (2) POSIX ACLs on directories/files (read/write/execute per principal) — fine-grained within a container; (3) SAS tokens (time-limited signed URLs) or account keys for delegated/temporary access. Best practice: prefer Entra ID RBAC + managed identities; use ACLs for folder-level sharing; avoid long-lived account keys.

Q28. What is a SAS token and when would you use it? mid

A Shared Access Signature is a signed URL granting scoped, time-boxed access to storage (specific container/blob, specific permissions, expiry) without sharing the account key. Use it to hand a partner/tool temporary upload/download access, or for a short-lived download link. Prefer user-delegation SAS (signed by Entra ID) over account-key SAS. Keep expiry short.

Q29. What is Azure Synapse Analytics? mid

Synapse is Azure's unified analytics platform combining a SQL data warehouse, Spark pools, data-integration pipelines (an embedded ADF), and a studio UI. You can query data warehouse tables, run Spark, and query lake files from one workspace. Note: for greenfield projects Microsoft now steers people to Microsoft Fabric, but Synapse is still heavily present in existing enterprise stacks and interviews.

Q30. Dedicated SQL pool vs serverless SQL pool in Synapse? hard

Dedicated SQL pool (formerly SQL DW) is a provisioned MPP warehouse: you buy DWUs, data is stored in distributed tables, and you pay while it's on — best for heavy, repeated BI workloads. Serverless SQL pool has no provisioned storage/compute; you write T-SQL over files in the lake (OPENROWSET) and pay per TB scanned — best for ad-hoc exploration and lightweight ELT. Dedicated = performance + fixed cost; serverless = flexibility + pay-per-query.

Q31. What is a distribution in a dedicated SQL pool and why does it matter? hard

A dedicated SQL pool spreads a table across 60 distributions (nodes). The distribution method controls how rows are placed: Hash (by a chosen column — best for large fact tables, keeps join keys colocated), Round-robin (even random spread — good for staging), Replicated (full copy on every node — good for small dimensions). Picking a good hash key avoids data skew and expensive data-movement (shuffles) during joins.

Q32. What is Azure SQL Database and when do you use it in a data pipeline? basic

Azure SQL Database is a fully managed (PaaS) relational OLTP database engine based on SQL Server. In a data platform it's often the source system (application database you extract from), a control/metadata store (watermark and config tables for ADF), or a serving layer for small operational reporting. It handles patching, backups, and HA for you.

Q33. What are Azure Event Hubs and why "Kafka-compatible"? mid

Event Hubs is Azure's managed, high-throughput event/stream ingestion service — it takes millions of events/sec, retains them in partitions, and lets consumers read at their own pace. It exposes a Kafka endpoint, so existing Kafka producers/consumers can point at Event Hubs by changing only connection config — no code rewrite. Use it as the front door for streaming data before Stream Analytics/Databricks processes it.

Q34. What is Azure Key Vault and how does ADF use it? mid

Key Vault is a managed secrets/keys/certificates store. Instead of putting a database password or connection string in an ADF linked service, you store it in Key Vault and reference it. ADF authenticates to Key Vault with its managed identity (granted a Key Vault access policy / "Key Vault Secrets User" role) and pulls the secret at runtime — so no credentials sit in your pipeline JSON or source control.

Q35. How do you monitor ADF pipelines and set up alerts? mid

Use the ADF Monitor tab to see pipeline/trigger/activity runs, durations, inputs/outputs, and error messages, and to re-run failed runs. Pipe diagnostics into Azure Monitor / Log Analytics for querying with KQL and long retention. Create metric or log alerts (e.g. "pipeline failed runs > 0") wired to an Action Group that emails/SMS/Teams-notifies you.

Q36. How do you control and reduce cost in Azure for a data platform? mid

Use Cost Management + Budgets with alerts; tag resources by project/env to attribute spend. Levers: right-size and auto-pause Databricks/Synapse dedicated pools when idle, use serverless/pay-per-query where workloads are spiky, tier cold data to Cool/Archive with lifecycle rules, store analytics data as compressed Parquet/Delta to cut both storage and scan cost, and prefer Copy activity over Data Flows for pure movement (Data Flows spin Spark clusters that cost more).

Q37. Walk through the free Azure account setup for learning. basic

Go to azure.microsoft.com/free, sign in with a Microsoft account, verify with a card (no charge; a small temporary auth may appear). You get a credit valid ~30 days plus a set of always-free services. Create a resource group, set a budget/alert immediately to avoid surprise charges, and delete resource groups after each lab to stop spend.

Q38. What does the DP-203 / Fabric DP-700 certification cover? basic

DP-203 ("Azure Data Engineer Associate") covered designing/implementing storage (ADLS), data processing (ADF, Databricks, Synapse Spark), security, and monitoring. Microsoft is retiring DP-203 in favour of DP-700 (Fabric Data Engineer), focused on Microsoft Fabric (lakehouse, pipelines, notebooks, real-time). Both map closely to the topics in this section, so studying either reinforces core Azure DE skills.

Q39. What is Microsoft Fabric and how does it relate to Synapse/ADF? mid

Fabric is Microsoft's newer unified SaaS analytics platform built on OneLake (a single Delta-based lake) with workloads for Data Factory pipelines, Spark notebooks (lakehouse), a warehouse, real-time analytics, and Power BI — all with one capacity billing model. It's the strategic successor to Synapse+standalone ADF. Concepts (pipelines, lakehouse zones, notebooks) carry over directly, so your ADF/Synapse knowledge transfers.

Q40. What is Delta Lake and why is it used on ADLS? mid

Delta Lake is an open storage format (Parquet + a transaction log) that adds ACID transactions, schema enforcement/evolution, time travel, and efficient upserts (MERGE) on top of files in the lake. On ADLS/Databricks it turns a plain data lake into a reliable "lakehouse," enabling incremental updates, concurrent writes, and rollback — things plain Parquet can't do safely.

Q41. Scenario: an ADF pipeline is running much slower than expected. How do you diagnose it? hard

Open the Monitor tab and look at per-activity durations to find the bottleneck. For a slow Copy, check the "Data Integration Units (DIU)" and "parallel copies" settings, source read speed, and the "Details" throughput chart; increase DIUs/parallelism, partition the source, and stage via ADLS if crossing regions. For a slow Data Flow, size the Spark IR bigger, avoid unnecessary shuffles/sorts, and enable partitioning. Also check for a SHIR that's undersized, network egress across regions, or a source database that's the real limiter.

Q42. Scenario: a Copy activity fails intermittently. What causes this and how do you make it resilient? hard

Intermittent failures usually mean transient issues: throttling by the source/sink, brief network drops, token expiry, or a busy SHIR node. Fixes: set activity retry count (e.g. 3) with a sensible interval; enable fault tolerance to skip incompatible/duplicate rows and log them; enable staged copy to add durability; scale the SHIR to multiple nodes for HA; and back off source concurrency. Capture failures on the failure path to alert and re-run just the failed window.

Q43. Scenario: when should you introduce a Self-hosted IR? mid

Whenever the data isn't reachable from Azure's public managed network: on-prem SQL Server/Oracle/file shares, databases locked behind a corporate firewall or accessible only via private endpoints/VNet, or when compliance forbids opening inbound ports. The SHIR sits inside that network and makes only outbound calls to ADF. If everything is public PaaS (ADLS ↔ Azure SQL), a plain Azure IR is enough.

Q44. Scenario: your Azure bill suddenly spiked. How do you investigate and prevent it? hard

Open Cost Management → Cost analysis, group by resource/resource-group/tag to find the culprit. Common causes: a Databricks/Synapse dedicated pool left running, a Data Flow trigger firing far more often than expected, huge cross-region egress, hot-tier storage that should be archived, or a runaway ForEach spinning many Spark clusters. Prevent with budgets+alerts, auto-pause/auto-terminate on clusters, lifecycle tiering, and reviewing trigger frequencies.

Q45. How do you deploy ADF pipelines across dev/test/prod (CI/CD)? hard

Connect the dev ADF to Git (Azure Repos/GitHub); work in feature branches, then publish from collaboration branch which generates ARM templates in the adf_publish branch. A pipeline (Azure DevOps/GitHub Actions) deploys those ARM templates to test/prod, overriding environment-specific values (linked service endpoints, Key Vault names) via ARM template parameters. This keeps environments consistent and changes traceable.

Q46. What is a Lookup activity vs a Get Metadata activity? mid

Lookup reads a row or rowset from a dataset/query and returns it as activity output (used to fetch a watermark, a config list, a count). Get Metadata returns metadata about a dataset — existence, size, last modified, child items in a folder, column schema. Use Get Metadata to check "did today's file arrive?" and Lookup to read control-table values.

Q47. How would you implement Slowly Changing Dimension Type 2 in Azure? hard

Land the source, then use a Databricks/Data Flow MERGE into a Delta dimension: match on the business key; when the incoming attributes differ from the current active row, expire the current row (set end_date, is_current=false) and insert a new row with a fresh surrogate key, new start_date, and is_current=true. Delta's MERGE makes this atomic; ADF orchestrates the notebook/data flow that runs it.

Q48. What is the difference between ETL and ELT, and which does Azure favour? mid

ETL transforms data before loading into the target (transform on a separate engine). ELT loads raw data first, then transforms inside the powerful target (lake/warehouse). Cloud platforms favour ELT because storage is cheap and compute (Databricks/Synapse/Spark) is elastic — land raw in ADLS, then transform in-place. ADF copies (EL) and Databricks/Data Flows/SQL pools transform (T).

Q49. How do you secure network access to Azure storage and databases? hard

Beyond identity, restrict the network surface: disable public access and use Private Endpoints (a private IP in your VNet) plus Private DNS, or at least storage firewall rules limiting to specific VNets/IP ranges and "trusted Azure services." For ADF reaching private resources, use a Managed VNet with managed private endpoints or a SHIR. Combine with RBAC + managed identities so both "network path" and "identity" are locked down.

Q50. Design a batch data platform on Azure end-to-end. hard

Sources (on-prem/app DBs, APIs, files) → ADF orchestrates ingestion (Copy, via SHIR for on-prem) into ADLS Gen2 bronze, partitioned by date; secrets in Key Vault, auth via managed identities. Azure Databricks/Delta cleans+conforms to silver and builds business aggregates in gold, using MERGE for upserts/SCDs and watermarks for incrementals. Serve gold via Synapse serverless / dedicated pool or Fabric warehouse to Power BI. Schedule with tumbling-window triggers, monitor with Azure Monitor alerts, govern with RBAC/Purview, and manage spend with budgets and auto-pause.

Amazon Web Services (AWS) for Data Engineering

The world's most-demanded cloud — its DE services map almost 1:1 to Azure, so learn the concept once and just relabel the service names.

What & Why

Why learn AWS too? AWS is the largest cloud provider globally and appears in the most DE job postings worldwide; many Indian product companies and startups run on AWS. The good news: the concepts you learned for Azure transfer directly. Object storage, an orchestrator, a Spark ETL engine, a query engine over files, a data warehouse, and a streaming ingest service all exist in both clouds — only the names change. So AWS is mostly a "map the names" exercise on top of the fundamentals you already have.

The core AWS DE stack. S3 is the object store / data-lake foundation (equivalent to ADLS). IAM is identity and access (equivalent to Entra ID + RBAC). AWS Glue provides a Data Catalog (metadata) plus serverless Spark ETL jobs and crawlers. Athena is serverless SQL (Trino/Presto) over files in S3. Redshift is the MPP data warehouse. EMR is managed Hadoop/Spark clusters. Kinesis (or MSK) handles streaming. Orchestration is done with Step Functions or MWAA (managed Airflow).

How AWS structures access. Everything is governed by IAM: users (people/long-lived), roles (assumable identities with temporary credentials — the preferred way services authenticate), and policies (JSON documents granting/denying specific actions on specific resources). A Glue job "assumes a role" that has an S3 policy, so it never needs a stored password — the same passwordless idea as Azure managed identities. Learn to read a policy's Effect / Action / Resource and you understand AWS security.

Learning approach. Set up a free-tier account, build one S3 → Glue crawler → Athena pipeline, and keep the Azure↔AWS mapping table (below) handy. In interviews you'll often be asked "how does this Azure thing map to AWS?" — knowing the pairs makes you look fluent in both.

Learning Checklist

Projects & Practice

  • Free-tier account + S3 → Glue → Athena pipeline — sign up at aws.amazon.com/free. Upload a Kaggle CSV (e.g. "NYC Taxi Trips" or "Online Retail") to an S3 raw/ prefix, run a Glue crawler to populate the Data Catalog, then query it in Athena. Proves: S3, IAM role for Glue, crawlers, catalog, serverless SQL.
  • Partitioned Parquet + Athena cost exercise — convert the raw CSV to partitioned Parquet (via Glue ETL or Athena CTAS), partitioned by e.g. pickup date. Run the same aggregation query against CSV vs partitioned Parquet and compare the "data scanned" (₹) in the Athena query stats. Proves: partitioning, columnar formats, Athena cost model.
  • S3-event-triggered Lambda ETL — write a small Lambda (Python) that fires when a file lands in S3, validates/cleans it, and writes the result to a curated/ prefix. Proves: event-driven ETL, Lambda, IAM roles, S3 event notifications.

Interview Q&A (50)

Q1. What are IAM users, roles, and policies? basic

An IAM user is a long-lived identity for a person or app with credentials. A role is an identity that has no permanent credentials; principals (users, services, other accounts) assume it to get temporary credentials — the preferred pattern for services. A policy is a JSON document listing permissions (Effect: Allow/Deny, Action, Resource) attached to users/groups/roles. You grant least privilege by attaching narrow policies.

Q2. Why prefer IAM roles over access keys for services? mid

Roles issue temporary, auto-rotating credentials (via STS) instead of long-lived access keys that can leak and never expire. A Glue job, Lambda, or EC2 instance assumes a role and gets short-lived creds transparently — nothing to store in code or config. This is the AWS analogue of Azure managed identities and is a top security best practice; long-lived keys should be avoided.

Q3. What is Amazon S3 and what is a bucket vs a prefix? basic

S3 (Simple Storage Service) is AWS's object storage — you store files ("objects") addressed by a key. A bucket is the top-level container (its name is globally unique and tied to a region). S3 has a flat namespace: the "folders" you see are just prefixes — parts of the object key before a "/". So raw/sales/2026/file.csv is one key; raw/sales/ is a prefix used for organisation and filtering.

Q4. Explain S3 storage classes and when to use each. mid

Standard: frequent access, default. Standard-IA / One Zone-IA: infrequent access, cheaper storage + retrieval fee (One Zone stores in a single AZ, less durable/cheaper). Intelligent-Tiering: auto-moves objects between tiers by access pattern. Glacier Instant/Flexible/Deep Archive: archival, cheapest storage, retrieval from ms to hours. Use lifecycle rules to transition data (e.g. raw > 90 days → Glacier).

Q5. What is S3 versioning and why enable it? basic

Versioning keeps every version of an object when you overwrite or delete it, so you can recover from accidental deletes/overwrites (a delete just adds a "delete marker"). It's valuable for protecting raw/landing data and satisfying audit needs. Trade-off: storage cost grows with versions, so combine it with lifecycle rules to expire old noncurrent versions.

Q6. How does S3 encryption work? mid

S3 encrypts data at rest by default. Options: SSE-S3 (AWS-managed keys, simplest), SSE-KMS (keys in AWS KMS — gives audit logs and per-key access control, common in fintech for compliance), and SSE-C (customer-provided keys). In transit, use HTTPS/TLS. For sensitive data prefer SSE-KMS so you control and audit key usage.

Q7. How do you design an S3 data-lake layout? mid

Use zone prefixes mirroring the medallion pattern: raw/ (immutable source copy), stage/ or cleaned/ (typed, deduped, Parquet), curated/ (business-ready aggregates). Partition by columns that match query filters, e.g. curated/sales/dt=2026-07-05/. Store in columnar Parquet/ORC, compress (Snappy), and avoid millions of tiny files. Separate buckets/prefixes also make IAM/Lake Formation permissions cleaner per zone.

Q8. What is an S3 bucket policy vs an IAM policy? mid

An IAM policy is attached to an identity (user/role) and says what that identity can do across resources. A bucket policy is a resource-based policy attached to the bucket itself, saying who (which principals/accounts) can access this bucket. Access is granted if either allows and nothing explicitly denies. Bucket policies are how you grant cross-account access or enforce rules like "require TLS/encryption."

Q9. What is a VPC and why does a data engineer care? mid

A VPC (Virtual Private Cloud) is your isolated private network in AWS with subnets, route tables, and security groups. DEs care because Redshift, EMR, RDS, and MWAA usually run inside a VPC; you place them in private subnets, use VPC endpoints so traffic to S3/Glue stays on the AWS network (cheaper/safer), and use security groups to control who can reach a database. It's the network-security layer of your pipeline.

Q10. What is AWS Lake Formation? mid

Lake Formation is a governance layer over the Glue Data Catalog that provides centralized, fine-grained permissions (database/table/column/row-level) for lake data, instead of managing many S3 bucket policies. You grant "SELECT on table X, columns a,b" to a principal, and Athena/Redshift Spectrum/Glue enforce it. It simplifies securing a multi-team lake and supports data sharing and tag-based access.

Q11. What is AWS Glue and what are its main pieces? basic

Glue is AWS's serverless data-integration service. Key pieces: the Data Catalog (a metastore of databases/tables/partitions/schemas), crawlers (scan S3/JDBC sources to infer schema and populate the catalog), and Glue ETL jobs (serverless Apache Spark — or Python shell — jobs that transform data). It's roughly the AWS analogue of ADF + a managed Spark engine + a Hive metastore.

Q12. What does a Glue crawler do and what is the Data Catalog? basic

A crawler scans a data location (an S3 prefix or a JDBC source), infers the schema and partitions, and creates/updates table definitions in the Data Catalog. The Data Catalog is a central metadata store (Hive-compatible) that Athena, Redshift Spectrum, EMR, and Glue jobs all read, so they share one definition of "what tables exist and where their files are." Without it, Athena wouldn't know your S3 files form a table.

Q13. What is Amazon Athena and how is it priced? basic

Athena is a serverless interactive query service that runs Trino/Presto SQL directly over files in S3, using the Glue Data Catalog for schema. There are no servers to manage. Pricing is primarily per terabyte of data scanned per query — which is why partitioning, columnar Parquet, and compression (all of which reduce bytes scanned) directly cut your bill.

Q14. How does partitioning speed up and cheapen Athena queries? mid

Partitioning stores data in prefixes keyed by a column (e.g. dt=2026-07-05/). When a query filters on that column, Athena prunes and reads only the matching partitions instead of the whole dataset — less data scanned = faster and cheaper. Add partitions via crawler, MSCK REPAIR TABLE/ALTER TABLE ADD PARTITION, or partition projection (compute partitions from a formula to avoid catalog lookups on huge tables).

Q15. What is CTAS in Athena and why use it? mid

CREATE TABLE AS SELECT (CTAS) runs a query and writes the results to a new S3 location as a new table — commonly used to convert raw CSV/JSON into partitioned, compressed Parquet, or to materialize an aggregate. It's a lightweight, serverless way to do transformation and file-format optimization without a Spark job. INSERT INTO can then append more partitions.

Q16. Why are "small files" a problem, and how do you fix them? hard

Thousands of tiny files force engines (Athena, Spark, Redshift Spectrum) to open and list each file, adding huge per-file overhead and slowing queries. Fix by compacting: rewrite into fewer, larger files (~128 MB–1 GB) using Glue/Spark coalesce/repartition, Athena CTAS, or a scheduled compaction job. Also use columnar formats and sensible partition granularity so you don't create too many partitions.

Q17. What is a Glue ETL job and what languages/engines does it use? mid

A Glue ETL job is serverless Apache Spark (PySpark/Scala) — or a lighter "Python shell" for small tasks — that reads from sources, transforms, and writes to sinks, using the Data Catalog and Glue's DynamicFrames (a schema-flexible DataFrame). You allocate DPUs (data processing units) for compute. It's how you do heavy transformations that Athena SQL alone can't, without managing a cluster.

Q18. Scenario: a Glue Spark job fails with out-of-memory. How do you fix it? hard

OOM usually comes from data skew, huge shuffles, wide transformations, or collecting to the driver. Fixes: increase worker type/DPUs and number of workers; repartition to spread data and reduce partition size; avoid collect()/broadcasting large tables (broadcast only small ones); handle skewed keys (salting); push filters/projections early to read less; enable Glue job bookmarks to process incrementally; and use columnar predicate pushdown. Check the Spark UI/CloudWatch logs to locate the failing stage.

Q19. What are Glue job bookmarks? mid

Job bookmarks let a Glue job track what it has already processed (by tracking S3 object timestamps/keys or a JDBC bookmark column) so that reruns process only new data — Glue's built-in incremental mechanism. Enable it in the job config and use bookmark-aware sources. It saves cost and prevents reprocessing the whole dataset each run.

Q20. What is Amazon Redshift and its node architecture? mid

Redshift is AWS's petabyte-scale, columnar, MPP data warehouse. A cluster has one leader node (parses/plans queries, aggregates results) and multiple compute nodes (each split into slices that store and process a portion of the data in parallel). Columnar storage + compression + MPP make analytic (OLAP) queries over big tables fast. Redshift Serverless removes cluster management and bills per capacity used.

Q21. What are Redshift distribution styles? hard

Distribution controls how a table's rows are spread across slices. KEY: distribute by a column so rows with the same value land together — colocate join keys to avoid data movement. ALL: full copy of the table on every node — good for small dimensions (fast joins, more storage). EVEN: round-robin spread — good when no clear join key or for staging. AUTO: Redshift picks and can change over time. Wrong choice causes skew and slow redistribution during joins.

Q22. What are Redshift sort keys? hard

A sort key defines the physical order of rows on disk, letting Redshift skip blocks (zone maps) when a query filters/ranges on that column — big speedups for time-range and filtered scans. Compound sort keys favour filters that match the leading columns' order; interleaved give more equal weight to multiple columns but are costlier to maintain. Choose sort keys matching your common WHERE/ORDER BY columns (often a date).

Q23. What do VACUUM and ANALYZE do in Redshift? mid

VACUUM reclaims space from deleted rows and re-sorts rows back into sort-key order (deletes/updates leave data unsorted, hurting performance). ANALYZE refreshes table statistics so the query planner makes good choices. Modern Redshift auto-vacuums and auto-analyzes in the background, but for heavy load jobs you may still run them manually after big loads.

Q24. What is Redshift WLM? hard

Workload Management controls query concurrency and memory by routing queries into queues. You can define queues with memory %, concurrency, and rules (e.g. a queue for short dashboards, another for heavy ETL) so a giant query doesn't starve everything else. Automatic WLM lets Redshift manage this dynamically, and Query Monitoring Rules can abort/log runaway queries.

Q25. What is Redshift Spectrum? mid

Spectrum lets Redshift query data directly in S3 (via external tables in the Glue Catalog) without loading it into the cluster. You join "hot" data in Redshift with "cold"/large historical data in S3 in one SQL query. It's billed per data scanned (like Athena). Good for keeping recent data in Redshift and archiving older data in S3 while still querying it.

Q26. What is the COPY command in Redshift? mid

COPY is the high-performance bulk loader that ingests data from S3 (or DynamoDB/EMR) into Redshift tables in parallel across all slices — far faster than row-by-row INSERTs. Best practice: load from multiple, evenly-sized, compressed files (a multiple of the slice count), specify the file format, and use a role for S3 access. UNLOAD does the reverse, exporting query results to S3.

Q27. What is Amazon EMR and when do you choose it? mid

EMR is managed Hadoop ecosystem — Spark, Hive, Presto, HBase, Flink — on clusters you size (EC2 or EMR Serverless). Choose EMR over Glue when you need full control of Spark versions/configs, custom libraries, long-running or very large clusters, non-Spark frameworks (Hive/HBase/Flink), or cheaper large-scale jobs via spot instances. Glue is simpler/serverless; EMR is more flexible/powerful.

Q28. When would you use AWS Lambda for ETL? mid

Lambda runs short (≤15 min), event-driven, stateless functions with no servers. Use it for light ETL: validate/transform a small file when it lands in S3, trigger a downstream job, call an API, or route events. It's not for heavy big-data processing (memory/time limits) — for that use Glue/EMR. Common pattern: S3 put event → Lambda → start a Glue job or Step Functions workflow.

Q29. Step Functions vs MWAA — how do you choose an orchestrator? hard

Step Functions is a serverless state-machine service — great for AWS-native, event-driven, relatively simple-to-moderate workflows with tight AWS service integrations and pay-per-transition pricing. MWAA (Managed Apache Airflow) suits complex DAGs, rich scheduling/backfilling, many custom/third-party operators, and teams that already know Airflow (Python DAGs). Rule of thumb: AWS-only, event-driven, low-ops → Step Functions; complex, code-first, multi-system pipelines → MWAA.

Q30. What is Amazon Kinesis and its main components? mid

Kinesis is AWS's streaming platform. Kinesis Data Streams is a durable, sharded stream you produce to and consume from (like Kafka topics/partitions). Kinesis Data Firehose is a fully managed delivery stream that buffers and auto-loads streaming data into S3/Redshift/OpenSearch with optional transform. Streams give low-level control + replay; Firehose gives zero-ops delivery to a sink.

Q31. Kinesis vs Kafka (MSK) — how do they compare? hard

Both are partitioned, replayable log streams. Kinesis is fully managed and AWS-native (shards, easy Firehose delivery, less ops) but AWS-locked and with some throughput/retention limits. Kafka (self-managed or Amazon MSK, managed Kafka) is the open-standard, portable, huge-ecosystem choice with fine-grained control and Kafka Connect/Streams — but more to operate (even MSK). Pick Kinesis for quick AWS-only pipelines, Kafka/MSK for portability, ecosystem, and existing Kafka skills.

Q32. What is AWS DMS and how is it used for CDC? mid

Database Migration Service migrates data between databases and can do ongoing replication. For CDC (Change Data Capture) it reads the source's transaction log and continuously streams inserts/updates/deletes to a target (e.g. S3 or Redshift). DEs use DMS to do a one-time full load of a source DB and then keep a lake/warehouse copy near-real-time in sync without hammering the source with repeated queries.

Q33. What is Amazon RDS and where does it fit in a data pipeline? basic

RDS is managed relational databases (PostgreSQL, MySQL, SQL Server, Oracle, MariaDB) with automated backups, patching, and HA (Multi-AZ). In a data platform RDS is usually a source (application OLTP DB you extract or CDC from via DMS) or a small metadata/control store. Aurora is AWS's higher-performance RDS-compatible engine.

Q34. Explain EventBridge, SQS, and SNS. mid

EventBridge is an event bus that routes events (from AWS services or your apps) to targets based on rules — great for event-driven orchestration and scheduling. SQS is a durable message queue for decoupling producers/consumers (point-to-point, pull, retries, DLQs). SNS is pub/sub fan-out (one message → many subscribers). A common pattern is SNS → multiple SQS queues, or EventBridge → Lambda/Step Functions.

Q35. What is CloudWatch and how do you use it for pipelines? basic

CloudWatch is AWS's monitoring service: it collects logs (Glue/Lambda/EMR job logs), metrics (durations, errors, throughput), and lets you build alarms and dashboards. You set an alarm like "Glue job failures > 0" or "Lambda errors spike" that triggers an SNS notification/email. CloudWatch Events/EventBridge can also schedule jobs. It's the AWS analogue of Azure Monitor.

Q36. How is S3/Athena/Glue pricing structured (the cost model)? mid

S3: pay for storage (per GB by class), requests (PUT/GET), and data transfer out. Athena: pay per TB scanned — so partitioning/Parquet/compression cut cost directly. Glue: pay per DPU-hour for ETL jobs and crawlers, plus catalog storage/requests. The overall lever for a DE is "scan/process fewer bytes": compress, columnar-format, partition, and compact small files.

Q37. Walk through the AWS free-tier setup for learning. basic

Sign up at aws.amazon.com/free with an email and card (small verification charge). Immediately create a billing alarm / AWS Budgets alert, enable MFA on the root user, and create an IAM user/role for daily work (don't use root). The free tier gives limited S3, Lambda, and other allowances for 12 months plus always-free amounts. Tear down resources after labs to avoid charges.

Q38. Scenario: an Athena query is slow and expensive. How do you optimize it? hard

Reduce bytes scanned: convert data to columnar Parquet/ORC with compression; partition on common filter columns and ensure queries filter on them (check "data scanned" in query stats); select only needed columns (never SELECT *); compact small files into larger ones; use partition projection for huge partition counts; and precompute heavy aggregations with CTAS. Also push filters early and avoid unnecessary joins/ORDER BY on the full set.

Q39. Scenario: choosing between Redshift, Athena, and EMR. hard

Athena: serverless, ad-hoc/occasional SQL over S3, no infra, pay-per-scan — great for exploration and light BI. Redshift: repeated, low-latency, concurrent BI/dashboards on curated data, complex joins, and tight SLAs — a provisioned warehouse worth its cost when query volume is high. EMR: custom/large-scale Spark/Hive processing, ML, or non-SQL frameworks where you need cluster control. Rule of thumb: explore → Athena; serve dashboards → Redshift; heavy custom processing → EMR/Glue.

Q40. Scenario: the "S3 eventual consistency" history and multipart upload. hard

Historically S3 was eventually consistent for overwrite/list operations, which could cause a just-written object to not appear immediately — pipelines added retries or committers to cope. Since Dec 2020, S3 provides strong read-after-write consistency for all operations, removing that class of bug. Multipart upload splits a large object into parts uploaded in parallel and reassembled; it improves throughput and resilience for big files, but incomplete multipart uploads leave orphaned parts that cost money — set a lifecycle rule to abort incomplete multipart uploads.

Q41. Scenario: how do you handle CDC from an OLTP database into your lake? hard

Use AWS DMS with CDC enabled to read the source transaction log and stream changes to S3 (as insert/update/delete records with operation flags), or use Kafka/Debezium into MSK/Kinesis. Land change records in a raw zone, then MERGE them into a Delta/Iceberg/Hudi table (or Redshift) to maintain the current state and history. This avoids repeatedly full-scanning the source and keeps the lake near-real-time.

Q42. What are open table formats (Iceberg/Hudi/Delta) on AWS? hard

They add ACID transactions, upserts/deletes, schema evolution, and time travel on top of S3 files (Parquet). Apache Iceberg is AWS's first-class choice (Athena, Glue, EMR, Redshift support it), Apache Hudi excels at CDC/upsert-heavy pipelines, and Delta Lake is Databricks-native. They turn a plain S3 lake into a reliable "lakehouse," enabling correct incremental updates and concurrent writes that raw Parquet can't do safely.

Q43. What is Amazon MSK? mid

MSK (Managed Streaming for Apache Kafka) is AWS's fully managed Kafka — you get real Apache Kafka brokers with AWS handling provisioning, patching, and HA. You keep Kafka's open API, Kafka Connect, and ecosystem while offloading ops. Choose MSK over Kinesis when you want Kafka compatibility/portability or already have Kafka-based tooling and skills.

Q44. How does Glue Data Catalog relate to Athena, Redshift Spectrum, and EMR? mid

The Glue Data Catalog is a shared, Hive-compatible metastore. Athena reads it to know table schemas/locations; Redshift Spectrum uses it for external tables; EMR/Spark and Glue jobs can use it as their metastore too. Populate it once (crawler or CREATE TABLE), and every engine sees the same tables/partitions — a single source of truth for lake metadata.

Q45. What is the difference between ETL and ELT on AWS? mid

ETL transforms before loading (e.g. a Glue Spark job cleans data, then writes curated Parquet). ELT loads raw first then transforms in the target (load raw to S3/Redshift, then transform with Athena CTAS or Redshift SQL). AWS supports both; ELT is popular because S3 storage is cheap and Athena/Redshift/Glue provide elastic transform compute over the landed raw data.

Q46. How do you secure a data lake on AWS end-to-end? hard

Layers: IAM least-privilege roles (no long-lived keys), S3 bucket policies + Block Public Access, SSE-KMS encryption with audited keys, Lake Formation for fine-grained table/column/row permissions on the catalog, VPC endpoints so traffic stays private, CloudTrail for API audit logging, and TLS in transit. For fintech, add KMS key policies, data masking/column filtering via Lake Formation, and separate accounts per environment.

Q47. Design an end-to-end batch data platform on AWS. hard

Sources (RDS/on-prem/APIs) → ingest via DMS (CDC), Glue, or Lambda into S3 raw (partitioned, KMS-encrypted). Glue crawlers catalog it. Glue/EMR Spark cleans to S3 curated (partitioned Parquet/Iceberg); orchestrate with Step Functions or MWAA. Serve via Athena (ad-hoc) and load curated marts into Redshift for dashboards (or query in place with Spectrum). Govern with Lake Formation, monitor/alert with CloudWatch, and control cost with S3 lifecycle + partitioning + right-sized compute.

Q48. Scenario: a Redshift cluster's queries are slowing down over time. What do you check? hard

Check for unsorted/unvacuumed tables from heavy DML (run VACUUM/ANALYZE or rely on auto), poor dist/sort key choices causing skew and broadcast/redistribution, WLM queue contention (a heavy query starving others), missing statistics, and small-file/too-many-columns scans. Use SVV_TABLE_INFO, the query plan (EXPLAIN), and console query monitoring to find the offender, then fix keys, tune WLM, and schedule maintenance.

Q49. What is Amazon Kinesis Data Firehose best used for? mid

Firehose is a zero-management delivery stream: point producers at it and it buffers (by size/time), optionally transforms (via Lambda) and converts format (e.g. JSON→Parquet), then auto-delivers to S3/Redshift/OpenSearch/Splunk. Use it when you just need streaming data reliably landed in a sink without writing/operating consumers — e.g. clickstream/logs → partitioned Parquet in S3.

Q50. Give the Azure↔AWS service mapping every DE should know. mid

Concepts are the same across clouds; only names differ. Learn one column and you can read job posts for both:

CapabilityAzureAWS
Identity & accessEntra ID + RBACIAM (users/roles/policies)
Passwordless service authManaged IdentityIAM Role (assume role)
Object / lake storageADLS Gen2 / BlobAmazon S3
Secrets storeKey VaultSecrets Manager / KMS
Orchestration / ingestionAzure Data FactoryGlue + Step Functions / MWAA
Serverless Spark ETLADF Data Flows / DatabricksAWS Glue ETL
Managed Spark clustersAzure Databricks / Synapse SparkAmazon EMR

Databricks

The lakehouse platform that runs Spark for you — the single most-demanded skill in modern DE job posts.

What & Why

Databricks is a cloud platform (runs on AWS, Azure, or GCP) built by the original creators of Apache Spark. Instead of setting up Spark clusters yourself, you log into a web workspace, spin up a cluster with a few clicks, and write code in notebooks. It bundles Spark, Delta Lake (its storage format), a SQL engine, job scheduling, and data governance into one product. The core idea it sells is the lakehouse: a cheap data lake (files on object storage like S3) that also behaves like a reliable data warehouse (ACID transactions, tables, permissions, fast SQL).

Why does every product/fintech DE job post mention it? Because it collapses a messy stack. Before lakehouse, teams stored raw files in a data lake (cheap but unreliable — no updates, no transactions) AND copied cleaned data into an expensive warehouse for analysts. Databricks lets both live on the same cheap storage. Data engineers use it to build pipelines; data scientists use it for ML on the same tables. One platform, one copy of the data.

For you: Databricks is where your PySpark, Delta, and pipeline skills come together. Interviewers ask about clusters, jobs, Unity Catalog, Autoloader, and the medallion architecture. The good news is you can learn nearly all of it for free on the Databricks Free Edition (formerly Community Edition), which gives you a real workspace and a small compute cluster.

Learning Checklist

Projects & Practice

  • Free Edition signup & first notebook — Sign up at the Databricks Free Edition (databricks.com/learn/free-edition). Upload a small Kaggle CSV (e.g. "NYC Yellow Taxi" or "Netflix Movies and TV Shows"), read it into a DataFrame, and write it as a Delta table. Proves: workspace navigation, clusters, DataFrame I/O.
  • Medallion pipeline notebook — Take a raw Kaggle CSV (e.g. "Online Retail" or "Brazilian E-Commerce (Olist)"). Build three layers: bronze = load raw CSV as-is to Delta; silver = clean types, drop bad rows, dedupe; gold = aggregate (e.g. revenue per month per category). Proves: medallion design, PySpark transforms, Delta writes.
  • Schedule it as a Job — Wrap the medallion notebook (or split into 3 tasks) in a Databricks Workflow with task dependencies bronze→silver→gold, a daily schedule, and a retry. Proves: orchestration, job clusters, production thinking.
  • Autoloader mini-demo — Drop new CSV files into a folder and use Autoloader (cloudFiles) to incrementally load only new files into a bronze Delta table. Proves: incremental ingestion, a very common interview topic.

Interview Q&A (50)

Q1. What is Databricks in one sentence? basic

Databricks is a cloud platform, built by the creators of Apache Spark, that provides managed Spark compute, the Delta Lake storage format, notebooks, job scheduling, and data governance so teams can build data and ML pipelines on a "lakehouse" without managing infrastructure themselves.

Q2. What is a "lakehouse" and what problem does it solve? basic

A lakehouse combines the cheap, scalable storage of a data lake (files on object storage) with the reliability and management features of a data warehouse (ACID transactions, tables, schema, permissions, fast SQL). It solves the old two-system problem where you kept raw data in a lake and copied cleaned data into a separate expensive warehouse, maintaining two copies and two pipelines. With a lakehouse, both analytics and ML run on one governed copy of the data on cheap storage.

Q3. Does Databricks run on one cloud? basic

No — it's available on AWS, Azure (as "Azure Databricks", a first-party service), and Google Cloud. The core experience (notebooks, clusters, Unity Catalog, Delta) is the same; only some infra details and the cloud storage backend differ.

Q4. What is a cluster in Databricks? basic

A cluster is a set of cloud VMs that runs your Spark code: one driver node (coordinates the job, runs your notebook's main thread) and one or more worker nodes (execute tasks in parallel). You attach a notebook or job to a cluster to run code on it. When you stop the cluster, the VMs are released and you stop paying.

Q5. What is a notebook and why cells? basic

A notebook is an interactive document of code cells you run one at a time, seeing output under each. Databricks notebooks let you mix languages per cell using magics: %python, %sql, %scala, %md (Markdown), %sh. Cells are great for exploration but for production you generally move logic into scheduled jobs.

Q6. All-purpose cluster vs job cluster — what's the difference and which is cheaper? basic

An all-purpose (interactive) cluster stays up while you develop, can be shared by several users, and is billed at a higher rate. A job cluster is created automatically when a scheduled job starts and terminated when it finishes — it's ephemeral, isolated, and billed at a lower DBU rate. Rule of thumb: use all-purpose for dev/exploration, job clusters for every scheduled production run.

Q7. What is a DBU? basic

A DBU (Databricks Unit) is Databricks' unit of processing consumption per hour. Your bill has two parts: the cloud VM cost (paid to AWS/Azure/GCP) plus the DBU cost (paid to Databricks). Different workloads have different DBU rates — e.g. job compute is cheaper per DBU than all-purpose, and SQL/serverless have their own rates. Bigger or more nodes = more DBUs/hour.

Q8. What is autoscaling and what does it save? mid

Autoscaling lets a cluster automatically add workers when there's a backlog of tasks and remove them when idle, between a min and max you set. It saves money on bursty workloads (you don't pay for a big fixed cluster during quiet periods) and improves performance on heavy stages. The driver is never autoscaled — only workers.

Q9. What are spot instances and where should you use them? mid

Spot (AWS) / Spot VMs (Azure) / Preemptible (GCP) instances are spare cloud capacity sold cheaply (often 60–90% off) but can be reclaimed by the cloud with little notice. Use them for worker nodes to cut cost, ideally with a fallback to on-demand. Keep the driver on-demand — if the driver is reclaimed, the whole job dies. Spot is great for fault-tolerant batch jobs, riskier for latency-sensitive work.

Q10. What is auto-termination and why does it matter for cost? basic

Auto-termination shuts down an all-purpose cluster after a set idle period (e.g. 30 minutes with no commands). It's the single biggest cost mistake beginners make — leaving a big cluster running overnight. Always set a short auto-termination; job clusters terminate themselves automatically after the job.

Q11. What is Photon? mid

Photon is Databricks' native, vectorized query engine written in C++ that accelerates SQL and DataFrame operations (scans, joins, aggregations) transparently. You enable it via the cluster's runtime; your code doesn't change. It costs more DBUs/hour but often finishes faster, so total cost can be lower for the right workloads. It shines on SQL-heavy and Delta workloads; it doesn't accelerate arbitrary Python UDFs.

Q12. What is the Databricks Runtime (DBR)? mid

DBR is the pre-built software image on each cluster: a specific Spark version plus Delta, Photon (optional), Python/Scala libraries, and optimizations. You pick a runtime version when creating a cluster (e.g. an LTS = Long Term Support version for stability). "ML" runtimes add ML libraries and GPU support. Matching your code to a runtime version avoids library surprises.

Q13. What is DBFS? mid

DBFS (Databricks File System) is a filesystem abstraction over cloud object storage, accessed via paths like dbfs:/… and /dbfs/…. It historically held uploaded files, libraries, and a default warehouse dir. It's the "old" way of accessing files; Unity Catalog Volumes are the governed replacement. Storing production data in the DBFS root is discouraged today.

Q14. What is a mount and how does it differ from a UC Volume? mid

A mount attaches an external cloud bucket (e.g. an S3 path) to a DBFS path using stored credentials, so any user on the workspace can read it — which is a governance hole (no fine-grained control). A Unity Catalog Volume is a governed storage location registered in UC, so access is controlled by GRANTs and audited, and paths look like /Volumes/catalog/schema/volume/…. Modern best practice: use UC Volumes and external locations instead of mounts.

Q15. What is Unity Catalog? mid

Unity Catalog (UC) is Databricks' unified governance layer across all workspaces in an account. It provides a central metastore, a three-level namespace catalog.schema.table, fine-grained permissions (GRANT/REVOKE), automatic data lineage, auditing, and governance of tables, views, volumes, models, and functions. Before UC, each workspace had its own Hive metastore with two-level names and no central governance.

Q16. Explain the three-level namespace in Unity Catalog. mid

UC names objects as catalog.schema.table (also called catalog.database.table). A catalog is the top container (often per environment or domain, e.g. prod, finance); a schema groups related tables; a table/view/volume is the object. You can set a default catalog and schema so short names resolve. The legacy Hive metastore only had two levels (database.table).

Q17. How do permissions work in Unity Catalog? mid

UC uses SQL-style GRANTs on securable objects, inherited down the hierarchy. Example: GRANT USE CATALOG ON CATALOG prod TO `data_readers`; then GRANT SELECT ON TABLE prod.sales.orders TO `data_readers`;. Privileges include USE CATALOG/SCHEMA, SELECT, MODIFY, CREATE TABLE, etc. Grants are given to users, service principals, or groups (prefer groups). Every object has an owner who can manage grants.

Q18. Why is Delta the default table format? basic

When you run CREATE TABLE or df.write.saveAsTable(...) in Databricks, you get a Delta table unless you specify another format. Delta adds a transaction log on top of Parquet files, giving ACID transactions, updates/deletes, time travel, and schema enforcement. Because it's the default and everything (Autoloader, DLT, streaming) targets it, "Delta everywhere" is a core Databricks assumption.

Q19. What are Databricks Jobs / Workflows? basic

Jobs (now under "Workflows") are the built-in orchestrator. A job is a DAG of tasks — each task can run a notebook, a Python script/wheel, a SQL query, or a DLT pipeline. You define dependencies between tasks, a schedule (cron) or trigger, retries, timeouts, and email/Slack alerts. It's Databricks' native alternative to needing Airflow for simple pipelines.

Q20. How do task dependencies work in a Workflow? mid

Each task lists the tasks it "Depends on". A task runs only after all its upstream dependencies succeed, forming a DAG (e.g. bronze → silver → gold). You can fan out (multiple tasks depend on one) and fan in. You can also set run-if conditions (e.g. run even if a parent failed) and pass values between tasks. Failed tasks can retry independently.

Q21. What is dbutils? Name a few uses. basic

dbutils is a Databricks utility library available in notebooks. Common uses: dbutils.fs.ls("/path") and cp/mv/rm for files; dbutils.widgets.text("date","") to create notebook parameters; dbutils.secrets.get(scope, key) to fetch credentials; dbutils.notebook.run("child", 60, args) to call another notebook; dbutils.jobs.taskValues to pass values between job tasks.

Q22. What are widgets and why use them in jobs? mid

Widgets are notebook parameters created with dbutils.widgets (text, dropdown, combobox, multiselect). They let one notebook be reused with different inputs — e.g. a run_date widget so a job can pass yesterday's date. In a Workflow task you set parameter values that populate the widgets, enabling parameterized, backfillable pipelines.

Q23. How does Git integration (Repos) work? mid

Databricks Repos let you clone a Git repo (GitHub, GitLab, Azure DevOps, Bitbucket) into the workspace and do branch/commit/pull/push from the UI. This enables proper version control and CI/CD: develop on a feature branch, run tests, merge to main, and have jobs run code from a specific branch or tag rather than editing notebooks live in production.

Q24. How do secrets work and why not hardcode credentials? mid

You create a secret scope and store key/value secrets in it (Databricks-backed or Azure Key Vault-backed). In code you fetch with dbutils.secrets.get("scope","key"), and the value is redacted in notebook output/logs. Hardcoding passwords in notebooks is a security risk (they end up in Git, logs, and shared workspaces) and fails audits — secrets keep them out of code.

Q25. What is a SQL warehouse and how does it differ from an all-purpose cluster? mid

A SQL warehouse is compute optimized for SQL/BI queries (used by the SQL editor, dashboards, and BI tools via JDBC/ODBC). It can be classic, pro, or serverless (starts in seconds, Databricks-managed). Unlike an all-purpose cluster (general Spark, notebooks, any language), a SQL warehouse only runs SQL, auto-scales for concurrency, and is tuned for low-latency analytics. DEs use all-purpose/job clusters for pipelines; analysts use SQL warehouses.

Q26. What is Autoloader and what problem does it solve? mid

Autoloader (the cloudFiles Structured Streaming source) incrementally ingests new files from cloud storage as they arrive, without re-scanning the whole folder each run. It tracks which files were already processed using a scalable checkpoint/notification mechanism, supports schema inference and evolution, and scales to millions of files. It replaces slow, error-prone "list the directory and diff" logic for file ingestion.

Q27. Give a minimal Autoloader snippet. mid 
df = (spark.readStream
        .format("cloudFiles")
        .option("cloudFiles.format", "csv")
        .option("cloudFiles.schemaLocation", "/Volumes/dev/raw/_schema")
        .load("/Volumes/dev/raw/incoming/"))

(df.writeStream
   .option("checkpointLocation", "/Volumes/dev/raw/_chk")
   .trigger(availableNow=True)
   .toTable("dev.bronze.events"))

trigger(availableNow=True) processes all currently-available new files then stops — great for scheduled batch-style incremental loads.

Q28. How does Autoloader track already-processed files? hard

It maintains state in a checkpoint (RocksDB-backed) so it remembers processed files without listing the whole directory. Two discovery modes exist: directory listing (default, lists incrementally) and file notification (subscribes to cloud events like S3/EventGrid notifications), which scales better for very high file volumes. The schema location stores the inferred/evolved schema separately from the checkpoint.

Q29. What is DLT / Lakeflow Declarative Pipelines? mid

Delta Live Tables (now branded Lakeflow Declarative Pipelines) is a declarative ETL framework: instead of writing orchestration, you declare tables/views and their transformations, and Databricks builds the dependency graph, manages the compute, handles incremental processing, retries, and data quality. You write things like @dlt.table functions returning DataFrames, and DLT figures out the order and runs them.

Q30. What are DLT expectations? mid

Expectations are declarative data-quality rules attached to DLT tables. You declare a constraint and an action: @dlt.expect("valid_amt", "amount > 0") just tracks/logs violations; @dlt.expect_or_drop drops bad rows; @dlt.expect_or_fail aborts the pipeline. This bakes quality checks and metrics into the pipeline instead of separate ad-hoc tests.

Q31. Explain the medallion architecture. basic

Medallion is a layered design for lakehouse data: Bronze = raw ingested data, appended as-is (a faithful landing zone, easy to reprocess). Silver = cleaned, deduplicated, type-cast, conformed and joined ("single source of truth" tables). Gold = business-level aggregates/marts optimized for reporting and ML. Data flows bronze → silver → gold, each layer more refined and trusted than the last.

Q32. Why keep a bronze layer instead of cleaning on ingest? mid

Bronze preserves the raw source exactly, so if a downstream bug or a changed business rule is found, you can reprocess from bronze without re-pulling from the source (which may no longer have the old data). It also decouples ingestion speed from transformation logic, gives you an audit trail, and lets multiple silver tables be built from the same raw feed.

Q33. What node/cluster size should a new pipeline start with? mid

Start small and measure. For a first pipeline, a small all-purpose or job cluster with autoscaling (e.g. 1 driver + 2–4 workers) is plenty; enable Photon for SQL-heavy work. Watch the Spark UI: if stages spill to disk or tasks queue, scale up; if workers sit idle, scale down. Prefer more, smaller nodes for parallelism unless you hit memory pressure (then fewer, larger nodes). Don't guess a huge cluster "to be safe" — that just burns money.

Q34. List concrete cost-optimization levers on Databricks. hard

1) Use job clusters not all-purpose for scheduled work. 2) Set aggressive auto-termination and autoscaling. 3) Use spot workers with on-demand driver. 4) Right-size nodes using the Spark UI. 5) Enable Photon where it speeds SQL enough to lower total cost. 6) OPTIMIZE Delta tables and prune with partitioning/Z-ORDER/liquid clustering to scan less data. 7) Cache/reuse instead of recomputing. 8) Use serverless SQL for spiky BI. 9) Consolidate tiny files; 10) tag clusters and monitor with system tables / cost dashboards.

Q35. How do you pass data between two tasks in a Workflow? mid

Use task values: in an upstream task, dbutils.jobs.taskValues.set(key="row_count", value=1234); in a downstream task, dbutils.jobs.taskValues.get(taskKey="bronze", key="row_count"). This lets you share small metadata (counts, dates, status). For large data you pass tables, not values — the tasks read/write shared Delta tables.

Q36. How would you parameterize a job for backfills? mid

Add widgets (e.g. start_date, end_date) to the notebook, read them with dbutils.widgets.get, and drive your filters from them. In the Workflow, set default parameter values for daily runs, but for a backfill trigger a "Run now with different parameters" (or loop a range) to reprocess historical dates. Making the job idempotent (overwrite/MERGE by date partition) is key so re-runs don't duplicate data.

Q37. Scenario: a scheduled job failed at 3am. Walk through your debug flow. hard

1) Open Workflows → the job run; identify which task failed and read its error message/stack trace. 2) Check the cluster/driver logs and Spark UI for the failed stage (OOM? shuffle spill? bad record?). 3) Distinguish transient (spot reclaim, cloud throttle, timeout) from deterministic (schema change, null in a key, permission denied). 4) If transient, confirm retries and maybe rerun. 5) If data-driven, inspect the offending input files/partition and the source. 6) Fix (adjust cluster, code, or upstream), rerun the single failed task (not the whole pipeline if downstream is idempotent). 7) Add an alert/expectation so it's caught earlier next time. 8) Document root cause.

Q38. Scenario: how do you decide between all-purpose and job cluster for a given workload? mid

Ask: is it interactive dev or scheduled production? Interactive exploration where a human iterates → all-purpose (kept warm, shared). Scheduled/triggered pipeline → job cluster (ephemeral, cheaper DBU rate, isolated so one job can't affect another). Also, a shared BI/ad-hoc SQL need → SQL warehouse. Cost, isolation, and startup latency drive the choice; production almost always uses job clusters.

Q39. Scenario: migrating from the Hive metastore to Unity Catalog — what's the approach? hard

1) Enable UC in the account and create a metastore. 2) Set up storage (external locations/volumes) and identities (SCIM groups). 3) Design the catalog/schema layout (e.g. by env or domain). 4) Use the UCX assessment/migration tooling or SYNC/CTAS to move tables: managed Hive tables → managed UC tables; external tables → registered as UC external tables. 5) Repoint code from two-level db.table to three-level catalog.schema.table and replace mounts with volumes/external locations. 6) Set GRANTs to replace ad-hoc ACLs. 7) Validate row counts and lineage, run in parallel, then cut over and decommission legacy access.

Q40. Managed vs external tables in Databricks — what's the difference? mid

For a managed table, Databricks/UC controls both the metadata and the underlying data files in the metastore's managed storage; DROP TABLE deletes the data. For an external table, you point at a specific external location you own; UC manages metadata but DROP TABLE leaves the files. Use managed for most lakehouse tables (simpler, UC-optimized); external when files must live at a fixed path or be shared with other tools.

Q41. What is a service principal and when do you use one? mid

A service principal is a non-human identity (like a robot user) used by automation/CI-CD and production jobs, so pipelines don't run as a specific person's account. You grant it UC permissions and use its token/OAuth for API and job execution. Best practice: run production Workflows as a service principal for stability, security, and auditability.

Q42. What is Delta Sharing? mid

Delta Sharing is an open protocol (governed by Unity Catalog) to share live Delta tables with other Databricks accounts, clouds, or external tools without copying data — the recipient reads directly from your storage under controlled, revocable access. It's used for cross-org data sharing (e.g. sharing curated datasets with a partner or a fintech customer) without ETL exports.

Q43. How does Databricks compare to running your own Spark on a VM cluster? mid

Self-managed Spark means you provision VMs, install/patch Spark, tune configs, build orchestration, and handle failures yourself. Databricks manages the runtime, one-click clusters, autoscaling, Delta, Photon, notebooks, Jobs, and Unity Catalog governance out of the box. You trade some cost (DBUs) and lock-in for huge time savings and reliability. Interviewers want you to know Databricks is "managed, optimized Spark + a platform", not raw Spark.

Q44. When would you still use Airflow instead of Databricks Workflows? hard

Databricks Workflows is great when the whole pipeline lives in Databricks. Choose Airflow (or another external orchestrator) when you must orchestrate across many systems (Databricks + APIs + dbt + non-Databricks jobs), need complex branching/backfill semantics, a company-standard orchestrator, or team-wide DAG management. Airflow can trigger Databricks jobs via the Databricks provider/operators, so the two are often combined.

Q45. What is cluster policy and why do platform teams use it? mid

A cluster policy is a set of rules/limits admins apply to control what clusters users can create — allowed node types, max workers, mandatory auto-termination, enforced tags, spot settings, runtime versions. It prevents runaway cost (no one spins a 100-node cluster) and enforces standards. In interviews, mentioning cluster policies signals cost/governance awareness.

Q46. Scenario: your gold aggregation query is slow and scans terabytes. What do you check? hard

1) Are silver/gold tables Delta and OPTIMIZEd (no tiny-file problem)? 2) Is the table partitioned or liquid-clustered on the filter/join columns so pruning skips files? 3) Is the query filtering early (predicate pushdown) or scanning everything? 4) Look at the Spark UI for skew or a huge shuffle; consider broadcast joins for small dims. 5) Enable Photon. 6) Cache reused datasets. 7) Materialize expensive intermediates. 8) Right-size the cluster. The goal is to scan less data and shuffle less.

Q47. What is the "small files problem" and how do you fix it on Databricks? mid

Streaming/frequent writes create many tiny Parquet files, which slows reads (huge listing + metadata overhead) and wastes I/O. Fixes: run OPTIMIZE to compact files, enable Auto Optimize (optimizeWrite + autoCompact), use liquid clustering, and avoid over-partitioning. Well-sized files (~100 MB–1 GB) dramatically improve scan performance.

Q48. How do you set up the Databricks Free Edition to practice? basic

Go to the Databricks Free Edition sign-up, register with an email, and you get a hosted workspace with limited serverless compute and Unity Catalog. Create a notebook, attach to the provided compute, upload a small CSV (Data → Add data or a UC volume), read it with Spark, and write a Delta table. It's enough to practice notebooks, Delta, SQL, medallion basics, and small Workflows for free.

Q49. What is a Databricks Asset Bundle (DAB)? hard

Asset Bundles are an infrastructure-as-code way to define Databricks projects (jobs, DLT pipelines, notebooks, cluster configs) in YAML, version them in Git, and deploy consistently across dev/staging/prod via the Databricks CLI. They enable CI/CD and reproducible deployments instead of hand-editing jobs in each environment. Mentioning DABs signals modern, production-grade Databricks practice.

Q50. Design question: build a daily pipeline from an S3 landing zone to a gold sales mart. Outline it. hard

Ingest: Autoloader (cloudFiles, trigger=availableNow) reads new files from the S3/UC volume into bronze.raw_sales (append, schema evolution on). Silver: a task cleans types, drops nulls in keys, dedupes, and MERGEs into silver.sales keyed by order_id (idempotent). Gold: a task aggregates daily revenue per region into gold.sales_daily. Wrap the three as a Workflow with dependencies bronze→silver→gold, on a job cluster with spot workers, a daily schedule, retries, and email alerts. Govern all tables in Unity Catalog with GRANTs, run as a service principal, and OPTIMIZE gold tables. Add DLT expectations or basic quality checks for validity. This covers ingestion, medallion, idempotency, orchestration, governance, and cost.

Delta Lake & Lakehouse

The storage format that gives cheap data-lake files database-grade reliability — ACID, updates, and time travel on object storage.

What & Why (from zero)

A data lake is just files (usually Parquet) sitting in cloud object storage like S3. It's cheap and scales infinitely, but plain files have big problems: there's no ACID guarantee (if a write job crashes halfway, readers see half-written garbage), you can't easily UPDATE or DELETE a row (files are immutable), two writers can corrupt each other, and there's no consistent view of "what is the table right now." Analysts couldn't trust it, so companies copied data into expensive warehouses.

Delta Lake fixes this. A Delta table is still just Parquet data files, but with a transaction log — a folder called _delta_log containing ordered JSON commit files that record every change (which files were added/removed) as an atomic transaction. Readers consult the log to know exactly which files make up the current table, so they never see partial writes. This log is what delivers ACID transactions, updates/deletes/merges, time travel (read an old version), schema enforcement, and concurrent-write safety — all on cheap object storage. That combination (lake storage + warehouse reliability) is the lakehouse.

Delta isn't the only open table format doing this. Apache Iceberg (popular with Snowflake, Trino, AWS, Netflix-origin) and Apache Hudi (Uber-origin, strong at streaming upserts/CDC) solve the same problem with different metadata designs. Interviewers like you to know Delta is Databricks-native and the default, while Iceberg is the more vendor-neutral open standard — and that the three are converging (Delta UniForm can expose Delta tables as Iceberg).

Learning Checklist

Projects & Practice

  • Create & explore Delta tables — In Databricks Free Edition, load a Kaggle CSV, write it as Delta, then inspect: DESCRIBE DETAIL, DESCRIBE HISTORY, and peek at the _delta_log folder. Proves: you understand what a Delta table physically is.
  • Upsert + time travel + OPTIMIZE exercise — Load orders, run a MERGE to upsert a batch of updated orders, then use time travel to compare the table before/after (VERSION AS OF), and run OPTIMIZE + VACUUM. Proves: MERGE, versioning, maintenance.
  • Build an SCD Type 2 dimension — Take a customers feed where addresses change over time. Use a MERGE pattern to keep history (close the old row, insert a new current row with effective dates). Proves: dimensional modeling in a lakehouse — a very common interview task.

Interview Q&A (25)

Q1. What exactly is a Delta table on disk? basic

It's a directory containing Parquet data files plus a _delta_log subfolder. The log holds ordered commit files (00000000000000000000.json, then ...001.json, etc.) recording each transaction as a set of "add file / remove file" actions, plus periodic Parquet checkpoints that summarize state so readers don't replay every commit. Reading the table means reading the log to find the current set of valid Parquet files.

Q2. What problems of a plain Parquet data lake does Delta solve? basic

Plain Parquet has no transactions (partial/failed writes are visible), no atomic updates/deletes, no concurrency control, no consistent snapshot, and no schema enforcement. Delta adds ACID transactions, UPDATE/DELETE/MERGE, optimistic concurrency, time travel, schema enforcement/evolution, and constraints — turning a pile of files into a reliable table.

Q3. What does ACID mean and how does Delta achieve it without a database server? mid

ACID = Atomicity, Consistency, Isolation, Durability. Delta achieves it via the transaction log: a commit is atomic because it's a single log file that either fully lands or doesn't; isolated via optimistic concurrency (each writer reads the current version, and the log commit fails if another writer committed conflicting changes first, forcing a retry); consistent because readers only ever see committed file sets; durable because both files and log live in durable object storage.

Q4. How does Delta handle two concurrent writers? mid

It uses optimistic concurrency control. Each writer reads the current table version, does its work, and tries to commit the next log version number. Object-storage atomic "put-if-absent" ensures only one writer wins that version; the loser detects the conflict, checks whether the changes are compatible, and either commits the next version or retries. Non-overlapping operations (e.g. appends to different partitions) generally succeed; true conflicts (both rewriting the same files) cause one to retry or fail.

Q5. How do you do time travel in Delta? basic 
-- by version
SELECT * FROM sales VERSION AS OF 12;
-- by timestamp
SELECT * FROM sales TIMESTAMP AS OF '2026-07-01';

-- PySpark
df = spark.read.option("versionAsOf", 12).table("sales")

DESCRIBE HISTORY sales lists versions, operations, and timestamps you can travel to.

Q6. Give real uses for time travel. mid

Recover from a bad write (RESTORE TABLE sales TO VERSION AS OF 11), audit "what did the table look like yesterday," reproduce an ML training set exactly, debug a pipeline by diffing two versions, and rollback accidental deletes. It works only within the retention window before VACUUM removes old files.

Q7. What is MERGE and what does it do? basic

MERGE (upsert) combines INSERT, UPDATE, and DELETE in one atomic statement based on a match condition — the core operation for applying changes/CDC to a table.

MERGE INTO target t
USING updates u
ON t.id = u.id
WHEN MATCHED THEN UPDATE SET *
WHEN NOT MATCHED THEN INSERT *;
Q8. Write a MERGE that also deletes flagged rows. mid 
MERGE INTO customers t
USING staged_changes s
ON t.customer_id = s.customer_id
WHEN MATCHED AND s.op = 'D' THEN DELETE
WHEN MATCHED AND s.op = 'U' THEN UPDATE SET *
WHEN NOT MATCHED AND s.op != 'D' THEN INSERT *;

This applies a CDC feed (op = I/U/D) idempotently in one transaction.

Q9. What is schema enforcement? basic

By default, Delta rejects a write whose columns/types don't match the table's schema (e.g. an extra column or a string where an int is expected). This prevents silently corrupting the table with malformed data — a key reliability feature versus plain Parquet, which would happily append mismatched files.

Q10. What is schema evolution and how do you enable it? mid

Schema evolution lets you deliberately change the schema, e.g. add new columns. Enable per-write with .option("mergeSchema","true") (or spark.databricks.delta.schema.autoMerge.enabled for MERGE), or explicitly with ALTER TABLE ... ADD COLUMNS. It's opt-in so you don't accidentally corrupt the schema — you evolve on purpose.

df.write.format("delta").mode("append")
  .option("mergeSchema","true").saveAsTable("events")
Q11. What is OPTIMIZE and why run it? mid

OPTIMIZE table compacts many small files into fewer, larger, right-sized files (bin-packing), which speeds up reads by reducing file-listing and metadata overhead (the "small files problem" from streaming/frequent writes). You often combine it with clustering. Run it periodically or enable Auto Optimize.

Q12. What is Z-ORDER and how does it help? mid

OPTIMIZE table ZORDER BY (col) physically reorders data so rows with similar values in the chosen column(s) sit in the same files. Combined with Delta's per-file min/max statistics, queries filtering on that column can skip (prune) files that can't match — reading far less data. Best for high-cardinality columns used frequently in filters/joins.

Q13. What is liquid clustering and how does it differ from partitioning/Z-ORDER? hard

Liquid clustering (CLUSTER BY) is a newer, flexible alternative to Hive-style partitioning and Z-ORDER. You declare clustering columns and Delta manages data layout incrementally as data changes, avoiding the rigidity of fixed partitions (which cause skew and small-file issues) and the need to fully rewrite for Z-ORDER. You can even change clustering keys over time. It generally gives good pruning with less tuning.

Q14. Why can over-partitioning hurt, and what's the guidance? mid

Partitioning on a high-cardinality column (e.g. user_id) creates millions of tiny directories/files, exploding metadata and slowing everything (the small-files problem). Guidance: only partition on low-cardinality columns you filter on often (like date), keep each partition reasonably large (>~1 GB), and prefer liquid clustering or Z-ORDER for high-cardinality access patterns instead of partitioning.

Q15. What is VACUUM and what's the danger of a low retention? mid

VACUUM table permanently deletes data files that are no longer referenced by the current table and are older than the retention threshold (default 7 days). This reclaims storage but breaks time travel to versions whose files it removed. Setting retention too low (e.g. 0 hours) can also delete files that in-flight readers/writers still need, causing failures — Delta warns/blocks this by default.

Q16. What is Change Data Feed (CDF)? mid

CDF makes a Delta table emit row-level changes (inserts, updates with pre/post images, deletes) between versions. Enable with TBLPROPERTIES (delta.enableChangeDataFeed = true), then read changes with table_changes('t', startVersion, endVersion). It lets downstream pipelines process only what changed (incremental/CDC) instead of rescanning the whole table.

SELECT * FROM table_changes('silver.orders', 20, 25);
Q17. How is a Delta table used in streaming? mid

A Delta table can be a Structured Streaming source (stream new appended data, tracked via a checkpoint) and a sink (append/merge streaming results with exactly-once guarantees). Combined with the transaction log and checkpoints, this gives reliable, incremental streaming pipelines — e.g. bronze appends stream into silver via foreachBatch MERGE.

Q18. What constraints does Delta support? mid

NOT NULL constraints and CHECK constraints. Example: ALTER TABLE orders ADD CONSTRAINT pos_amt CHECK (amount > 0);. Writes violating a constraint fail, enforcing quality at write time. (Delta also supports informational PRIMARY/FOREIGN KEY declarations in UC that aren't enforced but help tools/optimizers and documentation.)

Q19. Delta vs Iceberg vs Hudi — one line each. mid

Delta Lake: Databricks-native, log-based, the default there; strong ecosystem and Photon integration. Apache Iceberg: vendor-neutral open standard with a rich metadata/snapshot design; broad engine support (Snowflake, Trino, Spark, Flink). Apache Hudi: optimized for streaming upserts and incremental CDC (copy-on-write & merge-on-read). All three provide ACID table formats on object storage; the industry is converging (e.g. Delta UniForm exposes Delta as Iceberg metadata).

Q20. How do you implement SCD Type 2 with MERGE? hard

Keep history by "closing" the current row and inserting a new one when a tracked attribute changes. A common two-step pattern:

-- Step 1: close changed current rows
MERGE INTO dim_customer t
USING staged s
ON t.customer_id = s.customer_id AND t.is_current = true
WHEN MATCHED AND t.address <> s.address THEN UPDATE SET
  t.is_current = false, t.end_date = current_date();

-- Step 2: insert new current versions (new + changed)
INSERT INTO dim_customer
SELECT s.customer_id, s.address, current_date() AS start_date,
       NULL AS end_date, true AS is_current
FROM staged s LEFT JOIN dim_customer t
  ON s.customer_id = t.customer_id AND t.is_current = true
WHERE t.customer_id IS NULL OR t.address <> s.address;

This preserves full history with start/end dates and an is_current flag.

Q21. How do UPDATE/DELETE physically work if Parquet files are immutable? hard

Delta rewrites the affected files: it reads files containing target rows, writes new files with the changes applied, and commits a log entry that removes the old files and adds the new ones (copy-on-write). Newer Delta also supports deletion vectors — instead of rewriting a whole file for a few deleted rows, it records which rows are logically deleted in a side file, deferring the rewrite (merge-on-read) for much faster deletes/updates.

Q22. What are checkpoints in the Delta log and why do they matter? hard
