Every N commits (default 10), Delta writes a Parquet checkpoint summarizing the table state (all current add-file actions). Readers load the latest checkpoint plus only the JSON commits after it, instead of replaying thousands of JSON files — this keeps reads fast even for tables with a long history. It's an internal optimization you don't manage directly.

Q23. Scenario: a nightly job accidentally overwrote a table with empty data. How do you recover? mid

Use time travel: DESCRIBE HISTORY t to find the last good version, verify with SELECT count(*) FROM t VERSION AS OF N, then RESTORE TABLE t TO VERSION AS OF N (or CTAS from that version). This works as long as VACUUM hasn't purged those files (within retention). Afterward, add a row-count/expectation check so an empty overwrite fails the job next time.

Q24. Scenario: MERGE is very slow on a big target. How do you speed it up? hard

Narrow the target rewrite: partition or liquid-cluster/Z-ORDER on the merge key so Delta touches fewer files; add a predicate on the ON clause to prune (e.g. only recent partitions can match); run OPTIMIZE first to avoid tiny files; use deletion vectors; broadcast the (small) source; and ensure the source is deduplicated on the key (duplicate matches error or explode work). Fundamentally you want the MERGE to read/rewrite as few target files as possible.

Q25. Scenario: how would you build a CDC pipeline from a source DB into a silver Delta table? hard

Land the source's change events (from Debezium/Kafka or a CDC export) into a bronze Delta table as raw change records. Then in silver, run a MERGE per batch keyed on the primary key, applying op = I/U/D and keeping the latest change per key (dedupe by op timestamp). Make it idempotent so replays don't duplicate. Optionally enable CDF on silver so downstream gold consumes only changes. Orchestrate as a scheduled/streaming job with checkpoints. This is the canonical lakehouse CDC pattern.

Snowflake

The cloud data warehouse that separates storage from compute — the SQL-first platform fintech and SaaS companies love for its simplicity and elasticity.

What & Why

Snowflake is a fully-managed cloud data warehouse delivered as SaaS. You don't manage servers, indexes, or vacuuming — you just load data and write SQL. Its signature idea is the separation of storage and compute: your data lives once in cloud object storage (managed by Snowflake), and you spin up independent compute clusters (called virtual warehouses) to query it. Different teams can run big queries on their own warehouses over the same data without fighting for resources, and you scale (or pause) compute independently of how much data you store.

Why do fintech and SaaS companies love it? It's simple and elastic: near-zero admin, per-second billing, warehouses that auto-suspend when idle and auto-resume on demand, effortless scaling for concurrency, and powerful features like zero-copy cloning and time travel that make dev/test and recovery trivial. It handles semi-structured JSON natively (great for event/API data common in fintech), has strong security/governance for regulated industries, and lets analysts be productive with plain SQL. Compared to Databricks (Spark/Python/ML-centric lakehouse), Snowflake is the SQL/warehouse-centric choice — many companies use both.

For you: Snowflake is very approachable given your SQL background. Learn the architecture, warehouses and cost control, loading (stages, COPY INTO, Snowpipe), micro-partitions and pruning, time travel/cloning, and streams & tasks for pipelines. You can practice everything on a 30-day free trial with credits.

Learning Checklist

Projects & Practice

  • 30-day free trial setup — Sign up at signup.snowflake.com, pick a cloud/region, and get ~$400 in credits. Create a warehouse (XS), a database, and a schema. Proves: architecture, warehouses, worksheets.
  • Load a Kaggle CSV via stage + COPY INTO — Create a file format and an internal stage, PUT/upload a Kaggle CSV (e.g. "Credit Card Fraud" or "Online Retail"), then COPY INTO a table. Validate with counts and error handling (ON_ERROR). Proves: stages, file formats, bulk loading.
  • Incremental pipeline with streams + tasks — Create a stream on a raw table to capture new rows, and a task that runs on a schedule to MERGE those changes into a curated table. Proves: CDC/ELT orchestration inside Snowflake.
  • Query JSON with VARIANT + FLATTEN — Load a JSON dataset (e.g. nested events/API responses) into a VARIANT column and query nested fields and arrays using dot/colon notation and LATERAL FLATTEN. Proves: semi-structured handling — a big fintech/SaaS use case.

Interview Q&A (50)

Q1. What is Snowflake in one sentence? basic

Snowflake is a fully-managed, cloud-native SaaS data warehouse that separates storage from compute, so you store data once in managed cloud storage and run independent, elastic compute "virtual warehouses" over it using plain SQL — with no servers, indexes, or manual tuning to manage.

Q2. Describe Snowflake's three-layer architecture. basic

1) Database storage — your data stored compressed and columnar in cloud object storage as micro-partitions, managed entirely by Snowflake. 2) Compute (Query Processing) — virtual warehouses, independent MPP clusters that execute queries against the storage. 3) Cloud Services — the "brain": query optimizer, metadata, transaction management, security/authentication, and access control. The three scale independently.

Q3. What does "separation of storage and compute" actually buy you? basic

You can scale compute up/down or run many independent warehouses over the same single copy of data without moving/copying it, and pay for storage and compute separately. Teams don't contend for resources (ETL on one warehouse, BI on another), you pause compute when idle (storage stays cheap), and you resize instantly for a heavy job. Traditional warehouses tightly coupled the two, so scaling one forced scaling the other.

Q4. What is a virtual warehouse? basic

A virtual warehouse is a cluster of compute resources (CPU, memory, local SSD cache) that executes queries, loads, and DML. It has a size (XS, S, M, L, XL … up to 6XL) that determines how much compute it has and how many credits/hour it burns. Warehouses are independent — creating one doesn't affect your data, and you can have many for different workloads.

Q5. How do warehouse sizes relate to cost and speed? mid

Each size step (XS→S→M→L…) roughly doubles the compute and the credits consumed per hour (XS=1, S=2, M=4, L=8, …). A bigger warehouse can run a single large query about twice as fast, so cost per query can stay similar while latency drops — but only if the query can use the extra resources. Idle time on a big warehouse is expensive, so size to the workload and rely on auto-suspend.

Q6. What are auto-suspend and auto-resume? basic

Auto-suspend pauses a warehouse after a configured idle period (e.g. 60 seconds) so you stop paying when no queries run. Auto-resume automatically restarts it (in ~seconds) when the next query arrives. Together they make per-second billing efficient — you're generally billed only while queries actually run (with a 60-second minimum on resume).

Q7. Scaling up vs scaling out in Snowflake — what's the difference? mid

Scale up = resize a warehouse to a bigger size for faster/heavier individual queries (more compute per query). Scale out = a multi-cluster warehouse that adds/removes whole clusters to handle more concurrent queries/users, keeping queues short. Rule: size up for query complexity/data volume; scale out (multi-cluster) for concurrency.

Q8. What is a multi-cluster warehouse and its scaling policies? mid

A multi-cluster warehouse can run several clusters of the same size in parallel, spinning up extra clusters when concurrency/queuing rises and shutting them down when demand drops, between a min and max cluster count. Policies: Standard (spin up eagerly to minimize queuing) vs Economy (spin up more conservatively to save credits). It's the answer to "many users hitting dashboards at once."

Q9. What are databases, schemas, and stages? basic

A database contains schemas, which contain objects (tables, views, stages, etc.); you reference objects as db.schema.object. A stage is a location that holds data files for loading/unloading: internal stages (Snowflake-managed: user @~, table @%t, named @my_stage) and external stages (your own S3/GCS/Azure bucket registered in Snowflake).

Q10. How do you bulk load data with COPY INTO? basic 
-- upload local file to internal stage
PUT file:///data/orders.csv @my_stage;

-- load staged files into a table
COPY INTO orders
FROM @my_stage
FILE_FORMAT = (TYPE = CSV SKIP_HEADER = 1)
ON_ERROR = 'CONTINUE';

COPY INTO bulk-loads files from a stage, tracks which files were loaded (so re-runs skip them for ~64 days), and can validate/skip errors.

Q11. What is a file format object and why use one? basic

A file format is a reusable, named definition of how to parse files — type (CSV/JSON/PARQUET/AVRO/ORC/XML), delimiter, compression, header handling, null strings, date formats, etc. You create it once (CREATE FILE FORMAT ...) and reference it in stages and COPY INTO, keeping loads consistent and avoiding repeating parsing options everywhere.

Q12. What is Snowpipe and how does it differ from COPY INTO? mid

Snowpipe is continuous, event-driven micro-batch loading: as new files land in a stage (often via cloud notifications like S3 events), Snowpipe automatically runs a COPY to load them within a minute or two — no manual/scheduled COPY needed. It's serverless (billed per compute used, not a warehouse) and ideal for near-real-time ingestion. Plain COPY INTO is a one-shot bulk load you trigger yourself.

Q13. When would you choose Snowpipe vs a scheduled COPY vs Snowpipe Streaming? hard

Use a scheduled/bulk COPY for periodic batch files (e.g. hourly/daily dumps) where a warehouse is already running. Use Snowpipe for files arriving unpredictably and continuously where you want low-latency, serverless auto-loading. Use Snowpipe Streaming (row-level API) for true low-latency streaming ingestion of rows (e.g. from Kafka) without staging files at all. Choice = latency need + whether data is files or row streams.

Q14. What are micro-partitions? mid

Snowflake automatically stores table data in micro-partitions — small (~50–500 MB uncompressed) contiguous, columnar, immutable units, typically thousands per large table. For each micro-partition Snowflake keeps metadata (min/max values, distinct counts, nulls per column). You don't create or manage them; they're the physical foundation for pruning and are created transparently as you load.

Q15. What is partition pruning and why does it matter? mid

When a query filters (e.g. WHERE order_date = '2026-07-01'), Snowflake uses each micro-partition's min/max metadata to skip partitions that can't contain matching rows, scanning only relevant ones. Good pruning means reading a fraction of the data → faster, cheaper queries. Pruning is most effective when data is naturally or deliberately clustered on your filter columns.

Q16. What is a clustering key and when do you need one? hard

A clustering key defines how Snowflake co-locates data across micro-partitions (e.g. CLUSTER BY (order_date)) so pruning is more effective on that column. Data is naturally somewhat clustered by load order; you only add an explicit clustering key on very large tables (hundreds of GB+/TB) where queries filter on a column that isn't well-clustered and pruning is poor. Snowflake auto-reclusters in the background, which costs credits — so don't over-cluster small tables.

Q17. How do you check whether a table is well-clustered? hard

Use SYSTEM$CLUSTERING_INFORMATION('table', '(col)') to see clustering depth/overlap, and inspect the query profile: if a filtered query scans most partitions ("partitions scanned" ≈ "partitions total"), pruning is poor and a clustering key may help. SYSTEM$CLUSTERING_DEPTH gives an average overlap metric — lower is better.

Q18. What is time travel in Snowflake? basic

Time travel lets you query, clone, or restore data as it existed in the past, within a retention window (1 day standard, up to 90 days on Enterprise). You can SELECT ... AT (TIMESTAMP => ...) / BEFORE (STATEMENT => ...) / AT (OFFSET => -3600), UNDROP TABLE, or clone a past state — great for recovering from mistakes and auditing.

SELECT * FROM orders AT(OFFSET => -60*10); -- 10 min ago
UNDROP TABLE orders;
Q19. What is fail-safe and how does it differ from time travel? mid

Fail-safe is a 7-day period after time travel expires during which Snowflake retains data for disaster recovery only — you cannot query it; only Snowflake support can recover it. Time travel is self-service and queryable; fail-safe is a last-resort safety net. Both contribute to storage cost, which is why long retention on churny tables gets expensive.

Q20. What is zero-copy cloning? mid

CREATE TABLE t_clone CLONE t; creates an independent, writable copy instantly with no data duplication — the clone just references the same underlying micro-partitions via metadata. Storage is only consumed as the clone (or original) diverges (copy-on-write). It's perfect for spinning up full-size dev/test environments, taking cheap snapshots, and what-if analysis without paying to copy terabytes.

Q21. Give real uses of zero-copy cloning. mid

Clone prod into a dev/QA database to test a migration on real data safely; snapshot a table before a risky bulk update so you can restore; give each developer their own sandbox; create point-in-time copies for reproducible analytics; and combine with time travel (CLONE ... AT(TIMESTAMP => ...)) to recover a table's past state cheaply.

Q22. What is a stream in Snowflake? mid

A stream is a change-tracking object on a table (or view) that records row-level changes — inserts, updates, deletes — since it was last consumed (CDC). Querying the stream returns the changed rows plus metadata columns (METADATA$ACTION, METADATA$ISUPDATE). When you consume a stream in a DML statement (e.g. a MERGE), its offset advances, so you process each change exactly once. It's the foundation for incremental ELT.

Q23. What is a task and how do streams + tasks build a pipeline? mid

A task runs a SQL statement (or calls a procedure) on a schedule or when triggered, and tasks can be chained into a DAG (AFTER dependencies). Combine them: a stream captures new/changed rows in a raw table; a task runs periodically, checks SYSTEM$STREAM_HAS_DATA, and MERGEs the stream's changes into a curated table. That gives you an incremental, in-Snowflake ELT pipeline without external orchestration.

CREATE TASK load_curated
  WAREHOUSE = etl_wh SCHEDULE = '5 MINUTE'
  WHEN SYSTEM$STREAM_HAS_DATA('raw_stream')
AS
  MERGE INTO curated c USING raw_stream s
  ON c.id = s.id WHEN MATCHED THEN UPDATE SET *
  WHEN NOT MATCHED THEN INSERT *;
Q24. What are dynamic tables and how do they compare to streams+tasks? hard

A dynamic table is a declarative alternative: you write a SELECT defining the desired result and a TARGET_LAG (freshness, e.g. '5 minutes'), and Snowflake automatically and incrementally keeps it up to date — no manual streams, tasks, or MERGE. It's simpler to maintain for transformation pipelines. Streams+tasks give you more imperative control (custom logic, arbitrary DML), while dynamic tables trade control for a declarative, self-managing pipeline.

Q25. What is a materialized view and its limitations? mid

A materialized view stores precomputed query results and is automatically maintained as the base table changes, speeding up expensive repetitive queries (e.g. heavy aggregations). Limitations: it's built on a single table (no joins), supports a limited set of functions, incurs maintenance/storage cost, and is an Enterprise-edition feature. For multi-table precomputation, dynamic tables or scheduled tables are often better.

Q26. Explain Snowflake's caching layers. mid

Three levels: Result cache (cloud services) returns identical query results for ~24h without any compute if the underlying data hasn't changed — even on a suspended warehouse. Warehouse/local disk cache holds recently read micro-partition data on the warehouse's SSDs, speeding repeat scans while the warehouse is running (lost when it suspends). Metadata cache answers some queries (like counts/min/max) purely from metadata. These caches dramatically cut cost/latency for repeated work.

Q27. How does RBAC work in Snowflake? mid

Privileges are granted to roles, and roles are granted to users (and to other roles, forming a hierarchy) — users never get privileges directly. Example: GRANT SELECT ON TABLE db.sch.t TO ROLE analyst; GRANT ROLE analyst TO USER deepesh;. System roles include ACCOUNTADMIN (top), SECURITYADMIN (manages grants/roles), USERADMIN (users/roles), SYSADMIN (owns objects/warehouses), and PUBLIC. Best practice: build custom functional roles under SYSADMIN and limit ACCOUNTADMIN use.

Q28. What is the VARIANT data type? mid

VARIANT is a semi-structured column type that stores JSON, Avro, ORC, Parquet, or XML values (objects, arrays, mixed types) in one column. Snowflake stores it in an optimized columnar form and lets you query nested fields with SQL using dot/bracket/colon notation — so you can load raw JSON without a fixed schema and still query it efficiently. This is a big reason fintech/SaaS teams (with lots of JSON events/APIs) like Snowflake.

Q29. How do you query nested JSON in a VARIANT column? mid 
-- payload is a VARIANT holding {"user":{"id":7},"tags":["a","b"]}
SELECT
  payload:user.id::int      AS user_id,
  payload:user:id::int      AS user_id_alt,
  t.value::string           AS tag
FROM events,
LATERAL FLATTEN(input => payload:tags) t;

Use col:path to navigate, ::type to cast, and LATERAL FLATTEN to explode arrays into rows.

Q30. What does FLATTEN do? mid

FLATTEN is a table function that "explodes" an array or object in a VARIANT into one row per element (like Spark's explode). Used with LATERAL, it joins those exploded rows back to the parent row, giving output columns like value, index, and key. It's how you turn nested arrays (e.g. a list of line items in a JSON order) into a normal tabular result.

Q31. What is Snowpark? mid

Snowpark is a DataFrame-style API (Python, Java, Scala) that lets you write transformation and ML logic in code that runs inside Snowflake's compute, pushing operations down to warehouses instead of pulling data out. It also supports UDFs and stored procedures in Python. It's Snowflake's answer for Python/data-engineering/ML workloads (somewhat analogous to Spark DataFrames), so you're not limited to pure SQL.

Q32. Snowflake vs Databricks — how do you frame the difference? mid

Snowflake is a SQL-first cloud data warehouse: near-zero admin, elastic warehouses, excellent for analytics/BI and SQL/ELT, with Snowpark for Python. Databricks is a Spark-based lakehouse: strong for large-scale data engineering, Python/Scala, streaming, and ML, with open Delta storage. Snowflake favors simplicity and SQL; Databricks favors flexibility and code/ML. Many companies use both (Databricks/Spark for heavy engineering + ML, Snowflake for the warehouse/BI). Pick based on team skills and workload.

Q33. How is data stored physically — do you manage indexes? mid

No traditional indexes. Data is stored columnar and compressed in immutable micro-partitions with automatic min/max metadata; the optimizer uses that metadata for pruning instead of indexes. There's no VACUUM, no manual statistics, no index tuning — you manage clustering only on very large tables. This "no-knobs" storage is a core simplicity selling point.

Q34. What are the main levers for controlling Snowflake cost? hard

1) Right-size warehouses to the workload (don't run L when S suffices). 2) Short auto-suspend (e.g. 60s) so idle compute stops. 3) Use multi-cluster only for real concurrency needs. 4) Separate warehouses per workload to isolate/attribute cost. 5) Set resource monitors with credit quotas and alerts/suspend actions. 6) Improve pruning (clustering) and reuse the result cache. 7) Manage time-travel retention on churny tables to cut storage. 8) Use serverless features (Snowpipe/tasks) judiciously and monitor with ACCOUNT_USAGE views.

Q35. What is a resource monitor? mid

A resource monitor tracks credit consumption against a quota over an interval (e.g. monthly) for the account or specific warehouses, and can trigger actions at thresholds — notify, suspend (after running queries finish), or suspend immediately. It's the guardrail against runaway credit usage. Only ACCOUNTADMIN manages them, and you attach them to warehouses.

Q36. What is the query profile and what do you look for? hard

The Query Profile (in the UI / GET_QUERY_OPERATOR_STATS) is the execution plan with per-operator stats. Look for: partitions scanned vs total (poor pruning if nearly all), bytes spilled to local/remote storage (warehouse too small / memory pressure), a huge join explosion or exploding row counts, expensive window/sort operators, and time spent in remote I/O. It's the primary tool for diagnosing slow queries.

Q37. Scenario: a query is suddenly slow. How do you diagnose it? hard

1) Open the query profile: check pruning (partitions scanned/total), spilling to remote storage (undersized warehouse), and skew. 2) Check if the result cache was missed because data changed. 3) See if the warehouse was queued (concurrency) — a case for multi-cluster. 4) Look for exploding joins (missing/duplicate keys) or a filter that no longer prunes (bad clustering). 5) Fixes: right-size/scale up the warehouse to stop spilling, add/adjust a clustering key for pruning, rewrite the join, or add multi-cluster for concurrency. Confirm with a re-run.

Q38. Scenario: your monthly credits are being burned fast ("runaway usage"). What do you do? hard

1) Query ACCOUNT_USAGE.WAREHOUSE_METERING_HISTORY / query history to find the top warehouses and queries by credits. 2) Common causes: warehouses with no/long auto-suspend sitting idle, oversized warehouses, a runaway task/Snowpipe, or expensive recurring queries. 3) Immediate: set short auto-suspend, right-size, and attach a resource monitor to cap/suspend. 4) Optimize the heavy queries (pruning, caching, materialized/dynamic tables). 5) Split workloads onto separate warehouses so you can attribute and control cost. 6) Alert on future spikes.

Q39. Scenario: you must dedupe rows during/after a load. How? hard

Load into a staging table, then dedupe with a window function keeping the latest per key:

INSERT INTO clean
SELECT * EXCLUDE rn FROM (
  SELECT s.*, ROW_NUMBER() OVER (
    PARTITION BY id ORDER BY updated_at DESC) AS rn
  FROM staging s)
WHERE rn = 1;

Or MERGE from a deduped source into the target on the key. Also ensure COPY INTO's load-history prevents re-loading the same files, and consider a stream so you only process new rows. Enforce uniqueness in the transform, since Snowflake doesn't enforce PRIMARY KEY constraints.

Q40. Are PRIMARY KEY / UNIQUE constraints enforced in Snowflake? mid

No — PRIMARY KEY, UNIQUE, and FOREIGN KEY constraints are informational only (except NOT NULL, which is enforced). They document intent and help the optimizer and BI tools, but Snowflake won't stop you inserting duplicates. So you must enforce uniqueness yourself in your ELT logic (dedupe/MERGE), which is a common interview gotcha.

Q41. How does COPY INTO avoid loading the same file twice? mid

COPY INTO records load metadata (file name + a hash/ETag) in the table's load history for ~64 days and, by default, skips files it has already loaded. You can override with FORCE = TRUE to reload. This gives idempotent bulk loads. For validation, COPY ... VALIDATION_MODE = 'RETURN_ERRORS' checks files without loading.

Q42. What is a transient table and when do you use it? mid

A transient table is like a permanent table but with no fail-safe and minimal time travel (0–1 day), so it costs less to store. Use it for staging/intermediate/ELT scratch data that you can recreate and don't need long-term recovery for. Temporary tables go further — they exist only for the session. Choosing transient for staging is a simple cost optimization.

Q43. What are external tables in Snowflake? mid

An external table lets you query files that stay in your external stage (S3/GCS/Azure) as if they were a table, without loading them into Snowflake storage — Snowflake reads them on the fly using metadata about partitions. Useful for querying a data lake directly or cheaply accessing rarely-used data. Queries are generally slower than native tables; pair with materialized views over the external table for speed.

Q44. What is data sharing / the Marketplace? mid

Secure Data Sharing lets you grant another Snowflake account live, read-only access to your data with no copying — the consumer queries it with their own compute against your storage (via metadata pointers). The Snowflake Marketplace uses this to distribute third-party datasets. It's great for sharing curated data with partners/customers without ETL exports, and access is instantly revocable.

Q45. How does Snowflake handle concurrency for many dashboard users? mid

Use a multi-cluster warehouse that scales out additional clusters as concurrent queries queue up, then scales back down when demand falls — keeping response times low during peaks without over-provisioning. Combined with the result cache (repeated identical dashboard queries served instantly) and separating BI onto its own warehouse, this handles bursty BI concurrency well.

Q46. What editions/features matter (Standard vs Enterprise)? mid

Standard covers core warehousing. Enterprise adds features DEs often need: up to 90-day time travel, materialized views, multi-cluster warehouses, and column-level security/masking. Business Critical adds stronger compliance/security (HIPAA/PCI, customer-managed keys) — relevant for fintech. Knowing that time travel > 1 day and multi-cluster require Enterprise is a common practical detail.

Q47. How would you build an incremental ELT pipeline entirely in Snowflake? hard

Land raw files via Snowpipe (or COPY) into a raw table. Put a stream on the raw table to capture new rows. Schedule a task (or a task DAG) that, WHEN SYSTEM$STREAM_HAS_DATA, MERGEs the changes into a curated/dimension table (handling dedupe/SCD), then downstream tasks build marts. Alternatively, use dynamic tables with a TARGET_LAG to declaratively keep curated tables fresh. Govern with roles, isolate on an ELT warehouse with auto-suspend, and monitor credits. This is a fully in-Snowflake, near-real-time ELT.

Q48. How does Snowflake achieve ACID transactions? hard

Snowflake supports ACID transactions with multi-statement BEGIN/COMMIT/ROLLBACK and snapshot isolation. Because micro-partitions are immutable, DML creates new micro-partitions and the Cloud Services layer atomically swaps metadata pointers on commit — readers always see a consistent snapshot, and an aborted transaction simply doesn't publish its new partitions. This metadata-based, copy-on-write approach is also what powers time travel and zero-copy cloning.

Q49. What does the free trial give you and how do you start practicing? basic

The 30-day free trial gives roughly $400 in credits and a full Standard/Enterprise account on your chosen cloud/region. Start by creating a warehouse (XS with 60s auto-suspend to save credits), a database and schema, then use a Worksheet to run SQL. Load a Kaggle CSV via a stage + COPY INTO, and try VARIANT/JSON, time travel, cloning, and a simple stream+task pipeline. It's enough to cover every interview topic hands-on.

Q50. Design question: fintech wants near-real-time transaction analytics with JSON events. Sketch a Snowflake design. hard

Ingest: stream transaction events (JSON) via Snowpipe Streaming (from Kafka) or Snowpipe from an S3 stage into a raw table with a VARIANT payload. Parse: a stream on the raw table + a task (or a dynamic table) uses FLATTEN and payload:field::type to shred JSON into typed columns and MERGE into a clean transactions table (deduped by txn_id). Curate: downstream tasks/dynamic tables build gold aggregates (fraud signals, per-account rollups). Serve: a multi-cluster BI warehouse for dashboards, using the result cache. Govern/cost: RBAC roles, column masking on PII, resource monitors, short auto-suspend, and a clustering key on high-volume tables for pruning. This covers ingestion latency, semi-structured handling, incremental ELT, concurrency, security, and cost — exactly the fintech concerns.

Git & Version Control

Git tracks every change to your code and lets teams collaborate without overwriting each other — every DE job assumes you know it.

What & Why

Git is a version control system: it takes snapshots (called commits) of your files so you can see history, undo mistakes, and work in parallel with others. Think of it as an infinite, labelled undo history for a whole folder (a repository or "repo"). GitHub (or GitLab/Bitbucket) is a website that hosts your repos in the cloud so teams share them — Git is the tool, GitHub is the hosting service.

Coming from production support, you already read logs and trace changes. Git is the same instinct applied to code: "who changed this transformation, when, and why?" Every commit has an author, timestamp, and message. When a pipeline breaks after a deploy, git log and git blame tell you exactly what changed.

For data engineers Git holds your SQL models, Python ETL scripts, Airflow DAGs, dbt projects, and config. Nothing goes to production without going through Git first — CI/CD (later section) triggers off Git. If you can only learn one DevOps tool first, learn Git. It is table stakes: interviewers assume it, and you will use it every single day.

The mental model: your work lives in three places — the working directory (files you edit), the staging area / index (changes you've marked ready with git add), and the repository (committed history). You move changes: edit → add → commit → push to the remote (GitHub).

Learning Checklist

Projects & Practice

  • Put every portfolio project on GitHub — each with a proper README (what it does, how to run, architecture diagram), a .gitignore, and clean commit history. Recruiters open your GitHub before your call; make it look like a working DE's repo, not a dump.
  • Mock PR review flow — create a feature branch, make a change, open a Pull Request to main, write a description, review your own diff, and merge with a squash. Do this 5+ times until the loop is muscle memory. Bonus: ask a friend to leave a review comment and address it.
  • Fix a bad commit history — deliberately make messy commits, then practice git rebase -i to squash/reword, and git revert to undo a pushed commit safely.
  • Contribute to open source — fork a small data project, fix a typo/docs issue, open a real PR. One merged external PR is strong signal on a resume.

Interview Q&A (25)

Q1. What is Git and how is it different from GitHub? basic

Git is a distributed version control tool that runs locally and records snapshots (commits) of your files. GitHub is a cloud service that hosts Git repositories and adds collaboration features (Pull Requests, issues, Actions). You can use Git with no GitHub at all; GitHub is one of several hosts (GitLab, Bitbucket are others).

Q2. How do you start a new repo and make your first commit? basic

Initialise, stage files, then commit.

git init
git add .
git commit -m "Initial commit"

init creates the hidden .git folder, add stages files into the index, commit saves the snapshot with a message.

Q3. What is the difference between the working directory, staging area, and repository? basic

Working directory = your actual files as you edit them. Staging area (index) = changes you've marked ready to commit with git add. Repository = the committed history in .git. The staging area lets you commit a curated subset of your changes rather than everything at once.

Q4. How do you clone a repo and connect a local repo to a remote? basic 
# clone an existing remote
git clone https://github.com/user/repo.git

# or add a remote to an existing local repo
git remote add origin https://github.com/user/repo.git
git push -u origin main

-u sets the upstream so future git push/git pull need no arguments.

Q5. What is a branch and how do you create/switch one? basic

A branch is a movable pointer to a line of commits — it lets you work on a feature without touching main.

git switch -c feature/add-dq-checks   # create + switch (modern)
git checkout -b feature/add-dq-checks # older equivalent
git switch main                        # go back
Q6. What is the difference between git merge and git rebase? mid

merge combines two branches and creates a merge commit, preserving the true history (branches visibly diverge and rejoin). rebase replays your branch's commits on top of another branch, producing a linear history as if you'd started from the latest code. Rule of thumb: rebase your local, unpushed feature branch to keep history clean; use merge for shared branches. Never rebase commits that others have already pulled.

Q7. Walk me through the Pull Request workflow. mid 
git switch -c feature/x
# ...make changes...
git add . && git commit -m "Add feature x"
git push -u origin feature/x

Then on GitHub: open a PR from feature/x into main, write a description, request reviewers. CI runs tests. Reviewers comment; you push fixes (the PR updates automatically). Once approved and green, you merge (squash/merge/rebase) and delete the branch. This is the core team loop — nothing merges to main without review.

Q8. How do you resolve a merge conflict? mid

Git marks the file with conflict markers:

<<<<<<< HEAD
your version
=======
their version
>>>>>>> feature/x

Edit the file to the correct final content, remove the markers, then git add file and git commit (or git rebase --continue). Use git status to list conflicted files and git merge --abort to bail out.

Q9. What goes in a .gitignore and why does it matter? basic

Patterns for files Git should never track: secrets (.env), large data (*.csv, data/), build junk (__pycache__/, .venv/), IDE files (.idea/). It prevents leaking credentials and bloating the repo.

.env
__pycache__/
*.parquet
.venv/
.DS_Store
Q10. You committed a secret (API key) — what do you do? hard

First: rotate the secret immediately — assume it's compromised the moment it hit a remote. Then remove it from history with git filter-repo (or BFG Repo-Cleaner), add the file to .gitignore, and force-push. Simply deleting it in a new commit is NOT enough — the old commit still contains it in history.

Q11. What is git stash and when do you use it? basic

Stash parks uncommitted changes so your working directory is clean — useful when you must switch branches urgently but aren't ready to commit.

git stash            # save & clear working changes
git stash list
git stash pop         # reapply the most recent stash
Q12. How do you see history and what changed? basic 
git log --oneline --graph --all   # compact history
git diff                          # unstaged changes
git diff --staged                 # staged changes
git blame path/to/file.py         # who changed each line

git show <commit> displays a single commit's diff.

Q13. Difference between git reset, git revert, and git checkout/restore? hard

reset moves the branch pointer back (rewrites history) — --soft keeps changes staged, --mixed (default) keeps them unstaged, --hard discards them. revert creates a new commit that undoes a previous one (safe for shared/pushed history). restore/checkout <file> discards changes to specific files in the working directory. Rule: on pushed commits, use revert, never reset --hard.

Q14. How do you undo the last commit but keep your changes? mid 
git reset --soft HEAD~1   # undo commit, changes stay staged
git reset HEAD~1          # undo commit, changes unstaged
git reset --hard HEAD~1   # undo commit AND discard changes (careful!)
Q15. What are tags and how do releases work? mid

Tags mark a specific commit as a named point, usually a version. Annotated tags carry a message and are what you use for releases.

git tag -a v1.0.0 -m "First release"
git push origin v1.0.0

On GitHub a tag becomes a Release; CI/CD often deploys on a tag push. Follow SemVer: MAJOR.MINOR.PATCH.

Q16. What is HEAD? mid

HEAD is a pointer to your current position — normally the tip of the branch you're on. HEAD~1 means one commit before HEAD. A "detached HEAD" means you've checked out a specific commit rather than a branch; commits made there can be lost unless you create a branch.

Q17. What is the difference between fetch, pull, and push? mid

fetch downloads remote changes but does NOT merge them into your working branch. pull = fetch + merge (or --rebase) into your current branch. push uploads your local commits to the remote. Fetch first when you want to inspect before integrating.

Q18. Explain GitHub Flow vs trunk-based development. hard

GitHub Flow: main is always deployable; create a short-lived feature branch, open a PR, review, merge, deploy. Simple and common. Trunk-based: everyone commits to main (trunk) with very short-lived branches (hours), heavy use of feature flags and CI to keep trunk releasable. Trunk-based scales well with strong CI and reduces long-lived merge pain. Git Flow (with develop/release/hotfix branches) is older and heavier — mostly avoided for continuously-deployed data pipelines.

Q19. How do you handle Jupyter notebooks in Git? hard

Raw .ipynb files are JSON with embedded outputs, so diffs are noisy and merge conflicts are painful. Options: (1) nbstripout as a git filter to strip outputs before commit; (2) jupytext to pair notebooks with a plain .py that Git diffs cleanly; (3) code review the paired .py. Never commit notebooks with large outputs or data embedded.

Q20. What is Git LFS and when do data teams need it? mid

Git Large File Storage replaces big binary files (models, sample datasets, images) with small pointers, storing the real files separately. Git repos are meant for text/code; committing large binaries bloats every clone forever. That said, most data belongs in object storage (S3/ADLS), not Git — use LFS only for necessary binaries.

Q21. What makes a good commit message? basic

Imperative mood, a concise subject (<50 chars) that says what and why, optional body explaining reasoning. Example: Fix null handling in revenue aggregation beats fixed stuff. Conventional Commits (feat:, fix:, chore:) help automate changelogs.

Q22. How do you squash multiple commits into one? hard 
git rebase -i HEAD~3

In the editor, change pick to squash (or s) on the commits you want folded into the one above, save, then write the combined message. Alternatively, use "Squash and merge" in the GitHub PR UI to keep main history clean.

Q23. What is cherry-pick and when is it useful? mid

git cherry-pick <commit> applies a single commit from one branch onto your current branch. Useful for hotfixes: you fixed a bug on a feature branch and need just that fix on main without merging everything else.

Q24. Your teammate force-pushed and now your branch is broken — what happened and how do you recover? hard

They rewrote shared history (rebased/reset then push --force), so your local commits reference commits that no longer exist upstream. Recover with git fetch then git reset --hard origin/branch if you have no local-only work, or git rebase --onto to replay your unique commits. Prevention: use --force-with-lease, and never force-push shared branches.

Q25. How would you structure Git for a data engineering project? hard

One repo per pipeline/project (or a monorepo for a platform). Directories like src/, dags/, sql/ or models/ (dbt), tests/, ci/. A strong .gitignore, a README, pinned dependencies (requirements.txt/pyproject.toml), and CI config. Protect main with required PR reviews and passing checks. Data itself lives in object storage, never in Git; only code, config, and schema live in the repo.

Docker & Containers

Docker packages your code plus its exact environment into a portable container so "it works on my machine" becomes "it works everywhere" — the backbone of running Kafka, Airflow, and Spark locally.

What & Why

A container is a lightweight, isolated box that holds your application together with everything it needs to run — the right Python version, libraries, OS packages, and config. You build it once and it runs identically on your laptop, a colleague's machine, or a cloud server. Docker is the most popular tool for building and running containers.

Containers vs Virtual Machines: a VM virtualises an entire operating system (its own kernel, gigabytes in size, slow to boot). A container shares the host's kernel and only packages the app and its dependencies — megabytes, starts in seconds. That efficiency is why containers won: you can run dozens on one machine.

Why DE needs Docker: data tools are painful to install natively. With Docker you spin up Kafka, Airflow, Spark, or Postgres locally with a couple of commands and throw them away when done — no polluting your machine. It gives reproducible pipelines: the container that runs your ETL in testing is byte-for-byte the one that runs in production, so environment drift disappears. Airflow, Spark, dbt, and every cloud data platform ship as containers.

Mental model: a Dockerfile is a recipe. You build it into an image (a frozen template). You run an image to get a live container (a running instance). One image → many containers, like a class → many objects.

Learning Checklist

Projects & Practice

  • Dockerize your Python ETL script — write a Dockerfile that installs deps from requirements.txt, copies your script, and runs it. Build, run, and confirm it produces the same output as running locally. This is the single most useful Docker exercise for a DE.
  • Run Kafka + Airflow locally with docker-compose — use the official Airflow docker-compose.yaml and a Kafka image, bring the whole stack up with docker compose up, produce/consume a message, and trigger a DAG. Proves you can stand up real data infrastructure.
  • Postgres + your pipeline in one compose file — spin up Postgres as a source/sink and your ETL container together, wire them on a shared network, and load data end-to-end.
  • Shrink an image — take a fat image and rewrite it with a slim base (python:3.12-slim) and a multi-stage build; measure the size drop with docker images.

Interview Q&A (25)

Q1. What is a container and how does it differ from a VM? basic

A container packages an app plus its dependencies and shares the host OS kernel, making it lightweight (MBs) and fast to start (seconds). A VM virtualises a full OS including its own kernel, making it heavy (GBs) and slow. Containers give better density and reproducibility for the same hardware.

Q2. What is the difference between an image and a container? basic

An image is a read-only template (built from a Dockerfile). A container is a running instance of an image with a writable layer on top. One image can produce many containers — like a class and its objects.

Q3. Explain the key Dockerfile instructions. mid 
FROM python:3.12-slim      # base image
WORKDIR /app               # set working dir
COPY requirements.txt .    # copy files in
RUN pip install -r requirements.txt  # run build step
COPY . .
CMD ["python", "etl.py"]   # default command

FROM picks the base, COPY adds files, RUN executes commands at build time (layers), CMD/ENTRYPOINT define what runs when the container starts.

Q4. What is the difference between CMD and ENTRYPOINT? hard

ENTRYPOINT sets the fixed executable that always runs; CMD supplies default arguments that are easy to override at docker run. Common pattern: ENTRYPOINT ["python", "etl.py"] and CMD ["--date=today"] — running with an argument overrides the CMD but keeps the ENTRYPOINT. If you only use CMD, the whole command is overridable.

Q5. How do you build and run an image? basic 
docker build -t my-etl:latest .
docker run --rm my-etl:latest

-t tags the image, . is the build context (folder with the Dockerfile), --rm auto-deletes the container when it exits.

Q6. How do you inspect a running container's logs and shell into it? basic 
docker ps                 # list running containers
docker logs <id>          # view stdout/stderr
docker exec -it <id> bash # open a shell inside it

-it gives an interactive terminal. This is your RCA toolkit inside a container.

Q7. What is port mapping and why do you need it? mid

Containers are network-isolated, so a service listening on a port inside isn't reachable from your host by default. -p 8080:8080 maps host port 8080 to container port 8080, so you can open the Airflow UI in your browser. Format is -p host:container.

Q8. What are volumes and why are they important for data? mid

A container's filesystem is ephemeral — remove the container and its data is gone. Volumes persist data outside the container lifecycle. -v mydata:/var/lib/postgresql/data keeps a database's data across restarts; a bind mount -v $(pwd):/app maps your local code into the container for live editing. Essential for stateful services like Postgres/Kafka.

Q9. How do containers communicate with each other? mid

Put them on the same Docker network; then they resolve each other by container/service name as a hostname. In compose, a service named postgres is reachable at host postgres on its internal port. This is why your app connects to kafka:9092, not localhost, inside compose.

Q10. What is docker-compose and when do you use it? mid

Compose defines a multi-container application in one YAML file — services, networks, volumes, env — and starts it all with one command.

services:
  postgres:
    image: postgres:16
    environment:
      POSTGRES_PASSWORD: pass
  etl:
    build: .
    depends_on: [postgres]
docker compose up -d
docker compose down

Perfect for local dev stacks (Airflow + Postgres + Kafka).

Q11. How do you pass environment variables and handle secrets? hard 
docker run -e DB_HOST=postgres --env-file .env my-etl

Use -e or --env-file for config. Never bake secrets into the image with ENV or COPY — they persist in image layers and leak. In production use a secrets manager (Vault, AWS Secrets Manager) or Docker/K8s secrets injected at runtime.

Q12. What are image layers and how does build caching work? hard

Each Dockerfile instruction creates a cached layer. On rebuild, Docker reuses cached layers until it hits a changed instruction, then rebuilds everything after. So order matters: copy requirements.txt and pip install before copying your source code — that way changing your code doesn't re-run the slow dependency install.

Q13. Why put pip install before COPY . .? mid

To exploit layer caching. Dependencies change rarely; source code changes constantly. If you copy code first, every code change invalidates the dependency layer and reinstalls everything (slow). Copying requirements and installing first means code changes only rebuild the fast final layers.

Q14. What is a multi-stage build and why use it? hard

Multi-stage builds use multiple FROM statements so build-time tools stay out of the final image.

FROM python:3.12 AS build
RUN pip install --target=/deps -r requirements.txt

FROM python:3.12-slim
COPY --from=build /deps /usr/lib/python3.12/site-packages
COPY . .
CMD ["python","etl.py"]

Result: a small, secure runtime image without compilers or caches.

Q15. What is a registry and how do you push an image? mid

A registry stores and distributes images — Docker Hub (public), or cloud registries ECR (AWS), ACR (Azure), GCR/Artifact Registry (GCP).

docker tag my-etl:latest myrepo/my-etl:1.0
docker push myrepo/my-etl:1.0

CI builds and pushes images; production pulls them by tag.

Q16. How do you reduce Docker image size? hard

Use a slim/alpine base (python:3.12-slim), multi-stage builds, combine RUN steps and clean caches (rm -rf /var/lib/apt/lists/*), add a .dockerignore to exclude data/venv, and avoid installing dev tools in the final stage. Smaller images pull faster and have a smaller attack surface.

Q17. What is a .dockerignore? basic

Like .gitignore but for the build context — it stops files (data, .venv, .git, secrets) from being sent to the Docker daemon and copied into the image. Keeps builds fast and images clean.

Q18. How do you clean up unused Docker resources? basic 
docker ps -a          # list all containers
docker rm <id>         # remove a container
docker rmi <image>     # remove an image
docker system prune -a  # remove all unused images/containers/networks

Docker accumulates disk usage fast; prune periodically.

Q19. Your container exits immediately — how do you debug it? hard

Check docker logs <id> for the error, and docker ps -a for the exit code. A container runs only as long as its main process; if the CMD finishes or crashes, it exits. Common causes: the CMD is a one-shot script (expected), a missing dependency, or a bad path. To poke around, override the command: docker run -it my-image bash.

Q20. How would you run Airflow locally with Docker? mid

Use the official Airflow docker-compose.yaml (from the Apache Airflow docs). It defines the webserver, scheduler, a Postgres metadata DB, and a Redis/executor. Run docker compose up airflow-init then docker compose up, and open localhost:8080. Mount your dags/ folder as a volume so DAGs appear without rebuilding.

Q21. What is the difference between docker run and docker start? mid

docker run creates a NEW container from an image and starts it. docker start restarts an EXISTING stopped container, preserving its state and writable layer. Repeated run without --rm leaves many stopped containers behind.

Q22. What is Kubernetes and how does it relate to Docker? hard

Docker builds and runs individual containers. Kubernetes (K8s) is an orchestrator that runs containers across many machines: it schedules them, restarts failures, scales replicas, handles networking and rolling deploys. Docker = one house; K8s = managing a whole city of them. As a DE you'll meet K8s via managed platforms (EKS, GKE, Databricks) rather than administering it yourself early on.

Q23. What are a Pod, Deployment, and Service in Kubernetes (overview)? hard

A Pod is the smallest unit — one or more tightly-coupled containers. A Deployment manages a set of identical Pods, handling scaling and rolling updates. A Service gives a stable network name/IP to reach those Pods even as they come and go. You don't need deep K8s early, but recognising these terms in a design discussion helps.

Q24. How does Docker help reproducibility in data pipelines? mid

The image pins the exact Python version, library versions, and OS packages. The same image runs in CI, staging, and production, so "works on my machine" and version-drift bugs disappear. Combined with pinned requirements.txt, a pipeline behaves identically everywhere — critical when a job must produce the same output every run.

Q25. What are container health checks and why do they matter? hard

A HEALTHCHECK instruction (or compose healthcheck) runs a command periodically to report whether the service is truly ready — e.g. Postgres accepting connections. Orchestrators use it to gate depends_on, restart unhealthy containers, and route traffic only to healthy ones. Without it, a dependent service might start before its database is actually accepting connections, causing flaky pipeline startups.

CI/CD for Data Engineering

CI/CD automatically tests your pipeline code on every change and deploys it safely — it's how professional data teams ship without breaking production.

What & Why

CI = Continuous Integration: every time you push code or open a PR, an automated system checks out your code, installs dependencies, runs linters and tests, and reports pass/fail. It catches broken code before it reaches anyone else. CD = Continuous Delivery/Deployment: once tests pass, the same automation packages and ships your code to an environment (dev, staging, prod) — either at the push of a button (delivery) or fully automatically (deployment).

Think of it as a robot reviewer and deployer. You stop manually copying notebooks to Databricks or clicking "publish" in ADF. Instead, merging a PR triggers a workflow that lints, tests, and deploys. This removes human error — the #1 cause of the production incidents you handle today.

Why data teams need it: pipeline code (SQL transforms, Python jobs, dbt models) is code, and untested code that touches production data corrupts reports and dashboards silently. CI runs pytest on your transformations and data tests on sample data so a bad change is blocked at the PR. CD promotes the exact same tested artifact through dev → staging → prod, giving you repeatable, auditable deploys and easy rollback.

The most common tool you'll meet is GitHub Actions — YAML workflow files in .github/workflows/ that GitHub runs on triggers like push and pull_request. GitLab CI and Azure Pipelines are equivalents.

Learning Checklist

Projects & Practice

  • Add a CI workflow to a portfolio repo — write .github/workflows/ci.yml that on every PR: installs deps, runs ruff + black --check, and runs pytest. Watch the green check appear on your PR. This alone signals "I work like a professional."
  • Auto-deploy on merge — extend the workflow so merging to main deploys a Databricks notebook/job via a Databricks Asset Bundle (databricks bundle deploy), authenticating with a GitHub Secret. Even a stub deploy demonstrates the full CI→CD loop.
  • Break it on purpose — push code with a failing test and a lint error; confirm the PR check goes red and blocks merge. Then fix and watch it go green. Understanding failure is the point.
  • dbt CI — set up a workflow that runs dbt build against a dev schema on PR so model + data tests run automatically.

Interview Q&A (25)

Q1. What is CI/CD in plain terms? basic

CI (Continuous Integration) automatically builds and tests code on every change so bugs are caught early. CD (Continuous Delivery/Deployment) automatically packages and ships the tested code to environments. Together they replace manual, error-prone deploys with a repeatable pipeline.

Q2. What is the difference between Continuous Delivery and Continuous Deployment? mid

Continuous Delivery: every change is automatically built, tested, and made ready to deploy, but a human clicks the button to release to production. Continuous Deployment: passing changes go all the way to production automatically with no manual gate. Data teams often use Delivery for prod (manual approval) and full Deployment for dev/staging.

Q3. What is a GitHub Actions workflow? basic

A YAML file in .github/workflows/ that GitHub runs automatically on events. It has triggers (on), one or more jobs, and each job has steps.

name: CI
on: [pull_request]
jobs:
  test:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    steps:
      - uses: actions/checkout@v4
      - run: pip install -r requirements.txt
      - run: pytest
Q4. What are triggers and which are common for data pipelines? basic

Triggers (the on: key) fire the workflow. Common ones: pull_request (run tests before merge), push to main (deploy), schedule (cron for nightly checks), and workflow_dispatch (manual button). Example: on: {push: {branches: [main]}}.

Q5. What is the difference between a job and a step? mid

A job runs on its own fresh runner (VM) and can run in parallel with other jobs. Steps run sequentially inside a job, sharing the same filesystem. You'd split lint and deploy into separate jobs (deploy needs: [lint, test]) so deploy only runs after checks pass.

Q6. How do you handle secrets in GitHub Actions? mid

Store them in repo/org Settings → Secrets, then reference them — never hardcode.

steps:
  - run: databricks bundle deploy
    env:
      DATABRICKS_TOKEN: ${{ secrets.DATABRICKS_TOKEN }}

Secrets are masked in logs. For cloud, prefer OIDC federation over long-lived tokens where possible.

Q7. How do you test data transformations in CI? mid

Write pytest tests that run your transform on small, known input and assert the output.

def test_dedup():
    df = transform(sample_df)
    assert df["id"].is_unique
    assert df["amount"].notna().all()

Use fixed sample data (fixtures), not production data, so tests are deterministic. Add data tests (schema, null/range checks) too.

Q8. What is linting and which tools do data engineers use? basic

Linting statically checks code for style and errors before it runs. Python: ruff (fast linter) and black (auto-formatter). SQL: sqlfluff. In CI: ruff check ., black --check ., sqlfluff lint models/. Enforcing them keeps a team's code consistent and catches bugs cheaply.

Q9. What is environment promotion (dev/staging/prod)? mid

Code and config flow through separate environments: dev (build/experiment), staging (production-like validation with realistic data), prod (live). The same tested artifact is promoted forward rather than rebuilt, so what you validated is exactly what runs live. Each environment has its own credentials and data, isolated from the others.

Q10. How do you deploy to Databricks in CI/CD? hard

Use Databricks Asset Bundles (DABs): a databricks.yml declares your notebooks, jobs, and cluster config as code, with per-target settings for dev/prod.

- run: databricks bundle validate
- run: databricks bundle deploy -t prod
  env:
    DATABRICKS_HOST: ${{ secrets.DATABRICKS_HOST }}
    DATABRICKS_TOKEN: ${{ secrets.DATABRICKS_TOKEN }}

CI validates on PR; merging to main deploys the bundle to the prod workspace.

Q11. How do you deploy Azure Data Factory pipelines? hard

ADF pipelines are defined as JSON/ARM templates. The pattern: develop in a collaboration Git branch, "publish" to generate ARM templates in an adf_publish branch, then deploy with the az CLI or a deployment task: az deployment group create --template-file ARMTemplateForFactory.json --parameters .... Parameters swap linked-service connection strings per environment (dev/prod).

Q12. How does dbt fit into CI? hard

On each PR, CI runs dbt build (which runs models + tests) against a temporary/dev schema. "Slim CI" uses dbt build --select state:modified+ --defer with a stored manifest so only changed models and their children run, saving warehouse cost. Failing data tests block the merge — bad SQL never reaches prod.

Q13. What is a rollback strategy and how do you implement one? hard

A rollback returns production to the last-known-good version quickly. Implement by deploying versioned, immutable artifacts (tagged images/bundles): to roll back, redeploy the previous tag. For data, keep the ability to re-point to a prior table version (e.g. Delta time travel / snapshots). Always test the rollback path, not just the deploy path.

Q14. How do you cache dependencies to speed up CI? mid 
- uses: actions/setup-python@v5
  with:
    python-version: '3.12'
    cache: 'pip'

Caching pip/uv downloads (or Docker layers) avoids re-downloading packages every run, cutting CI time from minutes to seconds. Cache keys are based on the lockfile hash so a dependency change invalidates the cache.

Q15. What is a matrix build? mid

A matrix runs the same job across combinations of parameters in parallel — e.g. multiple Python versions.

strategy:
  matrix:
    python-version: ['3.10','3.11','3.12']

Useful to ensure a pipeline library works across the versions your platforms use.

Q16. How do you prevent broken code from reaching main? mid

Enable branch protection on main: require a passing CI check and at least one approving review before merge. The CI workflow runs on pull_request; if lint/tests fail the merge button is disabled. This turns your test suite into an enforced gate rather than an optional courtesy.

Q17. Why version pipeline code and how do tags help deploys? mid

Versioning gives every deploy a precise identity, so you know exactly what's in prod and can reproduce or roll back. A common pattern triggers deployment on a version tag: on: {push: {tags: ['v*']}}. The tag ties the deployed artifact to a specific commit, giving an audit trail.

Q18. What is the difference between build-time and run-time in a pipeline deploy? hard

Build-time: CI compiles/packages the code, resolves dependencies, and produces an artifact (image, wheel, bundle) — done once. Run-time: the scheduler (Airflow/Databricks Jobs) executes that artifact against real data, injecting environment-specific config and secrets. Keeping config out of the build lets one artifact run in any environment.

Q19. How would you test that a pipeline is idempotent in CI? hard

Run the transform twice on the same input in a test and assert the output is identical and no duplicates appear (e.g. run a MERGE/upsert twice, check row counts and uniqueness are stable). Idempotency tests protect against the classic "re-run doubled the data" incident.

Q20. What are self-hosted runners and when do you need them? hard

GitHub-hosted runners are ephemeral cloud VMs. Self-hosted runners are machines you provide, used when jobs need private network access (an on-prem database or a VNet-locked warehouse), special hardware, or licensed software. Data teams use them to reach systems behind a corporate firewall.

Q21. How do you deploy Airflow DAGs via CI/CD? mid

Store DAGs in Git; CI lints and dry-parses them (python -c "import dag" or airflow dags list in a container) to catch import errors. CD then syncs the dags/ folder to the Airflow environment — e.g. sync to the S3/GCS bucket that MWAA/Composer reads, or bake DAGs into the Airflow image. A broken DAG should fail CI, not the scheduler.

Q22. What is a pre-commit hook and how does it complement CI? mid

pre-commit runs checks (ruff, black, trailing-whitespace, secret scanning) locally before a commit is created, catching issues in seconds instead of waiting for CI. It complements CI: developers get instant feedback, CI enforces the same checks so nothing slips through if someone skips the hook.

Q23. How do you handle database migrations in CI/CD? hard

Keep schema changes as versioned, ordered migration scripts (Alembic, Flyway, Liquibase). CI validates they apply cleanly to a fresh DB; CD applies pending migrations to each environment before deploying the code that depends on them. Make migrations backward-compatible (expand-then-contract) so old and new code can run during a rolling deploy.

Q24. What metrics indicate a healthy CI/CD process? hard

The DORA metrics: deployment frequency (how often you ship), lead time for changes (commit → prod), change failure rate (% of deploys causing incidents), and mean time to recovery (how fast you fix a bad deploy). Fast pipelines, low failure rate, and quick recovery indicate maturity.

Q25. Design a CI/CD pipeline for a dbt + Airflow data platform. hard

On PR: lint (sqlfluff, ruff), parse DAGs, run dbt build (modified models + tests) against a CI schema, run pytest on Python transforms. On merge to main: deploy DAGs to the Airflow bucket/image, run dbt run against prod (or promote the compiled artifact), tag a release. Guards: branch protection requiring green checks + review; secrets via GitHub Secrets/OIDC; environment-specific configs; a manual approval gate before prod and a documented rollback (redeploy previous tag).

Data Quality & Reliability

Making data trustworthy — validation, reconciliation, monitoring, and incident RCA. This is your Jio superpower repackaged as interview gold.

What & Why

Data quality (DQ) is the discipline of ensuring data is correct, complete, and timely so people can trust the numbers. Data reliability extends that to making pipelines dependable: they run on time, don't lose or duplicate records, and alert humans when something's wrong. A pipeline that moves bad data fast is worse than no pipeline — it produces confidently wrong dashboards.

This is where your existing Jio experience is a direct advantage. Production support, validation, reconciliation, and root cause analysis (RCA) are exactly the muscles data teams pay for. When you say "I reconcile source-to-target counts, investigate mismatches, and write RCAs," a fintech interviewer hears "this person will protect our data." Reframe your current work in DE language and it becomes some of your strongest interview material.

In a data platform, DQ checks sit at ingestion (is the source file complete?), after transformation (do totals reconcile?), and before serving (is the dashboard table fresh and unique?). Reliability adds SLAs (data must land by 6am), freshness monitoring, idempotent pipelines, safe backfills, and alerting that tells the on-call the right thing without drowning them in noise.

Modern teams codify this with tools like Great Expectations, Soda, or dbt tests for assertions, and data observability platforms (Monte Carlo, etc.) that auto-detect anomalies in volume, freshness, and schema. But the tools are secondary — the thinking (which checks, what tolerance, how to triage) is what you're selling.

Learning Checklist

Projects & Practice

  • Build a DQ check suite (Python + SQL) — for a portfolio pipeline, implement checks for row-count reconciliation, null-rate thresholds, uniqueness of a primary key, value ranges, and freshness. Emit a pass/fail report and fail the pipeline on critical breaches. Wire it into your CI.
  • Write an RCA doc template with a worked example — sections: summary, impact, timeline, detection, root cause (5 Whys), resolution, prevention/action items. Fill it with a realistic scenario (e.g. "revenue dropped 40% because an upstream schema change dropped a column"). This artifact proves you can run incidents — huge for a support-to-DE pivot.
  • Set up dbt tests + Great Expectations — add not_null, unique, accepted_values, and relationships tests to a dbt project, and a GE suite validating a raw file at ingestion.
  • Reconciliation harness — build a script that compares source vs warehouse counts and aggregate sums by partition, flags rows outside a tolerance, and lists the mismatched keys.

Interview Q&A (25)

Q1. What are the dimensions of data quality? basic

Completeness (no missing records/fields), accuracy (matches reality), consistency (same value across systems), timeliness/freshness (arrives on time), uniqueness (no unwanted duplicates), and validity (conforms to format/domain rules). A good DQ suite has at least one check per dimension on critical tables.

Q2. Write a SQL check for null rate on a critical column. basic 
SELECT
  COUNT(*) AS total,
  COUNT(customer_id) AS non_null,
  1 - COUNT(customer_id)*1.0/COUNT(*) AS null_rate
FROM orders;

Alert if null_rate exceeds a threshold (say 1%). Comparing today's rate to the trailing average catches spikes.

Q3. How do you detect duplicates in SQL? basic 
SELECT order_id, COUNT(*) AS n
FROM orders
GROUP BY order_id
HAVING COUNT(*) > 1;

If order_id is meant to be unique, any row here is a data-quality breach. For "duplicate events", group by the natural key + event timestamp.

Q4. Scenario: null rate on a key column spiked 3x overnight. Walk me through your response. hard

1) Contain: if downstream reports are affected, pause the publish/serving step. 2) Scope: is it all rows or a specific partition/source? Query null rate by source system and load hour. 3) Trace upstream: a spike usually means a producer change — schema change, a renamed field now landing NULL, a failed join, or a new source feeding bad data. Check recent deploys and lineage. 4) Root cause & fix: correct the mapping/join, backfill affected partitions. 5) Prevent: add a null-rate DQ gate and a data contract so producers can't silently drop the field.

Q5. What is reconciliation and how do you do source-to-target counts? mid

Reconciliation confirms the target holds what the source sent. The basic check compares counts (and key aggregates) per partition: source_count vs target_count per day. Beyond counts, reconcile a sum (e.g. total transaction amount) and, for stronger guarantees, a checksum/hash of key columns. Differences outside tolerance trigger investigation.

Q6. Scenario: revenue in the finance warehouse doesn't match the source system. How do you find the mismatch? hard

Reconcile top-down then narrow: compare total revenue, then by day, then by region/product until the discrepancy localises. Common causes: (a) timezone/date-boundary differences moving revenue across days, (b) currency conversion, (c) filters/de-dup dropping rows, (d) late-arriving records not yet loaded, (e) refunds handled differently, (f) a join fan-out double-counting. Identify the exact keys present in one side but not the other with an anti-join, then trace the transform that dropped/duplicated them.

Q7. What is a data contract? mid

A formal, versioned agreement between a data producer and consumers specifying schema, semantics, allowed values, freshness, and quality guarantees. It's enforced (validated at the boundary, CI-checked) so producers can't silently break downstream. It shifts DQ left — problems are rejected at the source instead of discovered in a dashboard.

Q8. What are Great Expectations, Soda, and dbt tests — and when do you use each? mid

dbt tests: SQL-based assertions co-located with models (not_null, unique, relationships, custom); best inside a dbt project. Great Expectations: a Python library for rich "expectations" with data docs; good for validating raw files/DataFrames at ingestion. Soda: check definitions in YAML (SodaCL) that run against warehouses, easy for analysts. Choose by where your transformation lives and who writes the checks.

Q9. What is data freshness and how do you monitor it with an SLA? mid

Freshness = how recent the data is. Monitor the max event/load timestamp per table and alert if the gap exceeds a threshold. An SLA states a promise ("orders table updated by 06:00 with data no older than 15 min"); the SLO is the measurable target, and you track error budget against it.

SELECT MAX(loaded_at), NOW() - MAX(loaded_at) AS staleness FROM orders;
Q10. What is idempotency and why does it matter in pipelines? hard

An idempotent operation produces the same result no matter how many times it runs. It matters because pipelines retry — on failure, a re-run must not double the data. Achieve it with deterministic partition overwrites (delete-then-insert a partition) or MERGE/upsert on a stable key, rather than blind append. Idempotency is what lets you safely retry and backfill.

Q11. Scenario: you're seeing duplicate events downstream. How do you fix it? hard

Confirm and quantify duplicates via GROUP BY on the natural key. Root causes: at-least-once delivery (Kafka/queue redelivery), retried loads without idempotency, or a fan-out join. Fixes: deduplicate on a stable event id (ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY event_id ORDER BY ...) = 1), make the sink upsert/MERGE on that id, and enforce a unique constraint. Long term, add exactly-once semantics or a dedup window keyed on event id.

Q12. What does exactly-once processing mean and is it truly achievable? hard

Exactly-once means each record affects the result once despite retries/failures. True end-to-end exactly-once is hard; in practice it's achieved as "effectively-once" = at-least-once delivery + idempotent writes (dedup on an event id or transactional/atomic sink commits). Frameworks (Kafka transactions, Spark Structured Streaming with idempotent sinks, Flink checkpoints) provide it within their boundaries.

Q13. Scenario: how do you safely backfill 6 months of data? hard

1) Make the pipeline idempotent (partition overwrite/MERGE) so re-runs don't duplicate. 2) Backfill by partition (per day/month), not one giant job, so you can resume on failure and limit blast radius. 3) Run into a shadow/staging table first, reconcile counts and sums against source, then swap. 4) Throttle to protect source and warehouse compute/cost. 5) Guard idempotency keys and watch for schema drift across the 6 months. 6) Validate each partition with DQ checks before promoting. 7) Communicate: downstream consumers may see numbers move as history is corrected.

Q14. Walk me through your incident RCA process. mid

Detect (alert or user report) → Triage & assess impact (who/what is affected, severity) → Mitigate (stop the bleeding: pause publish, roll back, serve last-good) → Diagnose root cause (logs, lineage, recent changes, 5 Whys) → Fix & backfill → Post-incident: write an RCA with timeline and action items, add a check/test so it can't recur silently. Blameless focus on the system, not the person.

Q15. What goes in a good RCA document? mid

Summary (one line), impact (tables/reports/users, duration, magnitude), detection (how you found out and how long that took), timeline (timestamped events), root cause (via 5 Whys — technical + process), resolution (what fixed it), and prevention/action items with owners and dates. The prevention section is the point: each incident should make the next one impossible or auto-detected.

Q16. What is the 5 Whys technique? basic

Ask "why?" repeatedly to move from symptom to root cause. E.g. Dashboard wrong → why? Table had nulls → why? Upstream dropped a column → why? Producer deployed a schema change → why? No data contract/CI check → why? DQ gating wasn't in place. The fix targets the deepest cause (add a contract), not just the symptom.

Q17. Scenario: late-arriving data keeps causing wrong daily totals. How do you handle it? hard

Distinguish event time from processing time and key aggregates on event time. Use a re-computation window: reprocess the last N days each run so late records land in the correct day (idempotent partition overwrite makes this safe). For streaming, use watermarks with allowed lateness. Mark data "provisional" until the window closes, and reconcile once late data settles.

Q18. What is data observability and how does it differ from testing? mid

Testing checks known assertions you write in advance (this column is unique). Observability continuously monitors data health across the "five pillars" — freshness, volume, schema, distribution, and lineage — and uses baselines/ML to auto-detect anomalies you didn't anticipate (row count dropped 40%, a new null pattern). Tools like Monte Carlo provide this; it catches unknown-unknowns that hand-written tests miss.

Q19. What is data lineage and why does it matter for incidents? mid

Lineage maps how data flows: which sources feed a table and which dashboards/models depend on it. During an incident it answers "what caused this?" (upstream) and "what's affected?" (downstream) instantly, so you can assess blast radius and notify the right consumers. Column-level lineage pinpoints exactly which transform introduced a bad value.

Q20. How do you design alerting to avoid alert fatigue? hard

Alert on symptoms that matter (SLA breaches, DQ gate failures affecting consumers), not every metric. Set severity tiers (page for critical, ticket for warnings), use sensible thresholds and baselines to cut false positives, deduplicate and group related alerts, add hysteresis/for-duration so a single blip doesn't fire, route to the right owner, and make every alert actionable with a runbook link. Regularly prune noisy alerts.

Q21. Scenario: a dashboard shows row counts dropped 40% today. What's your triage? hard

Check whether the drop is real or a pipeline issue: did the load complete? Compare landed row counts vs source counts (reconciliation). Is a source file missing/partial, a filter newly excluding rows, or an upstream job failed/late? Look at recent deploys and lineage. If it's a load failure, re-run idempotently; if the source genuinely sent less, confirm with the producer before "fixing" real data. Communicate provisional status to stakeholders.

Q22. How do you validate a pipeline before it goes to production? mid

Run it in staging on representative data, reconcile output against a trusted source or the legacy system (parallel run), assert DQ checks (nulls, uniqueness, ranges, freshness), verify idempotency by re-running, load-test at expected volume, and confirm monitoring/alerts fire correctly. Only promote after a clean parallel run over several cycles.

Q23. What is a checksum/hash reconciliation and when is it worth it? hard

Beyond comparing counts and sums, you compute a hash over concatenated key columns per row (or an aggregate hash per partition) on both source and target. Matching hashes prove not just the same number of rows but the same content. It's worth it for financial/regulatory data where a count match could still hide value corruption; it's heavier, so reserve it for critical tables.

Q24. How do you set DQ tolerance thresholds instead of exact matches? mid

Exact equality is often unrealistic (late data, rounding). Define tolerance as an absolute or relative band (e.g. counts within 0.1%, revenue within $1). Base thresholds on historical variance so they're neither too tight (noisy alerts) nor too loose (misses). Treat breaches beyond tolerance as incidents; log within-tolerance drift for trend analysis.

Q25. How would you frame your Jio production-support experience for a DE interview? mid

Translate it into DE-reliability language: "I owned data validation and source-to-target reconciliation, investigated mismatches to root cause, and wrote RCAs with preventive actions." Quantify impact (issues caught, downtime reduced, records reconciled). Map each task to a concept: reconciliation → DQ checks, RCA → incident management, monitoring → observability/SLAs. This positions you as someone who already protects data reliability — exactly what fintech DE teams need.

Data Engineering System Design

The final-round interview: given a vague problem, design a data pipeline out loud — requirements, architecture, components, and tradeoffs. Here's the framework and worked answers.

What & Why

System design rounds test whether you can turn an ambiguous business need ("we want real-time fraud detection") into a concrete, defensible architecture. Interviewers care less about the "right" answer than about your process: do you clarify requirements, reason about scale, pick appropriate tools, and articulate tradeoffs? It's the round that separates senior-thinking candidates from tool-listers.

Use a repeatable framework every time so you never freeze. 1) Clarify requirements — the 5 Vs: Volume (how much data), Velocity (batch vs streaming, latency needed), Variety (structured/semi/unstructured), plus consumers (who reads it, what SLA) and business goal. 2) Sketch a high-level architecture — source → ingest → store → transform → serve. 3) Choose components and justify each. 4) Address cross-cutting concerns — partitioning/scaling, idempotency, data quality, monitoring, cost, security. 5) State tradeoffs — every choice has a cost; naming it shows maturity.

Talk continuously, draw boxes and arrows, and ask before assuming. State assumptions explicitly ("I'll assume 1TB/day and a 1-hour freshness SLA"). It's a conversation, not a monologue — check in with the interviewer as you go.

A useful storage mental model: OLTP databases for transactions, data lakes for cheap raw storage, warehouses for analytics/BI, NoSQL for high-throughput key lookups, and streaming systems for low-latency event flow. Matching the store to the access pattern is half of good design.

Learning Checklist

Projects & Practice

  • Clickstream analytics — one-pager — design ingestion of website events to a warehouse for product analytics. Cover volume estimates, streaming vs micro-batch, schema, partitioning by date, dedup of events, and serving to BI. Write it as requirements → architecture → components → tradeoffs.
