  • Fintech transactions pipeline — one-pager — design a pipeline for payment transactions with strong correctness (exactly-once, reconciliation, audit), low-latency fraud checks, and compliance. Emphasise your DQ/reconciliation strengths.
  • CDC replication to warehouse — one-pager — design change-data-capture from an OLTP MySQL/Postgres to Snowflake: log-based CDC, ordering, deletes, schema evolution, and merge into target.
  • Practice out loud — set a 30-minute timer and talk through each design as if interviewing, drawing boxes. Record yourself; the goal is fluent, structured narration.

Interview Q&A (25)

Q1. How do you approach any data system design question? basic

Follow a framework: (1) clarify requirements — volume, velocity/latency, variety, consumers, SLAs, cost; (2) state assumptions; (3) sketch source → ingest → store → transform → serve; (4) choose and justify components; (5) address partitioning, idempotency, DQ, monitoring, cost; (6) name tradeoffs. Narrate throughout and check in with the interviewer.

Q2. What questions do you ask to clarify requirements? basic

How much data (per day, per event, growth)? How fresh must it be (real-time, hourly, daily)? Batch or streaming? What's the data shape and does the schema change? Who consumes it and how (BI, ML, API) and what's their SLA? What's the budget and existing stack? What are the correctness/compliance requirements? These answers drive every later choice.

Q3. Give the template for a batch pipeline design. mid

Source (DB/files/API) → Ingest (scheduled extract, e.g. Airflow-orchestrated) → Land raw in a lake (S3/ADLS, partitioned by date) → Transform (Spark/dbt) into cleaned + modeled layers (bronze/silver/gold) → Warehouse (Snowflake/BigQuery) → Serve (BI/exports). Cross-cutting: orchestration + retries, idempotent partition overwrites, DQ checks between layers, lineage, and monitoring/alerting.

Q4. Give the template for a streaming pipeline design. mid

Producers → Broker (Kafka/Kinesis, partitioned topic) → Stream processor (Spark Structured Streaming/Flink) doing enrichment, windowed aggregations, dedup → Sinks (a serving store like a NoSQL/warehouse for queries, plus the raw lake for replay/reprocessing). Concerns: partition key choice for parallelism/ordering, watermarks for late data, checkpointing, exactly-once via idempotent sinks, and backpressure handling.

Q5. Explain Lambda vs Kappa architecture and when you'd choose each. hard

Lambda runs a batch layer (accurate, reprocessable) and a speed/streaming layer (low-latency, approximate) in parallel, merging at serving — powerful but you maintain two codebases. Kappa uses a single streaming pipeline for everything and reprocesses by replaying the log — simpler, one codebase, but demands mature streaming and a durable, replayable log. Choose Kappa when your streaming stack is solid and replay is feasible; Lambda when batch accuracy and existing batch logic matter and streaming can't yet fully replace it.

Q6. How do you choose between a data lake, warehouse, OLTP DB, and NoSQL? hard

OLTP (Postgres/MySQL): transactional writes, row lookups, app backends. Data lake (S3 + Parquet): cheap storage of massive raw/semi-structured data, schema-on-read, ML. Warehouse (Snowflake/BigQuery): columnar analytics, BI, complex SQL joins/aggregations. NoSQL (DynamoDB/Cassandra): high-throughput key-based reads/writes, low latency, flexible schema, poor for ad-hoc analytics. Match the store to the dominant access pattern; lakehouse blends lake + warehouse.

Q7. Design a daily batch ETL for 1TB/day. hard

Requirements: 1TB/day, daily freshness SLA (say by 6am), structured source, BI consumers. Architecture: Airflow triggers a nightly extract → land raw Parquet in S3 partitioned by ingest date → Spark job cleans/transforms into silver, then aggregates to gold → load gold into Snowflake → BI reads. Key choices: partition by date for pruning and idempotent overwrite; right-size Spark and use columnar Parquet with ~128-256MB files to avoid small-file problems; DQ checks (counts, nulls, reconciliation) between layers gate promotion. Tradeoffs: batch is simple and cheap but not real-time; Spark handles the 1TB scale but adds ops overhead vs a warehouse-native ELT approach.

Q8. Design a real-time fraud detection pipeline. hard

Requirements: sub-second scoring on each transaction, high availability, low false negatives. Architecture: transactions → Kafka → stream processor (Flink/Spark Streaming) that enriches with features (recent velocity, device history) from a low-latency store (Redis/DynamoDB) and applies a rules + ML model → decision emitted to the payment service and to a Kafka topic → sink to lake/warehouse for analysis and model retraining. Concerns: partition Kafka by account for ordered per-account state; keep features fresh in a feature store; exactly-once/idempotent scoring keyed on transaction id; a fallback rule engine if the model is slow; monitor latency and score drift. Tradeoff: tighter latency vs richer features — cap enrichment lookups to hit the SLA.

Q9. Design CDC replication from MySQL to Snowflake. hard

Approach: log-based CDC (read the MySQL binlog via Debezium) rather than query-based polling, to capture inserts/updates/deletes with low load. Flow: Debezium → Kafka (one topic per table, keyed by primary key to preserve per-row ordering) → a sink/loader that lands change events in a Snowflake staging table → periodic MERGE into the target using the PK and event timestamp so the latest change wins; handle deletes via a tombstone/soft-delete flag. Concerns: ordering (partition by PK), schema evolution (propagate DDL, use flexible staging), initial snapshot + incremental handoff, idempotent MERGE for replays, and monitoring replication lag. Tradeoff: log-based is efficient and complete but operationally heavier than a simple nightly full-dump.

Q10. How do you deduplicate events at scale? hard

Require a stable event id from the producer. In batch, dedup with ROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY event_id ORDER BY event_time DESC) = 1 or a MERGE that ignores existing ids. In streaming, keep a keyed state store of seen ids within a bounded window (you can't remember forever) and drop repeats; use idempotent/transactional sinks so retries don't reinsert. Add a unique constraint at the sink as a backstop. Tradeoff: dedup window size vs state/memory cost.

Q11. How do you handle late-arriving data in a design? hard

Aggregate on event time, not processing time. In batch, reprocess a trailing window (e.g. last 3 days) each run with idempotent partition overwrite so late records land in the correct day. In streaming, use watermarks with an allowed-lateness bound and update or emit late results; anything beyond the bound goes to a side output for manual handling. Mark recent windows provisional until they close. Tradeoff: longer reprocessing/lateness windows improve completeness but cost compute and delay finality.

Q12. Design a schema evolution strategy. hard

Use a schema registry (Avro/Protobuf) with compatibility rules — prefer backward-compatible changes: add nullable/defaulted columns, never rename or drop in place. Store raw data in formats that tolerate evolution (Parquet/Avro), and version the schema in a data contract so producers can't break consumers silently. For the warehouse, apply additive DDL via migrations. Handle removals with expand-then-contract. Tradeoff: strict compatibility slows producers slightly but protects every downstream consumer.

Q13. How do you partition data and choose a partition key? mid

Partition by the column most queries filter on — usually date/event_date — so the engine prunes irrelevant data. Keep partitions a healthy size (avoid millions of tiny partitions or a few giant ones). Avoid high-skew keys that create hotspots. In warehouses, use clustering/sort keys on frequent filter/join columns. Good partitioning cuts scan cost and enables idempotent per-partition overwrites. Tradeoff: over-partitioning creates small-file/metadata overhead.

Q14. How do you scale a pipeline as data grows 10x? hard

Identify the bottleneck (ingest, compute, or serving). Scale horizontally: more Kafka partitions and consumers, larger/auto-scaling Spark clusters, partition/cluster the warehouse. Fix skew and small files. Push filtering/aggregation earlier (partition pruning, predicate pushdown). Consider incremental processing instead of full reloads. Decouple stages with a durable buffer (Kafka/object storage) so a slow stage doesn't stall producers. Monitor throughput and lag to know where to add capacity.

Q15. Design a migration from an on-prem system to the cloud. hard

Plan: inventory sources/pipelines/consumers and dependencies. Approach: lift raw data to cloud object storage first (bulk historical load + CDC for ongoing changes), rebuild transforms in cloud-native tools (Spark/dbt), and run the new pipeline in parallel with on-prem, reconciling outputs until they match. Cut consumers over gradually, keep rollback ready, then decommission on-prem. Concerns: network/egress for the initial transfer, security/compliance, cost modeling, and validation via reconciliation at every step. Tradeoff: parallel-run is safe but doubles cost temporarily.

Q16. Design a clickstream analytics pipeline. hard

Requirements: high event volume, near-real-time to hourly freshness, product-analytics consumers. Architecture: web/app SDK → collector API → Kafka (topic partitioned by session/user) → stream processor for sessionization, dedup on event id, and bot filtering → land raw in the lake (partitioned by date/hour) and write modeled aggregates to the warehouse → BI/product analytics. Concerns: schema for flexible event properties (semi-structured JSON), dedup, late events via event-time windows, and cost control on huge volume (aggregate early, tier storage). Tradeoff: real-time serving vs cost — micro-batch may suffice if hourly freshness is acceptable.

Q17. Design a fintech transactions pipeline emphasizing correctness. hard

Requirements: exactly-once, full auditability, reconciliation, compliance/retention. Architecture: transactions → Kafka (keyed by account, ordered) → stream/micro-batch processor writing idempotently (dedup on transaction id, transactional sink) → immutable raw ledger in the lake (append-only, retained for audit) + curated warehouse tables. Reliability layer: daily source-to-target reconciliation of counts and amounts with tolerance, checksums on critical tables, freshness SLAs, and a data contract with the payment service. Concerns: PII encryption, access controls, and immutable audit trail. Tradeoff: stronger guarantees (transactions, reconciliation) add latency and cost, justified by financial correctness.

Q18. How do you build monitoring and data quality into a design? mid

Treat DQ and observability as first-class components, not an afterthought. Add DQ gates between layers (counts, nulls, uniqueness, ranges) that fail the pipeline on critical breaches; monitor freshness against SLAs; reconcile source-to-target; track pipeline metrics (runtime, rows, lag); and design tiered alerting with runbooks. Emit lineage so incidents can be scoped. Mentioning this unprompted signals production maturity to interviewers.

Q19. How do you factor cost into a data system design? mid

Cost = storage + compute + movement. Use tiered/cheap object storage for raw and lifecycle-expire old data; columnar formats and partition pruning to scan less; right-size and auto-scale compute, prefer incremental over full reloads; batch where real-time isn't required; avoid small files and cross-region egress. State the cost tradeoff of each choice (e.g. streaming is pricier than batch) so the design is defensible on budget.

Q20. How do you ensure exactly-once / idempotency in a design? hard

Assume at-least-once delivery and make writes idempotent: dedup on a stable event/transaction id, use MERGE/upsert or deterministic partition overwrite instead of blind append, and use transactional or atomic sink commits so a retry can't partially reapply. In streaming, combine checkpointing with idempotent sinks. Add a unique constraint as a safety net. Design so any stage can be safely retried — that's what makes the pipeline reliable.

Q21. When would you choose ELT over ETL? mid

ETL transforms before loading — suited to constrained targets or heavy cleansing needs, or when you can't land raw sensitive data. ELT loads raw first and transforms inside a powerful warehouse (dbt on Snowflake/BigQuery) — the modern default: cheap storage keeps raw for reprocessing, and warehouse compute scales transforms. Choose ELT when you have a scalable warehouse and want flexibility; ETL when transformation must happen before landing (privacy, legacy targets).

Q22. Design a system to serve both real-time and historical analytics. hard

This is the Lambda/Kappa question in disguise. Land all events in a durable log (Kafka) and the lake. A streaming layer serves fresh/real-time metrics into a low-latency store; a batch/warehouse layer computes accurate historical aggregates. Serve them through a unified query layer or a serving store that merges recent + historical. Prefer Kappa (single stream + replay) if streaming is mature; Lambda if batch accuracy is essential. Reconcile the two layers to avoid divergence. Tradeoff: dual layers add complexity and a consistency reconciliation burden.

Q23. How do you handle PII and security in a data pipeline design? mid

Classify PII, then minimise and protect it: encrypt at rest and in transit, tokenize/mask/hash sensitive fields, enforce least-privilege access (role-based, column/row-level security in the warehouse), and keep an audit log of access. Isolate raw PII zones, apply retention/deletion policies (GDPR right-to-erasure), and avoid copying PII into wide analytics tables. Bake this into the design from the start for a fintech role.

Q24. How do you communicate tradeoffs effectively in a design interview? mid

For each major decision, state the option chosen, why, and the cost you're accepting: "I'll use batch here — cheaper and simpler, accepting up to an hour of latency, which meets the stated SLA." Acknowledge the alternative and when you'd flip. This shows you understand there's no free lunch and that you optimise for the stated requirements, not for buzzwords.

Q25. What are the most common mistakes candidates make in DE system design rounds? mid

Jumping to tools before clarifying requirements; not stating assumptions; ignoring scale/cost; forgetting cross-cutting concerns (idempotency, DQ, monitoring, security); over-engineering (streaming when batch suffices); and going silent. Fixes: always start with the requirements framework, narrate continuously, right-size the solution to the stated volume/latency, and explicitly cover reliability and tradeoffs. Structured, communicative reasoning beats a fancy but unjustified architecture.

Azure Playbooks (ADF, ADLS, Key Vault, Databricks)

Click-by-click guides for the Azure Data Factory / ADLS work that fills most Indian-market DE job descriptions.

Task: Create a free Azure account + resource group + ADLS Gen2 storage and upload a file

Goal: get a working Azure subscription and a data lake to land raw files. Every Azure DE project starts here — you need a place to store data before you can move or transform it.

  1. Go to https://azure.microsoft.com/free and click Start free. Sign in with a Microsoft account (create one if needed).
  2. Enter a phone number (SMS verification) and a credit/debit card. You get USD 200 credit for 30 days plus always-free services; the card is only for identity — you are not charged unless you upgrade.
  3. After signup lands you in the Azure Portal (https://portal.azure.com). In the top search bar type Resource groups → open it → + Create.
  4. Set Subscription = your free subscription, Resource group = rg-de-lab, Region = Central India (or nearest). Click Review + create → Create. A resource group is just a folder that groups related resources so you can delete them together.
  5. Top search bar → Storage accounts → + Create. Pick rg-de-lab, name it globally-unique like stdelab+random digits (lowercase, no dashes), region same as above, Performance = Standard, Redundancy = LRS (cheapest).
  6. Go to the Advanced tab. Under Data Lake Storage Gen2 find Enable hierarchical namespace and tick it. This is what turns a plain blob account into ADLS Gen2 (real folders, not just prefixes). Review + create → Create, wait ~1 min, then Go to resource.
  7. Left sidebar → Data storage → Containers → + Container. Name it raw → Create. Open it → Upload → pick a small CSV from your machine → Upload.

Verify: Open the raw container; your CSV appears with a size > 0 KB. Click it → Edit tab shows the file contents.

Gotchas: "Storage account name is already taken" — names are global, add more random digits. If you forget to enable hierarchical namespace, you get a blob account and ADF/Databricks lake paths behave oddly — you cannot toggle it after creation, so recreate. Don't pick Premium performance (bills against credit fast). Delete the whole rg-de-lab resource group when done for the day to avoid surprise usage.

Task: Build your first ADF pipeline — copy a CSV from GitHub (HTTP) to ADLS

Goal: learn the ADF building blocks (linked service → dataset → Copy activity → debug → trigger) by ingesting a public file into your lake. This is the single most common ADF task at work: pull a file from a source and land it.

  1. Portal search → Data factories → + Create. Resource group rg-de-lab, name adf-de-lab-+digits, region Central India. Skip Git config for now (choose Configure Git later). Create → Go to resource → Launch studio (opens adf.azure.com).
  2. In ADF Studio, left sidebar → Author (pencil icon). Next to Pipelines click the + ... / Pipeline → Pipeline. Name it pl_copy_http_to_adls on the right-hand Properties pane.
  3. From the Activities panel (left of canvas), expand Move & transform and drag Copy data onto the canvas.
  4. Select the Copy activity → Source tab → + New dataset → choose HTTP → format DelimitedText (CSV) → Continue. On the dataset, next to Linked service click + New: Base URL = the raw GitHub URL folder e.g. https://raw.githubusercontent.com/, Authentication = Anonymous → Create. Set Relative URL to the rest of the path, e.g. cs109/2014_data/master/countries.csv. Tick First row as header.
  5. Sink tab → + New dataset → Azure Data Lake Storage Gen2 → DelimitedText → Continue. New linked service → pick your storage account stdelab... via Account key or System-assigned managed identity → Create. Set file path: container raw, directory github, file countries.csv.
  6. Top toolbar → Debug. Watch the Output tab at the bottom; the Copy activity shows In progress → Succeeded.
  7. To run on a schedule: top toolbar → Add trigger → New/Edit → + New → type Schedule, set recurrence (e.g. every 1 day) → OK. Then Publish all (top toolbar) to save everything to the service — Debug runs work unpublished, triggers do not.

Verify: In the storage account → raw/github/countries.csv now exists. In ADF, left sidebar → Monitor → Pipeline runs shows one Succeeded run.

Gotchas: "Publish all" is greyed out if you have unsaved errors — the pipeline name may have a red dot; fix the flagged activity. Managed-identity sink fails with 403 AuthorizationFailure until you grant ADF's identity the Storage Blob Data Contributor role on the storage account (Storage account → Access control (IAM) → Add role assignment). If GitHub returns 404, you used the HTML page URL, not the raw.githubusercontent.com URL. Trigger doesn't fire? You forgot to Publish after adding it.

Task: Parameterized incremental load with a watermark (Lookup + Copy + Stored Proc)

Goal: load only rows changed since last run instead of the whole table every time. This "high-watermark" pattern is asked in almost every ADF interview and is how real pipelines stay cheap and fast.

  1. In your source SQL DB, create a watermark table (run once): 
    CREATE TABLE dbo.watermark (
  TableName  NVARCHAR(128) PRIMARY KEY,
  LastLoaded DATETIME2
);
INSERT INTO dbo.watermark VALUES ('orders', '1900-01-01');
  2. Create a stored proc that advances the watermark after a successful load: 
    CREATE PROCEDURE dbo.usp_update_watermark
  @NewWatermark DATETIME2, @TableName NVARCHAR(128)
AS
UPDATE dbo.watermark
SET LastLoaded = @NewWatermark
WHERE TableName = @TableName;
  3. ADF Author → new pipeline pl_inc_orders. On the pipeline Parameters tab add p_table (String, default orders).
  4. Drag a Lookup activity, name it lkp_old_wm. Source query: SELECT LastLoaded FROM dbo.watermark WHERE TableName = '@{pipeline().parameters.p_table}'. Tick First row only.
  5. Drag a second Lookup lkp_new_wm (connect green success arrow from lkp_old_wm). Query: SELECT MAX(updated_at) AS NewWM FROM dbo.orders, First row only. This is the newest timestamp currently in the source.
  6. Drag a Copy data activity after lkp_new_wm. Source query uses both watermarks: 
    SELECT * FROM dbo.orders
WHERE updated_at >  '@{activity('lkp_old_wm').output.firstRow.LastLoaded}'
  AND updated_at <= '@{activity('lkp_new_wm').output.firstRow.NewWM}'
    Sink to ADLS raw/orders/@{formatDateTime(utcNow(),'yyyyMMddHHmmss')}.parquet.
  7. Drag a Stored procedure activity after Copy. Point it at dbo.usp_update_watermark. Import parameters and set @NewWatermark = @{activity('lkp_new_wm').output.firstRow.NewWM}, @TableName = @{pipeline().parameters.p_table}.
  8. Debug. First run pulls everything after 1900. Run again immediately — Copy reads 0 rows because the watermark caught up. Insert a new order row in source, run again — only that row copies.

Verify: Second consecutive run shows Rows read: 0 in the Copy activity output. After inserting one new source row, the next run copies exactly 1 row and the watermark table's LastLoaded advances.

Gotchas: Use > on the old watermark and <= on the new one — using >= on both re-copies boundary rows every run (duplicates). If you update the watermark before the copy succeeds and the copy fails, you silently lose data — always put the stored proc after a successful Copy. Expression fields need the @{...} interpolation form inside strings; a bare @activity(...) only works when the whole field is the expression. Source column must actually be monotonic (an updated_at that never goes backwards).

Task: Monitor & debug a failed ADF pipeline

Goal: read the Monitor tab, find the failing activity and error code, fix it, and rerun from the point of failure without redoing successful work. On-call DEs do this constantly.

  1. ADF Studio left sidebar → Monitor → Pipeline runs. Failed runs show status Failed in red. Click the pipeline name to open the run.
  2. You now see the activity-level view. Find the red Failed activity. Hover it and click the error icon (speech bubble) to read the message, or the Output (glasses) icon for full JSON.
  3. Note the errorCode. Common ones: 2200 user error in the activity (bad query/path), DFExecutorUserError data flow logic, 2100 failed dependency, UserErrorFileNotFound missing source file, SqlOperationFailed SQL threw an error.
  4. Fix the root cause in Author (e.g. correct the file path, add a null-safe cast, grant the missing IAM role) and Publish.
  5. Back in Monitor, open the failed run → top of the run view has Rerun and Rerun from failed activity. Choose Rerun from failed activity so upstream succeeded activities are skipped.
  6. For repeating failures, set up alerting: Monitor → Alerts & metrics → New alert rule → target metric Failed pipeline runs → notify an action group (your email).

Verify: The rerun shows the previously-failed activity now Succeeded and the overall run status flips to Succeeded. The alert rule appears under Alerts & metrics with state Enabled.

Gotchas: Monitor shows the last run's error, but if you re-published a fix and re-debug from Author instead of rerunning, you create a separate debug run — check you are looking at the right run timestamp. "Rerun from failed activity" is disabled for Debug runs; it only applies to triggered/published runs. Integration-runtime timeouts (self-hosted IR offline) show as failures with IntegrationRuntimeNotAvailable — the fix is starting the IR, not editing the pipeline.

Task: Connect ADF to an Azure Databricks notebook activity

Goal: orchestrate a Databricks transformation from ADF, passing parameters in. This is the standard "ADF orchestrates, Databricks transforms" pattern you will see in most modern Azure data platforms.

  1. In the Databricks workspace, top-right → your email → Settings / User settings → Developer → Access tokens → Generate new token. Copy it now (shown once). Create a simple notebook e.g. /Shared/etl_silver that reads a widget: dbutils.widgets.text("run_date",""); rd = dbutils.widgets.get("run_date").
  2. In ADF Author → pipeline → drag Notebook activity (under Databricks) onto the canvas.
  3. Select it → Azure Databricks tab → + New linked service. Choose your Databricks workspace from the subscription, select Access Token auth, paste the token (better: reference Key Vault — see next playbook). For cluster choose New job cluster (spins up per run, cheaper) or an existing interactive cluster.
  4. Settings tab → Notebook path → browse to /Shared/etl_silver. Under Base parameters add run_date = @{formatDateTime(utcNow(),'yyyy-MM-dd')}.
  5. Debug. ADF submits the job; a new cluster boots (2-4 min on first run) then the notebook runs.

Verify: ADF Copy/Notebook activity output shows Succeeded and a runPageUrl. Click it to open the exact Databricks job run and confirm your notebook received the run_date widget value.

Gotchas: Token expired / revoked → linked service test fails with 401; regenerate. Job-cluster boot adds minutes to every run — for chained notebooks reuse an instance pool or an interactive cluster. If the notebook needs a value back in ADF, end it with dbutils.notebook.exit("myvalue") and read it via @activity('Notebook1').output.runOutput. Region mismatch between ADF and the workspace works but adds egress latency.

Task: Store secrets in Azure Key Vault and use them in ADF

Goal: stop pasting passwords/tokens into linked services; pull them from Key Vault at runtime. Hardcoded secrets are an instant interview red flag and a security audit failure.

  1. Portal search → Key vaults → + Create. RG rg-de-lab, name kv-de-lab-+digits, region same, keep defaults → Create → Go to resource.
  2. Left sidebar → Objects → Secrets → + Generate/Import. Name databricks-token, paste the value → Create. Repeat for sql-password etc.
  3. Grant ADF read access. Key vault → Access control (IAM) (if using RBAC model) → Add role assignment → role Key Vault Secrets User → assign to your data factory's managed identity (search the ADF name). If the vault uses the Access policy model instead: Access policies → + Create → Secret permissions Get, List → principal = the ADF.
  4. In ADF Author → Manage (toolbox icon) → Linked services → + New → Azure Key Vault → pick the vault → Create. This registers the vault as a source of secrets.
  5. Edit any linked service that needs a secret (e.g. the Databricks one). For the token/password field switch the dropdown from Value to Azure Key Vault, choose the KV linked service and the secret name databricks-token. Test connection → Save.

Verify: The linked service Test connection succeeds while showing no plaintext secret in its JSON (it stores only the secret reference). Removing/renaming the secret in Key Vault makes the test fail — proving it resolves at runtime.

Gotchas: Forbidden / access denied resolving a secret = the ADF managed identity lacks Get on secrets; RBAC role assignments can take a few minutes to propagate. Mixing the two permission models (RBAC vs access policies) is the #1 confusion — check the vault's Access configuration to see which model is active. Secret versions: referencing without a version always gets the latest, which is usually what you want. Enabling purge protection is smart but means you cannot fully delete the vault for the retention window.

Databricks Playbooks (Clusters, Delta, Jobs, Spark UI)

Hands-on Databricks + Delta Lake tasks — the lakehouse skills fintech/product DE roles now expect.

Task: Sign up for Databricks Free / Community Edition + workspace tour

Goal: get a free Databricks workspace to practice Spark and Delta without a cloud bill. This is your daily-driver playground for the rest of these playbooks.

  1. Go to https://www.databricks.com/learn/free-edition (or databricks.com/try-databricks). Enter your email and choose Free Edition / Community Edition — pick the free option, NOT the "start 14-day trial on my own cloud" path (that one bills your cloud account).
  2. Verify your email, set a password, and you land in the workspace at a *.cloud.databricks.com URL. Bookmark it.
  3. Tour the left sidebar: Workspace (your notebooks/folders), Catalog (databases & tables via Unity Catalog), Compute (clusters), Workflows / Jobs (schedules), SQL Editor (ad-hoc queries).
  4. Left sidebar → Workspace → Users → your email → this is your personal home folder. Right-click → Create → Notebook to make your first notebook; set default language to Python.

Verify: A new notebook opens; typing print("hello") and pressing Shift+Enter attaches compute and prints the result under the cell.

Gotchas: The old Community Edition had a fixed tiny cluster; the newer Free Edition uses serverless compute — the button names differ but the workflow is the same. Free tiers idle-terminate compute after a while; just re-run a cell to restart. Don't put real credentials in a free workspace. If signup insists on selecting a cloud provider and asks for cloud credentials, you're on the paid-trial path — back out and look for the "Free/Community" link explicitly.

Task: Create compute + first PySpark notebook reading an uploaded CSV

Goal: stand up a cluster and read a file into a Spark DataFrame. Reading data into Spark is step one of literally every Databricks job.

  1. Left sidebar → Compute → Create compute / Create cluster. Name it learn, keep the default runtime (latest LTS), single node is fine for learning → Create compute. Wait for the green dot (2-5 min). On serverless Free Edition, skip this — compute attaches automatically.
  2. Upload data: left sidebar → Catalog → + Add / Add data → Upload files to volume / Create table from upload → drag your CSV. Note the path it gives you (a Unity Catalog Volume path like /Volumes/main/default/myvol/data.csv, or a legacy /FileStore/... path).
  3. Open a notebook, attach it to the learn cluster (dropdown at top-left of the notebook), and read the file: 
    df = (spark.read
      .option("header", True)
      .option("inferSchema", True)
      .csv("/Volumes/main/default/myvol/data.csv"))
df.printSchema()
df.show(5)
print("rows:", df.count())
  4. Do a quick transform to prove Spark works: 
    from pyspark.sql import functions as F
(df.groupBy("some_category_col")
   .agg(F.count("*").alias("n"))
   .orderBy(F.desc("n"))
   .show())

Verify: df.show(5) prints a table of rows and df.count() matches the number of data lines in your CSV (file lines minus 1 for the header).

Gotchas: Path does not exist — copy the exact path shown after upload; Volume vs FileStore vs DBFS paths are not interchangeable. Every numeric column reads as string when you forget inferSchema (or better, supply an explicit schema for production). A CSV with commas inside quoted fields needs .option("quote", '"').option("escape", '"'). show() only triggers a job because Spark is lazy — nothing computes until an action.

Task: Build a bronze → silver → gold Delta pipeline in a notebook

Goal: implement the medallion architecture — raw (bronze), cleaned (silver), aggregated (gold) — using Delta tables. This layering is the default design pattern in every lakehouse and a guaranteed interview topic.

  1. Bronze — land raw data as-is into a Delta table, adding ingest metadata: 
    from pyspark.sql import functions as F
bronze = (spark.read.option("header", True)
          .csv("/Volumes/main/default/myvol/orders.csv")
          .withColumn("_ingest_ts", F.current_timestamp())
          .withColumn("_source_file", F.input_file_name()))
bronze.write.format("delta").mode("overwrite").saveAsTable("main.default.orders_bronze")
  2. Silver — clean types, drop bad rows, deduplicate: 
    silver = (spark.table("main.default.orders_bronze")
          .withColumn("amount", F.col("amount").cast("double"))
          .withColumn("order_ts", F.to_timestamp("order_ts"))
          .filter(F.col("amount").isNotNull() & (F.col("amount") > 0))
          .dropDuplicates(["order_id"]))
silver.write.format("delta").mode("overwrite").saveAsTable("main.default.orders_silver")
  3. Gold — business-level aggregate the analysts query: 
    gold = (spark.table("main.default.orders_silver")
        .groupBy(F.to_date("order_ts").alias("day"))
        .agg(F.sum("amount").alias("revenue"),
             F.countDistinct("customer_id").alias("buyers")))
gold.write.format("delta").mode("overwrite").saveAsTable("main.default.orders_gold")
  4. Query the gold table in the SQL Editor or a cell: %sql SELECT * FROM main.default.orders_gold ORDER BY day.

Verify: Catalog shows three tables orders_bronze/silver/gold. SELECT COUNT(*) on silver is ≤ bronze (dupes/bad rows removed) and gold has one row per day.

Gotchas: mode("overwrite") replaces the whole table each run — fine for a full refresh, but for incremental use MERGE (next playbook). Casting a non-numeric string to double yields null silently, not an error — that's why the silver filter drops nulls. Delta needs the table format explicitly: writing without .format("delta") on some runtimes gives Parquet and you lose ACID/time-travel. Schema mismatch on overwrite needs .option("overwriteSchema","true").

Task: Create a Job / Workflow — schedule a notebook with task dependencies + email alert

Goal: run notebooks on a schedule with a proper DAG (bronze then silver then gold) and get emailed on failure. This is how notebooks become production pipelines.

  1. Left sidebar → Workflows / Jobs → Create job.
  2. First task: Name bronze, Type Notebook, select your bronze notebook, choose compute (job cluster is cheaper than all-purpose). Save.
  3. Click + Add task → silver task → under Depends on pick bronze. Add another task gold depending on silver. You now have a linear DAG.
  4. Right panel → Schedule / Triggers → Add trigger → Scheduled → e.g. cron for daily 02:00 (or "Every day"). Set the timezone (Asia/Kolkata).
  5. Right panel → Notifications → Add → your email → tick on failure (and optionally on start/success).
  6. Click Run now to test. Open the run → each task shows a graph node turning green in dependency order.

Verify: The job's Runs tab shows a successful run with three green task nodes executed in order bronze→silver→gold. Forcing a failure (e.g. break the silver notebook) triggers a failure email and the downstream gold task shows Skipped/Upstream failed.

Gotchas: All-purpose cluster jobs cost more and stay warm; use a job cluster for scheduled work. If tasks don't run in order, you forgot to set Depends on — without it Databricks runs them in parallel. Free Edition may restrict scheduling/notifications — you can still trigger with Run now. Cron in the wrong timezone is the classic "why did it run at 7:30 AM" bug — always set the job timezone explicitly.

Task: Delta operations drill — MERGE upsert, time travel, OPTIMIZE, VACUUM

Goal: practice the four Delta superpowers that separate a lakehouse from plain files. Interviewers love "how do you do upserts / roll back / compact small files in a data lake?" — the answer is these commands.

  1. MERGE (upsert) — update matching rows, insert new ones: 
    %sql
MERGE INTO main.default.orders_silver AS t
USING updates_view AS s
ON t.order_id = s.order_id
WHEN MATCHED THEN UPDATE SET t.amount = s.amount, t.status = s.status
WHEN NOT MATCHED THEN INSERT *;
  2. Time travel — read a past version after a bad load: 
    %sql
DESCRIBE HISTORY main.default.orders_silver;      -- see versions + timestamps
SELECT * FROM main.default.orders_silver VERSION AS OF 3;
SELECT * FROM main.default.orders_silver TIMESTAMP AS OF '2026-07-01T00:00:00';
    To roll back: RESTORE TABLE main.default.orders_silver TO VERSION AS OF 3;
  3. OPTIMIZE — compact many small files into fewer big ones (and optionally cluster data): 
    %sql
OPTIMIZE main.default.orders_silver;
OPTIMIZE main.default.orders_silver ZORDER BY (customer_id);
  4. VACUUM — physically delete old data files no longer referenced (frees storage). Default keeps 7 days for time-travel safety: 
    %sql
VACUUM main.default.orders_silver;              -- default 168h retention
VACUUM main.default.orders_silver RETAIN 168 HOURS;

Verify: After MERGE, re-running the same updates changes 0 rows (idempotent). DESCRIBE HISTORY lists a new version per operation (MERGE, OPTIMIZE, VACUUM each appear). After OPTIMIZE the numFiles in history drops.

Gotchas: MERGE fails with "cannot perform merge as multiple source rows matched" when your source view has duplicate keys — dedupe the source first. Never VACUUM ... RETAIN 0 HOURS on a live table: it deletes files newer than any running readers and breaks time travel (Databricks blocks it unless you disable a safety flag — don't). ZORDER only helps columns you actually filter on, and it's not free. Time travel only works on Delta tables, and only back to your VACUUM retention window.

Task: Debug a failed job with the Spark UI — find the slow stage and spot skew

Goal: read the Spark UI to locate why a job is slow or OOM-ing — usually a shuffle or data skew. "My Spark job is slow, how do you debug it?" is a top interview question and a real on-call skill.

  1. Open the failed/slow run: notebook cell → View / Spark Jobs under the cell, or Compute → your cluster → Spark UI.
  2. Jobs tab → find the job with the longest duration → click it → look at its Stages. The stage with the biggest Duration is your bottleneck.
  3. Open that stage → look at the Summary Metrics table. Compare the Max vs Median for Duration and Shuffle Read Size. If Max is many times the Median, a few tasks are doing most of the work → data skew.
  4. Check the Event Timeline / task list: a handful of long green bars while the rest finished fast confirms skew. Big Shuffle Read/Write numbers point to an expensive wide operation (join/groupBy).
  5. Fixes: for skew add a salt key to the join or enable AQE skew join (spark.sql.adaptive.enabled=true, on by default in modern runtimes); broadcast the small side of a join with F.broadcast(small_df) to avoid the shuffle entirely; repartition on the join key; filter/aggregate before the join to shrink data.

Verify: After applying a fix (e.g. broadcast join or AQE), the same query's slow stage shows a much smaller Max/Median gap and lower total shuffle bytes, and wall-clock time drops. The SQL / DataFrame tab query plan shows BroadcastHashJoin instead of SortMergeJoin.

Gotchas: An OOM'd task shows as Failed with java.lang.OutOfMemoryError in the executor logs (Spark UI → Executors → stderr) — usually skew or a broadcast that's too big. Broadcasting a table that isn't actually small OOMs the driver — keep broadcasts under a few hundred MB. Reading thousands of tiny files makes the scan stage dominate, not the shuffle — that's a compaction problem (OPTIMIZE), a different fix. Don't chase the stage with the most tasks; chase the one with the most time.

AWS Playbooks (S3, Glue, Athena, Lambda, Billing)

Serverless AWS data-engineering tasks on the free tier — plus the billing guardrails so you never get a surprise charge.

Task: Create a free-tier account + least-privilege IAM user + S3 bucket with raw/curated zones

Goal: set up AWS safely (never use the root account for daily work) and lay out an S3 bucket with proper zones. Good IAM hygiene and bucket layout are the first things a reviewer checks.

  1. Go to https://aws.amazon.com/free → Create a Free Account. Enter email, account name, verify, add a card (identity only), pick the Basic (free) support plan.
  2. Sign in as root once. Search IAM → Users → Create user → name deepesh-de → tick Provide user access to the console.
  3. Permissions: for learning attach the PowerUserAccess managed policy (everything except IAM/account management) OR, for least privilege, attach only AmazonS3FullAccess, AWSGlueConsoleFullAccess, AmazonAthenaFullAccess. Create the user and save the sign-in URL + password.
  4. Enable MFA on both root and this user: IAM → user → Security credentials → Assign MFA device → use an authenticator app. Sign out of root; do all further work as deepesh-de.
  5. Search S3 → Create bucket. Globally-unique name like de-lab-deepesh-2026, region ap-south-1 (Mumbai). Keep Block all public access ON. Create.
  6. Open the bucket → Create folder four times to build zones: raw/, curated/, athena-results/, scripts/. (S3 "folders" are just key prefixes but this layout keeps projects sane.)

Verify: You can sign in at the IAM user URL (not root), and the bucket lists the four prefixes. Uploading a file to raw/ succeeds; the bucket's Permissions tab shows Block all public access: On.

Gotchas: New IAM Identity Center vs classic IAM users — either works; the classic IAM → Users path above is simplest for a lab. Bucket names are global — "already exists" means add more uniqueness. If you skip region ap-south-1 you'll pay cross-region transfer later. Never uncheck Block public access to "make it work" — for Glue/Athena you grant access via IAM roles, not public buckets. Root account with no MFA is the classic account-compromise story.

Task: Upload CSV → Glue crawler → query with Athena

Goal: turn a raw CSV in S3 into a queryable SQL table without loading a database — the classic serverless "schema-on-read" pattern. This trio (S3 + Glue + Athena) is on nearly every AWS DE job description.

  1. Upload a CSV to s3://de-lab-deepesh-2026/raw/orders/orders.csv (keep it inside an orders/ folder — the crawler tables a folder, not a single file).
  2. Search AWS Glue → left menu Data Catalog → Databases → Add database → name de_lab.
  3. Glue left menu → Crawlers → Create crawler. Name crawl_orders. Data source = S3 path s3://de-lab-deepesh-2026/raw/orders/. Create a new IAM role (Glue offers to make AWSGlueServiceRole-...). Target database de_lab. Frequency On demand. Create.
  4. Select the crawler → Run. It finishes in ~1 min and creates a table (named after the folder, orders).
  5. Search Athena → open the query editor. First time only: Settings / Manage → Query result location → set s3://de-lab-deepesh-2026/athena-results/ → Save. Athena refuses to run any query until this is set.
  6. In the editor pick Data source = AwsDataCatalog, Database = de_lab, then run: 
    SELECT * FROM orders LIMIT 10;
SELECT status, COUNT(*) AS n FROM orders GROUP BY status ORDER BY n DESC;

Verify: The crawler run log says "created 1 table". Athena's SELECT * ... LIMIT 10 returns rows with correct column names, and query results appear as a CSV under athena-results/ in S3.

Gotchas: "No output location provided" — you skipped the Athena results-bucket step (step 5). Pointing the crawler at the file (orders.csv) instead of the folder can create a broken table — point at the prefix. If the header row shows up as a data row, set the table's skip.header.line.count = 1 (Glue table properties) or use Athena's OpenCSVSerde. Re-running a crawler after changing files updates the schema; delete the table if you want a clean re-detect.

Task: Convert to partitioned Parquet with Athena CTAS + compare scan cost

Goal: rewrite raw CSV as columnar, partitioned Parquet and measure how much less data Athena scans (Athena bills per TB scanned). Cutting scan cost via Parquet + partitioning is a core cost-optimization interview answer.

  1. Baseline: run your CSV query and note Data scanned shown under the results (e.g. 250 MB): 
    SELECT status, COUNT(*) FROM orders
WHERE order_date >= DATE '2026-01-01'
GROUP BY status;
  2. Create a partitioned Parquet table with CTAS (Create Table As Select): 
    CREATE TABLE de_lab.orders_parquet
WITH (
  format = 'PARQUET',
  parquet_compression = 'SNAPPY',
  external_location = 's3://de-lab-deepesh-2026/curated/orders_parquet/',
  partitioned_by = ARRAY['order_year']
) AS
SELECT order_id, customer_id, status, amount,
       year(order_date) AS order_year
FROM de_lab.orders;
  3. Query the new table with a partition filter and note the new Data scanned: 
    SELECT status, COUNT(*) FROM orders_parquet
WHERE order_year = 2026
GROUP BY status;
  4. Compare the two "Data scanned" numbers. Parquet skips columns you don't select and the partition filter skips whole folders, so the scan is typically a small fraction of the CSV scan.

Verify: The Parquet query's Data scanned is dramatically lower than the CSV query's (often 5-50x less). S3 shows partition folders like curated/orders_parquet/order_year=2026/ containing .snappy.parquet files.

Gotchas: The partitioned_by column must be the last column(s) in the SELECT list — CTAS requires this order or it errors. external_location must be an empty S3 prefix; pointing at an existing non-empty path fails. Partitioning on a super-high-cardinality column (e.g. order_id) creates millions of tiny files — partition on low-cardinality fields (year/month/region). Athena min-charges 10 MB per query, so tiny tables won't show the savings — use a reasonably sized dataset.

Task: Simple Lambda ETL triggered by an S3 upload

Goal: run Python automatically whenever a file lands in S3 — event-driven ingestion with zero servers. This "S3 → Lambda" trigger is the bread-and-butter serverless ETL pattern.

  1. Search Lambda → Create function → Author from scratch. Name s3_csv_rowcount, Runtime Python 3.12, Architecture x86_64 → Create.
  2. Replace the code in the editor with: 
    import boto3, csv, io

s3 = boto3.client("s3")

def lambda_handler(event, context):
    rec = event["Records"][0]["s3"]
    bucket = rec["bucket"]["name"]
    key = rec["object"]["key"]
    obj = s3.get_object(Bucket=bucket, Key=key)
    text = obj["Body"].read().decode("utf-8")
    rows = list(csv.reader(io.StringIO(text)))
    n = max(len(rows) - 1, 0)   # minus header
    print(f"{key} has {n} data rows")
    # write a marker to curated/
    out_key = key.replace("raw/", "curated/") + ".rowcount.txt"
    s3.put_object(Bucket=bucket, Key=out_key, Body=str(n).encode())
    return {"rows": n}
    Click Deploy.
  3. Give it S3 permissions: Configuration → Permissions → click the execution role → in IAM attach AmazonS3FullAccess (or a scoped policy for just your bucket).
  4. Add the trigger: Lambda function page → + Add trigger → S3 → bucket de-lab-deepesh-2026, Event type All object create events, Prefix raw/, Suffix .csv → acknowledge the recursion warning → Add.
  5. Test: upload a CSV to raw/ in S3.

Verify: Lambda → Monitor → View CloudWatch logs shows a log stream with your ... has N data rows line. A curated/....rowcount.txt object appears in S3 with the count.

Gotchas: AccessDenied in logs = the execution role can't read S3; attach the policy (step 3). NEVER set the output prefix the same as the trigger prefix — writing a .csv back into raw/ re-triggers the function infinitely (a real bill/loop bug); that's why the suffix filter and the curated/ output matter. Default timeout is 3s — increase under Configuration → General → Timeout for bigger files. boto3 is preinstalled in Lambda; pandas is NOT (needs a layer) — that's why this uses the stdlib csv module.

Task: Set up a CloudWatch alarm + budget alert so the free tier never bills you

Goal: get emailed the moment spend approaches a threshold, so a forgotten resource can't silently rack up charges. Every responsible cloud user sets this up on day one.

  1. Sign in as root (billing lives at the account level). Top-right account menu → Billing and Cost Management.
  2. Budgets → Create budget → template Zero spend budget (alerts at your first cent) OR Monthly cost budget with amount e.g. USD 1. Enter your email as the recipient → Create budget.
  3. (Optional CloudWatch billing alarm.) First enable billing metrics: Billing → Billing preferences → tick Receive CloudWatch billing alerts → Save.
  4. Switch region to US East (N. Virginia) us-east-1 (billing metrics only live there). Search CloudWatch → Alarms → Create alarm → Select metric → Billing → Total Estimated Charge → USD.
  5. Condition: Greater than 1 USD. Notification → Create new SNS topic → enter your email → Create. Name the alarm billing-over-1usd → Create.
  6. Confirm the SNS subscription — AWS emails you a "Confirm subscription" link; click it or the alarm can't notify you.

Verify: The budget appears under Budgets and the CloudWatch alarm shows state OK (or Insufficient data initially). Your inbox has an AWS "Subscription confirmed" email for the SNS topic.

Gotchas: The billing metric ONLY exists in us-east-1 — creating the alarm in Mumbai finds no metric. Billing data updates several times a day, not in real time, so Insufficient data at first is normal. If you never clicked the SNS confirmation link, alarms fire but no email arrives. Budgets and the first alarm are free; don't create dozens. Deleting unused resources (buckets, clusters, NAT gateways) is the real cost control — the alarm is just your smoke detector.

Local Dev Playbooks (Python, Docker, Kafka, Airflow, PySpark, Snowflake)

Get a real data-engineering toolchain running on your own (Windows) laptop — the environment you'll actually develop in every day.

Task: Set up a proper DE laptop — Python + venv + VS Code + Git + GitHub SSH (Windows)

Goal: a clean, reproducible dev environment with isolated Python, an editor, and password-free GitHub pushes. Every project you build rests on this foundation.

  1. Install Python: get it from python.org/downloads. In the installer, TICK "Add python.exe to PATH", then Install. Verify in a new terminal (PowerShell): python --version and pip --version.
  2. Install VS Code from code.visualstudio.com. Open it → Extensions (Ctrl+Shift+X) → install Python (Microsoft) and Jupyter.
  3. Install Git from git-scm.com/download/win (accept defaults; it bundles Git Bash). Verify: git --version. Set identity: 
    git config --global user.name  "Deepesh Niranjan"
git config --global user.email "deepeshniranjan@gmail.com"
  4. Create a project with an isolated environment (a venv keeps each project's packages separate): 
    mkdir de-project && cd de-project
python -m venv .venv
.venv\Scripts\activate          <-- Windows PowerShell/CMD
pip install pandas requests
pip freeze > requirements.txt
    Your prompt now shows (.venv).
  5. Generate a GitHub SSH key and add it: 
    ssh-keygen -t ed25519 -C "deepeshniranjan@gmail.com"
# press Enter for defaults
type %USERPROFILE%\.ssh\id_ed25519.pub    <-- copy this output
    On GitHub: avatar → Settings → SSH and GPG keys → New SSH key → paste → Add.
  6. Test and push: 
    ssh -T git@github.com          # expect "successfully authenticated"
git init
echo ".venv/" > .gitignore
git add . && git commit -m "first commit"
git branch -M main
git remote add origin git@github.com:<you>/de-project.git
git push -u origin main

Verify: ssh -T git@github.com greets you by username, git push succeeds without asking for a password, and the repo shows your files on github.com. In VS Code, the Python interpreter (bottom bar) points at .venv.

Gotchas: 'python' is not recognized' — you missed the "Add to PATH" checkbox; re-run the installer and tick it, then open a fresh terminal. On Windows, activation is .venv\Scripts\activate (not the Linux source .venv/bin/activate); if PowerShell blocks it, run Set-ExecutionPolicy -Scope CurrentUser RemoteSigned. Pushing over https:// asks for a password every time and now needs a Personal Access Token — SSH avoids that. Always commit requirements.txt and .gitignore the venv folder, never the venv itself.

Task: Run MySQL in Docker + connect from Python

Goal: spin up a throwaway database in seconds without installing MySQL on your OS, then query it from Python. Docker-for-databases is how DEs get consistent local dev/test data stores.

  1. Install Docker Desktop from docker.com/products/docker-desktop. On Windows it needs WSL2 — the installer enables it; reboot if asked. Confirm: docker --version and that the Docker Desktop whale icon says Running.
  2. Start MySQL in a container: 
    docker run --name mysql-de -e MYSQL_ROOT_PASSWORD=rootpass ^
  -e MYSQL_DATABASE=shop -p 3306:3306 -d mysql:8
    (Use ^ for line-continuation in Windows CMD, or put it all on one line. In Git Bash/PowerShell use a backtick ` or one line.)
  3. Check it's up: docker ps shows mysql-de. Open a SQL shell inside it: 
    docker exec -it mysql-de mysql -uroot -prootpass shop
-- then:
CREATE TABLE customers(id INT, name VARCHAR(50));
INSERT INTO customers VALUES (1,'Asha'),(2,'Ravi');
exit
  4. From Python (in your venv): pip install mysql-connector-python, then: 
    import mysql.connector
cn = mysql.connector.connect(host="127.0.0.1", port=3306,
        user="root", password="rootpass", database="shop")
cur = cn.cursor()
cur.execute("SELECT * FROM customers")
print(cur.fetchall())
cn.close()

Verify: The Python script prints [(1, 'Asha'), (2, 'Ravi')]. docker ps lists the container with port 0.0.0.0:3306->3306.

Gotchas: "port is already allocated" — something already uses 3306 (a locally-installed MySQL); map to a different host port like -p 3307:3306 and connect on 3307. MySQL 8 takes ~10-20s to initialize; connecting immediately gives "Can't connect" — wait, or watch docker logs mysql-de for "ready for connections". -d runs it detached (background); drop it to see logs live. Stopping (docker stop mysql-de) keeps data; docker rm deletes the container and its data unless you mounted a volume.

Task: Run Kafka locally with docker-compose + Python producer/consumer test

Goal: stand up a local Kafka broker and send/receive messages from Python — your first taste of streaming without any cloud. Kafka is the #1 streaming skill on fintech DE JDs.

  1. In a new folder create docker-compose.yml (modern KRaft mode, no ZooKeeper): 
    services:
  kafka:
    image: bitnami/kafka:latest
    ports:
      - "9092:9092"
    environment:
      - KAFKA_CFG_NODE_ID=0
      - KAFKA_CFG_PROCESS_ROLES=controller,broker
      - KAFKA_CFG_CONTROLLER_QUORUM_VOTERS=0@kafka:9093
      - KAFKA_CFG_LISTENERS=PLAINTEXT://:9092,CONTROLLER://:9093
      - KAFKA_CFG_ADVERTISED_LISTENERS=PLAINTEXT://localhost:9092
      - KAFKA_CFG_LISTENER_SECURITY_PROTOCOL_MAP=CONTROLLER:PLAINTEXT,PLAINTEXT:PLAINTEXT
      - KAFKA_CFG_CONTROLLER_LISTENER_NAMES=CONTROLLER
  2. Start it: docker compose up -d. Check: docker compose ps shows the broker running.
  3. In your venv: pip install kafka-python. Producer script producer.py: 
    from kafka import KafkaProducer
import json, time
p = KafkaProducer(bootstrap_servers="localhost:9092",
      value_serializer=lambda v: json.dumps(v).encode())
for i in range(5):
    p.send("orders", {"order_id": i, "amount": i*10})
    print("sent", i); time.sleep(0.5)
p.flush()
  4. Consumer script consumer.py (run in a second terminal): 
    from kafka import KafkaConsumer
import json
c = KafkaConsumer("orders", bootstrap_servers="localhost:9092",
      auto_offset_reset="earliest",
      value_deserializer=lambda m: json.loads(m.decode()))
for msg in c:
    print("got", msg.value)
  5. Run python consumer.py first, then in the other terminal python producer.py.

Verify: The consumer terminal prints got {'order_id': 0, ...} through 4 as the producer sends them. With auto_offset_reset="earliest", a consumer started later still reads all past messages on that topic.

Gotchas: NoBrokersAvailable — the broker isn't ready yet (Kafka takes ~15s) or ADVERTISED_LISTENERS is wrong; for host access it must advertise localhost:9092, not an internal hostname. Consumer prints nothing? Without auto_offset_reset="earliest" a fresh consumer group only sees messages sent after it started — send again while it's listening. Port 9092 in use → change the mapping. On older tutorials with a separate ZooKeeper container, KRaft mode above is simpler and current.

Task: Run Airflow locally + deploy your first DAG

Goal: run the Airflow scheduler + webserver locally and see a DAG you wrote appear and run in the UI. Airflow is the default orchestrator you'll be asked about in almost every DE interview.

  1. Easiest path — the Astro CLI. Install it (Windows: winget install -e --id Astronomer.Astro, or follow docs at astronomer.io). It needs Docker Desktop running.
  2. Scaffold and start a project: 
    mkdir airflow-lab && cd airflow-lab
astro dev init          # creates dags/, Dockerfile, etc.
astro dev start         # builds & runs Airflow in Docker
    (Alternative without Astro: use the official docker-compose.yaml from the Airflow docs and run docker compose up.)
  3. Open http://localhost:8080. Log in with admin / admin (Astro default).
  4. Add a DAG: create dags/hello_dag.py: 
    from airflow import DAG
from airflow.operators.python import PythonOperator
from datetime import datetime

def say():
    print("hello from Deepesh's first DAG")

with DAG("hello_dag", start_date=datetime(2026,1,1),
         schedule="@daily", catchup=False, tags=["lab"]) as dag:
    PythonOperator(task_id="say_hello", python_callable=say)
  5. Airflow scans the dags/ folder every ~30s. Refresh the UI, find hello_dag, toggle it On (unpause), then click ▶ Trigger DAG.

Verify: hello_dag appears in the DAGs list; after triggering, the Grid view shows a green (success) run and the task Logs contain "hello from Deepesh's first DAG".

Gotchas: DAG not showing? A Python import/syntax error in the file hides it — check the top of the DAGs page for an "Import Errors" banner, or run astro dev logs. Airflow won't backfill old dates only if catchup=False — leave it False for a learning DAG or it fires many historical runs. Port 8080 in use kills the webserver — free it or remap. schedule= replaced the old schedule_interval= in Airflow 2.4+; older tutorials use the old name.

Task: Install PySpark locally (Java!) + run your first script

Goal: run Spark on your laptop for offline practice — no cluster, no cloud. Great for iterating on transformations before running them on Databricks/EMR.

  1. Install Java (Spark runs on the JVM — this is the step everyone forgets). Install a JDK 17 (Temurin from adoptium.net). Verify: java -version prints 17.x.
  2. Set JAVA_HOME so Spark can find Java. Windows: Search → Edit the system environment variables → Environment Variables → New system variable JAVA_HOME = the JDK folder (e.g. C:\Program Files\Eclipse Adoptium\jdk-17...). Open a fresh terminal.
  3. In your venv install PySpark: pip install pyspark. (This bundles Spark itself — no separate Spark download needed.)
  4. Write first_spark.py: 
    from pyspark.sql import SparkSession, functions as F

spark = SparkSession.builder.appName("local-test").master("local[*]").getOrCreate()
spark.sparkContext.setLogLevel("WARN")

df = spark.createDataFrame(
    [(1,"Asha",100),(2,"Ravi",250),(3,"Asha",50)],
    ["id","name","amount"])
(df.groupBy("name").agg(F.sum("amount").alias("total")).show())
spark.stop()
  5. Run: python first_spark.py.

Verify: The script prints a table with Asha 150 and Ravi 250. The Spark banner/version prints and no Java errors appear.

Gotchas: "JAVA_HOME is not set" / "Unsupported class file major version" — Java missing or a version PySpark doesn't support (use JDK 17 or the version your PySpark release lists, not the newest JDK). On Windows you may hit a winutils.exe / HADOOP_HOME warning — for local DataFrame work it's usually harmless; if writes to disk fail, download winutils.exe for your Hadoop version and set HADOOP_HOME. master("local[*]") uses all cores; drop to local[2] if the laptop chokes. A stray PYSPARK_PYTHON pointing at the wrong interpreter causes worker crashes — keep it unset or point it at your venv python.

Task: Snowflake 30-day trial — warehouse/db/schema, stage + COPY INTO a CSV, query it

Goal: load a CSV into Snowflake the proper way (stage → COPY INTO) and query it. Snowflake is a top cloud-DW skill for product/fintech DE roles, and COPY INTO is its core loading command.

  1. Sign up at https://signup.snowflake.com — free 30-day trial with USD 400 credits. Pick Standard edition and any cloud/region. Verify email, set a password, and open Snowsight (the web UI).
  2. Open a SQL Worksheet (left nav → Projects → Worksheets → + Worksheet). Create the objects: 
    CREATE WAREHOUSE wh_de WITH WAREHOUSE_SIZE='XSMALL'
  AUTO_SUSPEND=60 AUTO_RESUME=TRUE;
CREATE DATABASE de_lab;
CREATE SCHEMA de_lab.raw;
USE WAREHOUSE wh_de; USE DATABASE de_lab; USE SCHEMA raw;
  3. Create the target table + a file format + a stage: 
    CREATE TABLE orders (order_id INT, customer STRING, amount NUMBER);
CREATE FILE FORMAT csv_ff TYPE=CSV SKIP_HEADER=1 FIELD_OPTIONALLY_ENCLOSED_BY='"';
CREATE STAGE my_stage FILE_FORMAT = csv_ff;
  4. Upload the CSV to the stage. Easiest in Snowsight: Data → Databases → de_lab → raw → Stages → MY_STAGE → + Files → drag your orders.csv. (CLI alternative: PUT file://orders.csv @my_stage; via SnowSQL.)
  5. Load and query: 
    COPY INTO orders FROM @my_stage FILE_FORMAT=(FORMAT_NAME=csv_ff);
SELECT * FROM orders LIMIT 10;
SELECT customer, SUM(amount) FROM orders GROUP BY customer;

Verify: COPY INTO returns a result row showing the file LOADED with a rows_loaded count matching your data lines. SELECT * FROM orders returns the rows with correct types.

Gotchas: Nothing loads / "0 files processed" — files staged from Snowsight land in the named stage; make sure the COPY INTO points at @my_stage, not the table's default stage. Header row imported as data = you forgot SKIP_HEADER=1 in the file format. Number parse errors = amounts have currency symbols/commas — clean them or use ON_ERROR='CONTINUE' to skip bad rows and inspect. Set AUTO_SUSPEND low so the warehouse stops when idle — a warehouse left running burns trial credits fast.

Portfolio Ladder: 3 Resume-Worthy Projects (Beginner → Advanced)

Three projects that, done in order, prove the exact skills product/fintech DE interviews test. Each is fully specified: architecture, tech, step-by-step build plan, likely interview questions, and resume-bullet wording.

What & Why

These three projects map directly to Phases 2, 4, and 5 of the roadmap. Project 1 proves you can model and query data. Project 2 proves you can run Spark on the cloud with a lakehouse pattern. Project 3 proves you understand streaming and orchestration. Together they cover ~80% of what a mid-level DE interview probes.

Do them in order — each reuses skills from the last. Put every one on GitHub with a README that includes an architecture diagram (a simple draw.io PNG is enough), setup steps, and sample outputs. In interviews you will be asked to "walk me through a project you built" — these are your answers.

Project 1 — Batch ETL & Star Schema (Beginner)

Goal: Build a classic batch ETL pipeline that ingests a raw dataset, cleans it in Python, loads it into a dimensional (star schema) model in MySQL, and answers business questions with SQL. Proves data modeling, ETL, and SQL — the DE fundamentals every interview starts with.

Architecture: Kaggle CSV → Python (pandas) cleaning/validation → transform into fact + dimension tables → load into MySQL star schema → analytical SQL queries + optional charts.

Exact tech: Python 3, pandas, SQLAlchemy or mysql-connector, MySQL (local or Docker), Git/GitHub. Optional: Jupyter for the analysis notebook.

Suggested dataset: Kaggle "Olist Brazilian E-Commerce", "Retail Transactions", or "NYC Yellow Taxi" — anything with a clear business event (order/trip) plus dimensions (customer, product, date, location).

  1. Download a Kaggle dataset with an obvious fact table (transactions/orders) and several dimension candidates.
  2. In a Python script, load the raw CSV(s) with pandas; profile it (nulls, dtypes, duplicates, ranges).
  3. Clean: fix dtypes, parse dates, deduplicate, handle nulls, standardize categorical values.
  4. Design a star schema on paper: one fact table (e.g. fact_sales) + dimensions (dim_customer, dim_product, dim_date, dim_location).
  5. Generate surrogate keys for dimensions; build a dim_date from scratch. Split the cleaned data into these tables in pandas.
  6. Create the schema in MySQL (DDL with primary/foreign keys); load the tables via SQLAlchemy to_sql or bulk insert.
  7. Write 8–10 analytical SQL queries: top products, revenue by month (window functions), customer cohorts, YoY growth.
  8. Write a README with an architecture diagram, ER diagram of the star schema, how-to-run, and the queries with sample results. Push to GitHub.

Verify: Foreign keys join cleanly with no orphan rows; your analytical queries return sensible numbers; a stranger could clone the repo and run it from the README alone.

Interviewers will ask: Why a star schema over a normalized model? Fact vs dimension — what's the difference? How would you handle a changing customer attribute (SCD Type 2)? What's a surrogate key and why use it? How did you handle nulls/duplicates?

Resume bullet examples:

  • "Built a batch ETL pipeline in Python/pandas to ingest and clean 100K+ e-commerce records, loading a star-schema dimensional model into MySQL for analytics."
  • "Designed a star schema (fact + 4 dimensions incl. a generated date dimension) and authored 10+ analytical SQL queries using window functions for revenue and cohort analysis."

Project 2 — Cloud Lakehouse with Delta & Medallion (Intermediate)

Goal: Ingest raw data into cloud object storage, process it with PySpark on Databricks into bronze/silver/gold Delta tables (medallion architecture), add data-quality checks, and orchestrate the whole thing with Airflow. Proves Spark + cloud + lakehouse + orchestration — the core of a modern DE role.

Architecture: Raw CSV → ADLS Gen2 / S3 (raw zone) → PySpark on Databricks → bronze (raw ingested as Delta) → silver (cleaned, deduped, typed) → gold (aggregated business tables) → DQ checks → Airflow DAG orchestrating the runs.

Exact tech: Databricks Community Edition, PySpark, Delta Lake, Azure ADLS Gen2 (or AWS S3), Apache Airflow (in Docker), Python for DQ checks (Great Expectations optional), Git/GitHub.

Suggested dataset: Kaggle NYC Taxi (large, partitionable by date), or a public transactions dataset — fits fintech/product narratives well.

  1. Create an Azure free-tier account; provision an ADLS Gen2 storage account and a container. Upload raw CSVs to a raw/ path.
  2. In Databricks (Community Edition), configure access to the storage; read the raw CSVs with PySpark, inferring/defining a schema.
  3. Bronze: write the raw data as-is to a Delta table (append, with an ingestion timestamp column).
  4. Silver: from bronze, clean dtypes, drop/handle nulls, deduplicate with row_number() windows, standardize columns; write as Delta.
  5. Gold: build aggregated business tables (e.g. daily revenue, trips per zone) with groupBy/agg; write partitioned Delta.
  6. Add DQ checks between layers: row-count sanity, null thresholds on key columns, uniqueness of primary keys, value-range assertions — fail the run if violated.
  7. Demonstrate a Delta MERGE (upsert) to handle late-arriving/updated records (SCD-style), and show DESCRIBE HISTORY / time travel.
  8. Orchestrate with Airflow: a DAG with tasks bronze → silver → gold → DQ, with dependencies and retries. Run Airflow via Docker Compose locally.
  9. README with medallion architecture diagram, DQ rules, and screenshots of the Airflow DAG. Push to GitHub.

Verify: Delta tables show version history via DESCRIBE HISTORY; the MERGE updates existing rows without duplicating; the Airflow DAG runs end-to-end green; a DQ failure actually stops the pipeline.

Interviewers will ask: Why the medallion (bronze/silver/gold) pattern? What does Delta give you over plain Parquet (ACID, MERGE, time travel, schema enforcement)? How does Spark handle a large join — what's a shuffle? How did you handle data skew? What DQ checks did you add and why? Why orchestrate with Airflow vs cron?

Resume bullet examples:

  • "Engineered a cloud lakehouse on Azure/Databricks processing millions of records with PySpark into bronze/silver/gold Delta tables (medallion architecture), including MERGE-based upserts and time travel."
  • "Orchestrated the pipeline with Apache Airflow (containerized via Docker) and added data-quality gates (null/uniqueness/range checks) that halt runs on violation."

Project 3 — Real-Time Streaming Pipeline (Advanced / Capstone)

Goal: Simulate a live event stream with a Kafka producer, process it in near-real-time with Spark Structured Streaming, write results to a Delta or Postgres sink, and visualize on a simple dashboard. Proves streaming, Kafka, and event-driven thinking — the differentiator for fintech (transactions, fraud, real-time analytics).

Architecture: Python producer (simulated transaction/click events) → Kafka topic → Spark Structured Streaming (windowed aggregations, watermarking) → Delta table / Postgres sink → dashboard (Streamlit or Metabase/Grafana).

Exact tech: Apache Kafka (via Docker Compose), Python (kafka-python or confluent-kafka) producer, PySpark Structured Streaming, Delta Lake or PostgreSQL sink, Streamlit or Grafana dashboard, Docker Compose to run the stack, Git/GitHub.

Data: Generate synthetic events in the producer — e.g. payment transactions with user_id, amount, merchant, timestamp, some flagged suspicious. Fintech-flavored story.

  1. Spin up Kafka + Zookeeper (or KRaft) with Docker Compose; create a topic (e.g. transactions).
  2. Write a Python producer that emits synthetic events every few hundred ms as JSON to the topic.
  3. Write a Spark Structured Streaming job that reads from Kafka, parses JSON, and applies a schema.
  4. Add streaming transforms: windowed aggregations (e.g. total amount per merchant per 1-min window) with watermarking for late data.
  5. Add a simple rule-based flag (e.g. amount > threshold in a short window) to mimic fraud detection.
  6. Write results to a sink: append to a Delta table (with checkpointing) and/or upsert to Postgres.
  7. Build a lightweight dashboard (Streamlit reading from the sink, or Grafana on Postgres) showing live metrics.
  8. Package the whole stack in Docker Compose so it runs with one command; write a thorough README with the streaming architecture diagram. Push to GitHub.

Verify: Producer emits continuously; the streaming job's output updates as new events arrive; killing and restarting the job resumes from the checkpoint without data loss; the dashboard reflects live numbers.

Interviewers will ask: Batch vs streaming — when each? What are Kafka partitions/consumer groups/offsets? What is a watermark and how does it handle late data? Exactly-once vs at-least-once — how does checkpointing help? How would this scale in production? Where could it fail and how would you make it fault-tolerant?

Resume bullet examples:

  • "Built a real-time streaming pipeline: Kafka ingesting simulated transaction events processed by Spark Structured Streaming with windowed aggregations and watermarking, sinking to Delta/Postgres with checkpointing for fault tolerance."
  • "Implemented a rule-based real-time anomaly flag over a live event stream and surfaced metrics on a Streamlit dashboard; entire stack containerized with Docker Compose for one-command startup."

Cheat Sheets: Dense DE Reference

Copy-paste-ready syntax and patterns for the things you'll reach for constantly — SQL windows, PySpark API, Spark tuning, Git, Docker, and Linux/CLI. Skim before interviews, keep open while building.

What & Why

These are working references, not tutorials — the exact syntax and the top patterns for each area. When you're mid-build and forget the argument order for a window function or a Spark broadcast hint, this is where you look. Learn the concepts elsewhere in the Bible; use this to move fast.

SQL Window Functions

Syntax skeleton:

<function>() OVER (
    PARTITION BY col1, col2      -- optional: reset per group
    ORDER BY col3 [ASC|DESC]     -- required for ranking / running calcs
    ROWS BETWEEN <start> AND <end> -- optional frame
)
PatternFunction
Rank rows (gaps on ties)RANK() OVER (ORDER BY sales DESC)
Rank rows (no gaps)DENSE_RANK() OVER (...)
Unique sequence / dedupeROW_NUMBER() OVER (PARTITION BY id ORDER BY ts DESC)
Top-N per groupWrap ROW_NUMBER in a CTE, filter WHERE rn <= N
Previous / next valueLAG(x,1) OVER (...) / LEAD(x,1) OVER (...)
Running totalSUM(x) OVER (ORDER BY d ROWS UNBOUNDED PRECEDING)
Moving average (3-row)AVG(x) OVER (ORDER BY d ROWS BETWEEN 2 PRECEDING AND CURRENT ROW)
% of group totalx / SUM(x) OVER (PARTITION BY grp)
First / last in groupFIRST_VALUE(x) / LAST_VALUE(x) OVER (...)
Bucket into N groupsNTILE(4) OVER (ORDER BY x) (quartiles)

Gotcha: you cannot use a window function in WHERE; compute it in a CTE/subquery, then filter in the outer query.

PySpark DataFrame API

from pyspark.sql import SparkSession, functions as F, Window
spark = SparkSession.builder.appName("app").getOrCreate()

# READ
df = spark.read.parquet("/path")                 # or .csv(path, header=True, inferSchema=True)
df = spark.read.format("delta").load("/path")

# INSPECT
df.printSchema(); df.show(5, truncate=False); df.count()

# TRANSFORM
df2 = (df.select("id", "amount", "ts")
         .filter(F.col("amount") > 0)
         .withColumn("amount_usd", F.col("amount") * 0.012)
         .withColumnRenamed("ts", "event_ts")
         .dropDuplicates(["id"])
         .na.fill({"amount": 0}))

# AGGREGATE
df.groupBy("merchant").agg(F.sum("amount").alias("total"),
                           F.countDistinct("id").alias("n"))

# JOIN
a.join(b, on="id", how="inner")                  # left / right / outer / anti / semi
a.join(F.broadcast(small), "id", "left")         # broadcast small side

# WINDOW
w = Window.partitionBy("user").orderBy(F.col("ts").desc())
df.withColumn("rn", F.row_number().over(w)).filter("rn = 1")   # latest per user

# UDF (prefer built-ins; UDFs are slow & opaque to the optimizer)
@F.udf("string")
def clean(x): return (x or "").strip().lower()

# WRITE
(df.write.mode("overwrite").partitionBy("dt")
    .format("delta").save("/out"))               # or .parquet("/out")

# SPARK SQL (leverage your SQL)
df.createOrReplaceTempView("t")
spark.sql("SELECT merchant, SUM(amount) FROM t GROUP BY merchant")

Spark Tuning Quick Hits

Issue / GoalDo this
Small dim joined to big factBroadcast the small side: F.broadcast(dim) (auto if < 10MB threshold)
Too many / too few shuffle partitionsspark.conf.set("spark.sql.shuffle.partitions", N) (default 200; tune to data size)
Let Spark auto-tune joins/partitionsEnable AQE: spark.sql.adaptive.enabled=true (on by default in Spark 3+)
Reuse a DataFrame multiple timesdf.cache() / df.persist(), then an action to materialize
Data skew (one key dominates)Salt the key: add random suffix to hot keys, join, then aggregate & strip salt
Reduce output file countdf.coalesce(1) (no shuffle) vs repartition(n) (full shuffle, even sizes)
Slow reads on huge tablesPartition writes by a filter column (partitionBy("dt")) for partition pruning
Diagnose a slow jobRead the Spark UI: look at stages, shuffle read/write, and skewed/straggler tasks
AvoidUDFs when a built-in exists; collect() on big data; wide transforms you don't need

Git Daily Commands

git clone <url>                 # copy a repo locally
git status                      # what changed
git add .                       # stage all changes  (or: git add <file>)
git commit -m "message"         # snapshot staged changes
git push                        # upload commits to remote
git pull                        # fetch + merge remote changes

git branch feature-x            # create branch
git checkout -b feature-x       # create + switch
git switch main                 # switch branch
git merge feature-x             # merge branch into current
git log --oneline --graph       # compact history

git stash / git stash pop       # shelve / restore uncommitted work
git reset --hard HEAD           # discard ALL local changes (careful!)
git diff                        # see unstaged changes
# .gitignore -> list files/dirs Git should never track (e.g. venv/, *.csv)

Docker Daily Commands

docker build -t myimg .         # build image from ./Dockerfile
docker images                   # list images
docker run -d -p 8080:8080 myimg  # run detached, map host:container port
docker ps                       # running containers  (add -a for all)
docker logs <id>                 # view container logs  (-f to follow)
docker exec -it <id> bash        # shell into a running container
docker stop <id> / docker rm <id>  # stop / remove container
docker rmi <img>                 # remove image
docker volume ls                # persistent storage

# Docker Compose (multi-container stacks: Kafka, Airflow, Postgres)
docker compose up -d            # start all services in background
docker compose ps               # status
docker compose logs -f <svc>     # follow a service's logs
docker compose down             # stop & remove  (add -v to drop volumes)

Linux / CLI for Data Engineers

CommandUse
ls -lh / cd / pwdList (human sizes) / change / print directory
cat / less / head -n 20 / tail -fView file / page / first rows / follow a growing log
wc -l file.csvCount rows (lines) in a file
grep "ERROR" app.logSearch text; grep -c counts, grep -i ignores case
cut -d',' -f1,3 file.csvExtract columns 1 & 3 from a CSV
sort / uniq -cSort lines / count unique (pipe: sort | uniq -c)
awk -F',' '{print $2}'Field-level processing on delimited data
sed 's/old/new/g' fFind/replace text in a stream
| (pipe) / > / >>Chain commands / write to file / append to file
df -h / du -sh *Disk free / size of each item in dir
chmod +x s.sh / ./s.shMake script executable / run it
ssh / scpConnect to remote host / copy files over SSH
ps aux | grep sparkFind running processes; kill <pid> to stop
crontab -eSchedule recurring jobs (the low-tech Airflow)

Pipe example: cat sales.csv | cut -d',' -f3 | sort | uniq -c | sort -nr | head — top values of column 3, counted and ranked.
